ANZAC.
(Greater than glories of the tivtor's place,
T t“ know through grim defeat how great the
prise.
To come through that ordeal that purifies
And breeds a new, a keener, stronger race:
As in some byshhuul scene that bears a trace
Of some great fire and Nature beautifies
More lavishly the aftergrowths that rise
Profusely o'er the devastated space.
Anzae, a name that speaks of bitter days,
The consummation of our hopes and fears,
Thy loss shall teach us more than victory;
And more than honors of a world-wide praise
Shall wc revere through all the coming
years,
Those sad, still jnounds that speak our fealty.

The Swine
At the beginning of the war the opinion
was widely prevalent that the Huns were a
peaceful and kindly people hurled to slaughter
by their wicked rulers. A closer acquaint-
ance with the Hun in action dispelled that
charming illusion. Hence there is scarcely
any need for such an indictment as De-
generate Germany, by Henry de Halsaile
(Laurie; 25.). Of course, it is easy, by record-
ing only the worse aspects of an individual,
to depict him as a devil; but the facts that
the author adduces are too well authenti-
cated, chiefly by German writers, for any
accusation of bias tt> stand.
First of all, it is high time to dispose of
the Hun’s claim to be our cousin. It is
obvious that there is no mental relationship
between the Englishman" and the Boche.
The eousinship disappears as soon as a com-
parison of the shape of the l’espective skulls
of the two races is made. Anybody who has
been to Germany must have noted the ex-
traordinarily shaped skulls there. The Ger-
man has a brachycephalic, or short-headed,
skull; the Englishman has the radically
different long-headed skull. The British
Tommy soon noted the difference: he calls
the Huns “square-heads.” The average Hun
has no back to his head; the protuberance
of the cerebellum is noticeably absent. There
are other extraordinary skulls to be found in
Germany, the cylindrical and “sugar-loaf”
skull, and the small, round-topped skull re-
sembling in shape that of an American negro.
You can’t make a negro into an Englishman
any more than you can make a Hun into a
civilised man. The anatomical structure of
the skull prevents it.
J*
We are not interested in the author’s
awful picture of mediaeval Gei’inany: 20th
century Germany is a sufficient and conclu-
sive text for the author's sei'mon on national
degeneracy. Much that he prints is too dis-
gusting to quote; and the curious thing is
■ that this indictment of a nation is practically
delivered by German critics. Of course, it is
notorious that the Hun is a hoggish eater,
with table manners that would annoy an
American millionaire. He is just as hoggish
a drinker. Our own poets will be envious to
hear that it is quite a common feat in Ger-
many for a man to drink a litre of beer with-
out taking breath. A litre is a quart plus
half a pint. One German student achieved
fame by being able to swallow two litres
between breaths.
There are, it is said, Australian women
who profess to see no objection to the Hun
swanking down our streets. They may be
interested to learn that the average weekly
income of - German women factory employees
is 6s. a week. At the Court Theatre in
Hanover in 1914 the directors paid their
female daneex-s 10s. a mouth. At the Court
Theatre in Eisenach 15s. a month is the
emolument of the “leading lady.” The direc-
tors of such theatres take the view that
pretty girls should not need any salax-ies at
all: thei-e are other obvious ways in which
they can make a living. We have heard
much of the lack of vagrant.- and unemployed
in Berlin. That is because ’he Huns wisely
keep them out of sight. In jne year, 1913,
over a million homeless human beings were
given lodging in the discreetly-secluded Ber-
lin City Shelter.
“A German baby is a pitiful object; it is
pinioned and bound up, like a mummy, in
yards of bandages which ai’e unfolded once
(at the outside, twice) a day; it is never
bathed, but presumably sometimes washed in
some occult manner. Its head is never
touched with soap and water until it is
eight or 10 months old, wheii the thick skull-
cap of encrusted dirt which has accumulated
is removed by the application of various
unguents. Many German women have
assured the writer that the fine heads of hair
one sees in Germany are entirely owing to
this decidedly unhygienic skullcap.” But,
anyhow, it hardly matters to the mother,
since she scarcely sees her baby, brought up
by that institution of the German household,
the wet-nurse. “The demand for these wet-
nurses is so great throughout the empire that
girls of the working-class, peasants and other-
wise. are encouraged to bring illegitimate
children into the world, and thus qualify
for these well-paid posts.”
The Prussian law permits the husband to
“inflict bodily chastisement in moderation”
upon his wife. It is left to the German hus-
band, by law. to determine the length of time
his wife may suckle a child; and, of course,
all decisions with regard to the children rest
absolutely with him. In Prussia women are
forbidden to take part in political associa-
tions and public meetings. And eight-tenths
of the agricultural laborers of the Father-
land are women. “A woman and a cow yoked
will draw the plough.” In Dresden the
wotuan is yoked with a dog to small waggons.
jt
Gennany, it is well known, is the centre
of the “white slave” traffic of the world. In
fact, the export of these human wares ought
to figure importantly in German trade
statistics.
A professor at the Berlin University
estimates the number of prostitutes in
Germany as no less than 1,500,000,
Berlin has no rival in this profession in the
world. Even Dresden can afford to have 32
streets for the exclusive accommodation of
Flossie. But the quotations given by the
author from official and representative Ger-
man authorities are innocuous compared
with the facts relating to the extent of sexual
perversion in Germany. It has become im-
portant enough to be termed the national
vice. Naturally, the foremost authorities on
this nauseous subject are Germans. Accord-
ing to a Berlin observer in that "gay” city
there are 40 resorts patronised by these per-
verts. Another authority computes that
there are 1,200,000 of these noxious beasts in
Germany, Bloch says that “sexual aberra-
tion is diffused throughout all classes of the
German population. We find it among work-
men just as much as among aristocrats,
princely personalities and intellectual
heroes.” The notorious Eulenburg scandal
caused no surprise to those who knew the
upper-class German at home. Similar astound-
ing figures are given, on unimpeachable
authority, in reference to the prevalence of
venereal disease. In 1910 it was publicly
stated that 90 per cent, of the male. Berliners
had at one time or another been afflicted with
sexual disease in some form, and that one-
third of these cases was of a grave charac-
ter. A German specialist states that one in
every five German men is a syphilitic.
It is therefore not astonishing to find fur-
ther evidences of the depravity and degener-
ation of the German race in the huge number
of convictions for indecent assaults on
children, rapes, etc. Even the worst forms
of sexual crimes go lightly punished. The
“lust-murder” is a particularly Teutonic
species of crime by reason of its frequency
in Germany and its comparative absence
elsewhere. And the German papers make
special features for their readers of these
unsavory items of interest. The bookshops
make displays in their windows of books on
sexual matters. The “most vital” of modern
German poets, according to Berlin critics,
is Richard Dehmel. His poems are absolutely
unfitted for reproduction in English.
Jit
The author’s indictment may be briefly
summed :
In Ggrmanv vice in the shape of prostitu-
tion has assumed colossal proportions. Bebel
states that Germany supplies half the prosti-
tutes of the world. Unnatural vice also has
assumed the gravest dimensions. Venereal
disease in Germany has risen to very, large
figures. The daughters of peasants and work-
ing people are. often, reared for the specific
purpose of pi’ostitution, sent on the streets
when little more than children to support
their parents. Pornographic literature of
the worst kind is on sale in all the book-
sellers’ windows, and is purchasable by girls
and boys. Criminality in Gei-many has
reached unheard of figures. In 1909 over
three-quarters of a million criminal acts and
offences were dealt with in the courts. Inter-
marriage, from mercenary motives, is so
common that it has seriously affected the
mental and physical health of a large part of
the population. Also, the effect of rampant
syphilis upon the mentality of the race is to
be remembered.
Evidently Kultur oxxght to be in gaol or in
a venereal hospital.

This page really does its darnedest to
comfort the correspondents who lay their
literary worries at its pink feet; but there
are some miracles that this page hasn’t the
time to work. Here are three polite but
important requests which are passed on to
its readers: —
“8.J.5.”: I was once informed that Dracula, by
Bram Stoker, The Beetle , by Richard March, and
another book were written as the result of a wag-er—
the weirdest book being the object aimed at. My
informant could neither remember the third book nor
its author. Gould you kindly tell me if such a wager
took place, and who was the other writer, and what
was the name of his book?
“Mosquito”: Between 15 and 20 years ago I read,
I believe on the Red Page, a review of a book written
by an eminent Russian (?) medical man, a Dr.
Varesoff (?). In summing-up, the reviewer said,
perhaps in other words: “That Dr. Varesoff proves
that surgery is the only scientific part of medicine”—
the other part, being only guesswork, being left un-
said. If I remember rightly, The Bulletin - also
said, “The profession will not relish the sale of the
book,” and you hinted that they would try to
suppress the sale of it. I have never seen a copy
of it, although I made feeble inquiries at the time.
Can you dig up that book for me—the title, author
and where I may be able to obtain same ?
“P.V.W.”: I once noticed an advertisement in
your paper, for a book, on mixing “American Cock-
tails” —they are hotel drinks. Since then I have been
trying to find the address in The Bulletin, but
cannot see it. Possibly you would remember the
people advertising. I would thank you for the
address of the people. Enclosed find stamps.
A pedagogue correspondent asks us “to
bring Australian prose and poetry before the
Councils of our Universities and the Head-
masters of our public schools —State schools
are well provided for. Well, aren’t we
doing it? Haven’t we been doing it for
years? But we can't expect headmasters
or University Councils to read a page that
is chiefly devoted to Australian literature.
“I find,” says our correspondent, “that these
headmasters (mostly imported) will have
none of Australian literature. A Cantab-
Oxon. remark actually used was: ‘Oh. I
can’t bring myself to read Australian
books!’ ” But there are signs: Last year in
A Victorian Anthology for Schools, issued by
the Melb. University, there were graciously
included selections from Lawson. Cuthbert-
son and Kendall. This book was the English
Text for the Junior Public Examination for
1915.
Fiction
Casuals of the Sea, by William McFee
(Seeker, per Dymoek's Library), is an excel-
lent novel. The author knows what he is
writing about, and the variety and extent
of his knowledge is bewildering. His can-
vas is a large and crowded one, and the
figures in his foreground have three dimen-
sions. One feels, after this intimate and
detailed presentation of the whole dam’
Gooderich family that they actually existed.
Yet the book rises to no heights of literax - y
art: it is bound to be popular. Boldly,
McFee has fashioned his novel as a piece
of life, subject to all the chances and acci-
dents that affect or extinguish life.
To those frail craft upon the restless sea
Of human life, who strike the rocks
uncharted,
Who loom, sad phantoms, near us, drearily,
Storm-driven, rudderless, with timbers
started.
To those poor casuals of the way-worn earth,
I'he feckless wastage of our cunning
schemes,
he dedicates his book. It is a story of igno-
rant and muddled lives, a story unfinished,
yet of . value. Characteristically, he allows
his first hero, the eldest son of the Gooderich
family, to die in the South African war: he
cuts him off, as war has a way of cutting
people off, in a casual paragraph. Yet the
implication remains: this youth had a genius
for the soldier’s trade. All that inchoate
brilliance is wasted by a shrapnel-burst. A
less sincere author would have left him to
distinguish himself in the present war. His
younger brother then becomes the hero—and
all that this soul of immense potentialities
achieves is a voyage as a fireman to Japan
and back, marriage to a managing woman,
brief fatherhood and death. Only the sister,
who ;akes the easier way open to girls of her
class, survives. After an affair with a
man, she took other men, then tired of that
life and married one of the men whom she
had met in her professional days. She ends
respectable, happy in her motherhood and in
the successful use of a small but commer-
cially valuable talent. If there is a moral
at all, it seems to be that a girl who knows
her way about will fight her way into a good
position. But it is certain that McFee
intends no moral at all, except that of the
unnecessary inevitable waste of good human
material under the conditions of modern
civilisation.
As is the custom nowadays, McFee’s por-
trait of this commonplace family is a full-
length one. He begins at each birth and
carries us right through each life. Minnie, the
daughter who early found out what’s what,
is a fascinating and convincing portrait of a
modern type of girlhood.
“This smiling slip of a girl in her cheap,
worn dress and the crackling banknotes in
her clenched hand, was instinctively a realist.
She might give him the purple buskins of
imperial romance while she thought of him
regardless of time and condition, but, in-
stantly, when the dream condensed to fact,
when they met face to face, she could only
be reached by the cash nexus, by dinners and
theatres, by things that cost money... .That
she loved him was indisputable, but her love
was not the supreme surrender which many
would have it, the deathless clinging many
think it to be. She was Danae in her tower
of brass, and Jove himself could only enter in
the form of a shower of gold.”
Minnie’s “fall,” or, as the author prefers to
call it, her emancipation, came when Anthony
ordered a bottle of port wine. “In many
wavs Minnie measured her present life from
that port wine. It marked the end of the
prelude of her emancipation. Thereafter
she stepped deliberately into the milieu of
the. first act. And now it should be suc-
cinctly stated in her defence that her life
had been in many ways restrained and
modest. That she had been the mistress
of more than one man stands clear against
her.. She should have been faithful, I
admit, just as men should be perfect. She
failed. I suspect, for the same reason that
men fail, for the same reason that a sow’s
ears are unsuitable material for silk purses.
.. . .Even crime, if co-ordinated and run on
business lines, loses its essentially criminal
aspect—this fact has a vital bearing on the
outlook of a woman such as Minnie Goode-
rich .... Her callous independence of soul
was an inevitable product of our age. We
have cackled of the romance of business
until business is our only romance. We
have slain Poesie, and her pale phantom
stalks amid our stark realities unrecognised
and undesired. We have seen Art on the
street selling herself for money, but we have
lost the right and the impulse to rebel.”
Well, Minnie comes out of it all right,
happy, contented and respectable. Assur-
edly there is no moral.
We do not reach the sea till the conclud-
ing part of the novpl; and when we do it is
worth while. McFee knows the sailorman
and the stokehold. His description of that
voyage to Japan is as graphic and as con-
vincing as the work of Conrad, minus that
master’s unique art. There are scenes
herein that the reader cannot forget. It
is a fine tribute to the sea and the manliness
that it engenders—even in the stokehold.

Australian Mystic Scries, Yol. I. v 0 1-
The Secret of Happiness, by Angus P Turn
iey That ‘‘Yol 1., No 1,” looks so patheti-
cally hopeful: how often it has been the
only child in the family! However, Angu
Francis Turnley states that “the thoughts I
express were born in Australia, and are my
own.” Here is a bright Australian Turnlev
thought: “Then there was borne upon the
wind the music of the lapping of the waters
on the shores, the murmurs of the rivers on
their way to the sea, the rustling of the foli-
age of the trees, and the incense of the bloom
of the flowers; and Nature, folding the old
man to her bosom, said : ‘Come with me for
your pilgrimage is o’er, and you have
reached Futurity, where Mutualism, unbe-
dimmed and glorious, crowns her children
with diadems of never-ending Happiness.' ’’
The author of this affecting tale would be •
glad “to receive written communications
from, or arrange personal interviews with”
ail those who are in sympathy with my Mys-
tic Modernism, in order that" an ever-widen-
ing Circle of Communion may be established
for the promotion of Communal Happiness.”
His business address is 25 Tattersall’s-lane,
off Lonsdale-street, Melb. Here’s a chance
for all who love unbedimmed and glorious
Mutualism!
J*
John Watson, the modest author of The
Bible a Fable — The Creator Libelled (Toma-
lin, Harris-street, Sydney), points out that:
Truth, like the violets, never seek the sun,
Content to let old time prove that it won.
His chapter headings are somewhat striking:
“Christ and Jonah. Jonah done his three
days in the whale’s belly, but Christ, from
the Cross, went to Paradise, till time was
up.”... .“Joshua’s command to the Sun, and
the insolence of the Scotch Church to curb
the Governor’s freedom, unearth the enemy
in our midst.” Perhaps his meaning may
be made more clear by the author’s chal-
lenge : “The author challenges the Church
to prove that he has not produced prims
facie evidence that the Devil owned Adam
and Eve; that the Devil lived in the Ark;
that Moses was a plagiarist; that when
Christ died there was not a tried Christian
on earth; that if the day ever came when
any one sect should gain supreme power, that
the rack and stake would have a place in its
curriculum; that humanitarianism is the only
safety valve the world will ever know; that
the Church has never drafted a proposal hav-
ing for its object the sweeping away of unre-
stricted individualism; that it lionises the
man who, having grown fat by using
Hodge’s sweat, returns a trifle towards his
burial; that the only footsteps left on the
sands of time have been starvation, tyranny
and murder, against those who refuse to bow
to the myth called faith.” The author asks,
but unfortunately does not answer, the ques-
tion, “Why was I born?” —asks it. too. in
verse. And after more than a hundred
pages of impassioned argument, he tells the
reader:—
This is what it’s all about,
Gentle reader, mind the knout.
Cleanse this stain from off our map,
And see thy brother in a Jap.
«
Persons named Moses and others are re-
minded that the competition for the best
Ten Commandments for Australians closes
on June 17. A guinea will be given to the
law T -giver who forwards by that date to
this page the Decalogue most suited to the
moral and spiritual needs of this continent.
“Jay-Jay” confirms “Jimmie Pannikin’s
complaint of the neglect of Australian litera-
ture in our schools:—
Amongst the 60 or more books supplied to schools
as supplementary readers about half a dozen are
Australian in character. In the old School Readers,
now consigned to the scrapheap, three of the poetical
selections in the whole series were by Australians.
The number of prose selections was certainly larger,
but they were by no means happily chosen. I'’the
new order of things—the school magazines—which
have replaced the out-of-date readers, little im-
provement is seen. During the last three months
about 30 verse selections have appeared in them.
Of these one only is by an Australian writer. As
regards the prose selections, only one of the maga-
zines has had anything bearing on matters Aus-
tralian.
To this we may add that The School
Paper, issued by the Queensland Dept of
Instruction, makes a special feature ot
Anzae day in its April issues.

burning sensation of inflammation of the
bladder, etc.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ship of Glass.
When Arthur the King sought unknown
seas
He sailed in a ship of glass;
The sharp rocks bristled, the storm winds
blew,
But the worst that the baffled waves could
do
Set the keel like harp-strings ringing;
And Arthur, stroking his golden beard,
Mused “King nor churl need be afeared
Who sails in a ship of glass.
God speed
My glorious ship of glass!”
Too few remember, yet all who list
May sail in the ship of glass ;
For the dauntless heart she is waiting now,
With sails of samite and earven prow,
And her keel of crystal ringing;
And he who would grapple with things to be
Ready and proud to his secret quay
May summon the ship of glass—
Self-crowned
May captain his ship of glass.
No ireful tempest, no black-fanged reef
Has power on the ship of glass ;
But a whimper of terror or faint mistrust
Will shiver the ship to diamond dust,
And silence the wild keel’s ringing;
And the timorous soul will sail no more
From the bounds of his own familiar shore
The king of a ship of glass—
No more
Will look on the ship of glass.
I had rather welcome the unknown seas
From the prow of the ship of glass
Than drowse by the fire in a castle hall,
And sweeter than any maid’s love-call
Is the call of the keel a-ringing.
God keep my courage high and clear
That I may venture many a year
Far, far in my ship of glass—
Ah me,
My delicate ship of glass!
VICTORIA.
ELSIE COLE.
The Wattle Path.
’Tis a little shadowed pathway leading turn
and turn,
And the path is mine to follow through the
cool arcades;
Past a wonderland of wattle, through the
pleasant fern,
And the birds are in the hollow singing ’mid
the shades.
Lightly drops the golden pollen on the nod-
ding frond;
Sways the dainty, wild clematis, twining
like a curl;
While from out the velvet shadow of the
glade beyond
Peals the sound of merry water laughing
like a girl.
Comes a whisper from the forest shyly to
mine ear:
“Tarry here and dally here through the
shining hours”;
All the fragrance on the bi’eeze is very sweet
and clear,
Stealing up from where the valley sprawls
amid the flowers.
Oh, a bright cloud, a white cloud, is like a
silver sail,
Blowing out and flowing out across the
afternoon ;
Peeping like a timid bride a-liiding in her
veil,
Dimly gleams the pallid beauty of the early
moon!
Here to drink the mead of Life beading in
the glass ;
Here to feel the Spring of Joy pulsing in the
veins!
Autumn slirs within her groves, golden
hours w T ill pass ;
Winter rides behind the hills marshalling
the rains !
Life is ours to live and love —after life we
sleep.
Love will not live on for ever, gold will
turn to grey ;
In the winter of my age I shall always keep
Warm within my waning heart this one
golden day !
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Failure of Evangeline.
My lady’s hand is white, her face is fair,
About her falls a cloud of lustrous hair,
The dainty garments that she loves to wear
Droop round her like the mist-rain of a
fountain.
Her eyes are wells of brown and amber
light,
Her breath is subtle perfume in the night.
She moves like some soft air, sun-kissed in
flight,
That drinks the dew-sweet of a dreaming
mountain.
My lady’s voice is music that enthralls,
Like some pink oread through my life she
calls;
Hers is an aureate throat, her accent falls
As richly as the gold of all Mount
Morgan ;
Yet —such a wretch am I! —her gems of
speech,
The kind domestic lessons she would teach,
Grate on my stubborn spirit till they reach
In drear effect the vilest barrel organ.
The fault is mine. But wonder often fills
My soul when I remember ancient thrills
Of greater lovers whose remembrance spills
Like attar down the page of elder story.
Was Phryne dumb? Did Cleopatra talk?
Or did these splendid women merely walk
And breathe and bloom like lilies on the
stalk?
Wa = «ilence the immortal beauty’s glory?
N.S.W.

Bulletin rent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas S umber, post paid.

Special Xotice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge ta the “Correspondence’ column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable ), Parag aphs,
Letters or Xewspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly eonidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions trill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 25. 1916.
THE POLICY OF KEEPING A CAT
CONCEALED IN A SACK.
According to Hughes in one of his orations
to the universe, the future policy of J. Bull
must be determined by neither Freetrade
nor Protection : it must be decided by differ-
ent issues, because the life of the nation is
more important than fiscal dogma. Possibly
J. Bull is sufficiently chastened in spirit
to understand what Hughes is talking
about: it is conceivable that the voice may
sound to the British citizen as that of a
prophet preaching deliverance by short and
easy stages. But to The Bulletln it all
>->unds suspiciously like the old invitation
to attend a fiscal-sinking ceremony. Hughes
has never been a sound Protectionist. At
!>est he is a disillusioned Freetrader. And
disillusionment has come very late; most of
kis political life has been spent in either
bitterly opposing Protection or surrepti-
tiously resisting its full application to Aus-
tralian industry. Between the ex-Free-
trader who merely sees he was wrong and
the Protectionist with faith in his doctrine
and the courage to press on with it. there is
a wide difference. Australia has never seen
in even the later Hughes anything but a
lukewarm friend of Australian industry.
Before he left to embark on his current cam-
paign he deliberately dodged the obligation
his Government was under of recasting the
tariff which his own Treasurer admits to
be all wrong. While he is roaring np and
down Britain about the things other people
should do. the work he is paid to tackle in
Australia remains untouched, the industrial
life of the nation is a jest, and a Customs
schedule is in operation whic-h encourages
wild public and private extravagance. How
can the results of past cowardice and
neglected opportunity be corrected by con-
tinuing to evade the clear call of duty, while
casting round for fresh **issues : That
only means rolling stones up hills and dash-
ing off in pursuit of the nnfragrant herring.
* * * *
Neither Hughes nor anyone else can sub-
stitute for Freetrade and Protection any
other factors for determining a nation’s fiscal
policy. These go to the heart of all policy,
and upon them rest the whole weight of the
economic structure. Certainly it is possible
to huddle economic and industrial considera-
tions away out of sight for a time. Aus-
tralia knows this through bitter experience
with dodgers and loanmongers and revenue-
tariff fiends who masquerade as something
else. Britain knows all about it after years
spent in assisting to strengthen the economic
fabric of other countries, while her own
industrial life was being paralysed: But
the reckoning comes with a rush, and it came
long ago to Australia, though the settle-
ment of the bill was deferred by the despe-
rate and demoralising device of pawning the
Australian kid. That has not been a
national policy. It has been the betrayal of
policies and of the people. And it has been
accomplished mostly because of an intolerant
dislike to the healthy discipline of fiscal
“dogma” (otherwise economic honesty) and
a hankering after the delightful sensation
of decorating the head with vine leaves and
straggling along primrose paths hunting wild
cats and other products of the boom. To
Australian ears Hughes's verbal eruptions
about “issues” therefore mean nothing.
These yarns about something else being more
important than the issue as between home
trade and foreign trade have in various forms
pervaded Australia since the arrival of the
oldest inhabitant. The ancient Parkes
used to work them off- Reid traded on them
for years. They were weary old perjuries
long’ before the Sosh tiger appeared on the
landscape. And always Hamburg and
Chicago have profited by them.
* * * •
Whatever he may pass to J. Bull, it is
certain that hot air is not what Australia
will want to be served with by Hughes
when he gets back. Australia will want to
know whether he is a doer as well
as a valiant talker, and to find ont
whether the Australian tariff is going to be
amended so that it may be an instrument
of protection to Australian industry, instead
of one for wringing taxation out of the
community and getting a second cut at the
loan money. This tariff issue is a clear one.
It can only be decided satisfactorily by re-
nouncing the F reetrade heresies still cling-
ing to political parties. The industries of
other countries have nothing to do with
Australia. They are the concern of the
people directly interested, and it is their
business to attend to them in their own way.
Incidentally they do this with a close atten-
tion to detail. The object of an Australian
tariff must not be to penalise or interfere
with or subsidise oversea industry, but to
build up Australian industry—to rescue
from existing chaos the country’s own
trade and give direct opportunity to such
enterprise as the Australian people may pos-
sess. There are no other issues. The issue
is whether Australia shall be made a self-
contained country—whether, like other
people, Australians shall produce what they
require and in manly self-reliance live by
their own labors. Settlement of that is not
going to be helped by having an uproar
about King O'Mallet's hat or the wicked-
ness of parsons who bicycle on Sundays.
GENTLEMEN. YELLOW JACK!
Dr. Cleland. Director of the N. S. Males
Laboratory of Microbiology, has suggested
that the outbreak of dengue at Mnrwillumbah
represents a “mutant of yellow fever.*’ In
plain Billjim language this means that he
regards dengue as yellow fever with the
sting ont, just as chicken-pox may be
regarded as smallpox with the death chill
off and some slight variations. Certainly
Cleland has been jumped upon from the
scientific standpoint for suggesting it: but.
apart from this, what would be. the position
if yellow fever arrived in Australia with
its sting in good working order? It has an
excellent chance of getting here. The open-
ing of the Panama Canal places onr east
coast within 25 days of such pest holes as
Guayaquil, where yellow fever is continually
present, and direct steamers are a regular
thing whenever the slides in the Culebra Cut
permit. The Stegomyin fasciota mosquito
which does the infecting is the commonest
domestic mosquito in Northern Australian
coastal towns anywhere from Grafton round
to Perth—the well-known “tiger*' mosquito
unpleasantly familiar to all who have visited
the Australian sub-tropics in summer or
autumn. It is allowed to breed unchecked
in every house-tank and blocked roof gutter,
and in all manner of domestic water collec-
tions. over thousands of miles of Australian
coast, partly because the clerical Under-
secretaries who translate such things to the
politicians when anybody happens to think
about them do not believe in “nonsense”
about mosquitoes, and partly because Mr.
Bumble and other prominent voters don't
want the expense of tackling the job on
their own property. Queensland once
started ont with a flourish of political trum-
pets to eradicate the beast fr< m the main
centres of population with a view to keep-
ing out yellow fever and dengue: but after
the glamor wore off, and despite the protests
of the Health Commissioner, the scheme was
gradually pared down to nothing. The
last stage has now been reached by putting
the work on to the municipal councils, and
vaguely promising that “the Health Depart-
ment will see that it is done.” When a
Health Department is unable to secure
proper management of a badly-run sanitary
service whose depot is within five minutes’
walk of the Department's own office, the pros-
pects of making a success through Mr.
Bumble of such elaborately technical
work as mosquito-destruction are consider-
ably poorer than those of obtaining iced
drinks in Hades.
♦ ♦ * ♦
The yellow-fever mosquito is an excellent
sea-traveller, and. provided it escapes an
avenging hand or other sudden end. will
live for 60 days or more. Once infected by
feeding on a yellow-fever case during the
first three days of the iljness. it becomes
able to transmit infection on the thirteenth
day and remains infections for the remainder
of its life. Onr local breed only requires the
original case, and the funerals will commence.
Elaborate quarantine precautions are taken
to prevent the admission by sea of cases in
human beings, but it is obviously impossible
to hnnr over a l*‘».00O-tnn steamer for a
quarter-inch mosquito, and one mosquito
would be sufficient. Distance is no protec-
tion. France. Spain, Italy and England
have been infected more than once by mos-
quitoes from South American vessels, and
relatively severe attacks have occurred in
the three first-mentioned countries. The
disease has not remained for long there, be-
cause the European climate does not suit
the Stegomyia mosquito. But the Austra-
lian climate, from Newcastle northwards,
suits it admirably. Yellow fever is very diffi-
cult to diagnose in its earlier stages, and
Australian doctors are not familiar with it.
It is unlikely that the first cases would be
identified as yellow fever, and the first posi-
tive information which would reac-h the
Health Authorities would be accompanied
by the sure and certain knowledge that in-
fected mosquitoes were loose in unknown
numbers and uncertain places.
* * * *
Now. mosquito destruction in dealing with
yellow fever is an extremely technical nn-
dertaking. requiring large stocks of material
and a trained staff of men. The only
trained staff in Australia was disbanded by
the Queensland Government on the first
breath of war-economy. There are no stocks
of material ready. Material can be obtained
or improvised, but trained men cannot. The
mortality from yellow fever is about oO per
cent. —one in every two cases dies, and dies
in a very unpleasant way. Other countries
have a healthy fear of yellow fever, so the
trade and commerce of an infected Aus-
tralia would be subjected to severe restric-
tions until snc-h time as the outbreak was
indisputably under control. Getting it nnder
control would be a matter for the six State
Health Departments working nnder six State
Health Acts all varying widely in their
powers, without any trained mosquito 3taffs,
and governed by six State politicians,
assisted by an indefinite number of Under
Secretaries. Chief Clerks, political friend-,
newspaper editors and prominent sup-
porters, all in a violent state of funk. The
Commonwealth Quarantine Service has
various more or less indefinite powers to
make regulations and to co-operate with
States under such conditions, but the enthu-
siastic manner in which the State Health
officers seem to have gone after the scalp of
the Chief Federal Quarantine man over the
Sydney smallpox outbreak renders it very
unlikely that any co-operation would be pos-
sible in the early stages. And it is the
early stages which count most in yellow
fever outbreaks.
* * * *
Each Health Act operates only within its
own State. If a yellow fever suspect from
Queensland climbed the fence into N. S.
Wales a yard ahead of the pursuing health
officers, he conld spread his disease unchecked
until the N. S. Wales myrmidons got on his
track armed with totally different powers
under a totally different Health Act. It is
an absurd position. Yellow fever is only one
of several disease enemies, any one of which
would work incalculable harm to Australia
if it once gained a footing. We have the
past experience of the plague days, and we
have the present reminders of smallpox in
Sydney and cerebro-spinal meningitis in our
military camps. War-stress renders the
maintenance of the public health an even
more outstanding national duty than it is
in time of peace. The press teems with
bright proposals to amalgamate the State
and Federal machinery for conducting elec-
tions. for assessing income tax and land
tax. and for lighthouses and pilot services.
Why not amalgamate health services, and get
this vitally essential matter under one con-
trol? Health Departments have little or no
value to State Governments, and are always
getting them into trouble with Bumble and
Alderman Boggs and other prominent sup-
porters. They cost a considerable amount of
money which would, from the State political
point of view, be better spent in filling np
Boggs and Bumble with food and drink to
keep them sweet, or in remunerative and re-
productive public works carried ont by Nob-
ton Gbiffiths. Boggs and Bumble might
c-ry ont in pain at this new attack of the
Socialistic tiger, but they would not be heard
very far off in these noisy days. With all
health administration under one control Aus- -
tralia will have some chance of escaping the
disease dangers which now threaten her from
within and without. Even if she does not
wholly escape she will be saved from the risk
of treatment by six different doctors who
regard her ailment from six different points
of view, and who are guided in their pre-
scribing by six politicians. 18 Under-Secre-
taries. 29 Chief Clerks, S 4 potential friends
and 319 prominent supporters.
CONCERNING RELIEF EXPEDITIONS.
The need for a relief expedition to the
South Pole, to bring home the other expedi-
tion which has been mislaid in those regi.>n<.
affords proof that the element of luck in
these frosty ventures is pretty considerable,
and that luck isn't an asset which any
party can hope to realise upon in the uncer-
tain state of the Arctic weather market. The
peril makes it a serious question whether
the whole business is worth while. Even
a Geographical S-xietv president, speak-
ing to people who knew, declared that the
total scientific value of the picnicking hadn't
so far been worth a single life. Every ex-
pedition goes out in the name of science, but
the athletic feat involved is always of far
greater interest than any results which could
add to the store of useful human knowledge.
And the general flavor of it all is not greatly
improved by the fact that the story of the
party's doings is sold to newspapers before-
hand to furnish sensational headlines in
London and New York. If anybody could
suggest a use for the big southern ice-cap
its exploration might be advisable; but. as
things stand, the venture mnst be classed
with tight-rope walking and other dangerous
acrobatic performances where the public
gape is the chief reward.
* * * * -
This habit of trusting to the relief expedi-
tion is not confined to Polar explorers. In
ventures of quite another kind and entered
into in quite a different spirit, tremendous
risks are taken with an eye always to the
relief that is expected to come if things
reach their worst. With all Europe leaning
to arms and several of the biggest nation*
already in the field, Britain sent forward a
microscopic army to the aid of Belgium The
war was only a few days old, and the enor
mous strength of Germany had not yet been
fully shown; hot the small highly-trained
British force went forward to risk‘de?tm
non, with a relief expedition in its mind if
it didn't succeed in bursting through to Ber
liu. Learning nothing from experience the
war authorities sent the Anzae army to the
Dardanelles on an exactly similar plan it
was to have a try and then wait for the re-
lief expedition. It tried magnificently but
the relief didn't come, or didn't come’soon
enough and in the right way; and the Turks
had to be allowed to keep their old city a
little longer. In the Euphrates valley‘the
same plan was pursued. There the relief
actually arrived, bot found itself blocked bv
mud, guns and other inconveniences to such
an extent that it conld not avert disaster
Other relief expeditions may or may not be
fitting out for other centres of misfortune-
but the British faith in the principle still
seems unshaken, and possibly somebody will
still be relieving something when the war
lies down and dies of sheer exhaustion or
blows itself to pieces with an extra charge
of explosives.
* * * *
In some respects the settlement of Austra-
lia is still in the condition of the original
expeditionary party which came here taking
the risks and casting a backward look by the
road it had come in the fear that at any
moment it might have to call for the organi-
sation of relief. That original party never
had any sort of notion that unaided it could
hold this continent; and it is a painful fact
that there never has been a time in the
history of Australia when that view has
varied. Always the notion has been that if
danger threatened we should have to shout
for help to a bigger country beyond the sea
and trust for onr very existence to the
help arriving before it was too late.
Considering our distance from England and
the long months occupied in making English
military preparations, onr hope wasn't at
any period a very brilliant one. Some time
after the establishment of the Commonwealth
the determined efforts of those who took
Australian nationalism seriously succeeded
in getting a rudimentary fleet and army cre-
ated. A few ships were built and the elder
children were partially trained to fill their
place in a citizen army. The thing thus
accomplished fell far short of the wishes of
those who desired to see the manhood of the
continent organised for defence; but it was
a beginning and gave some sort of promise
for the future. Then the war broke out and
onr small fleet proved itself. It is now com-
mon knowledge that in the early months of
the trouble, while Germany still had some
force in the Pacific, the city of Sydney only
escaped bombardment through the fact that
Australia possessed one powerful ship.
With all onr military activity since hostili-
ties began, the outlook is still unaltered, and
we still live in the belief that if this conti-
nent were seriously endangered we must look
to Europe for support. This habit of waiting
for the relief expedition is pernicious and. if
persisted in. must in the end be fatal. With
the examples of Belgium and Servia before
ns. we know, or ought to know, .that how-
ever good the inrentions of Britain might he,
and however willing her statesmen might be
to send aid, onr position in case of attack
would be hopeless long before that aid could
arrive. This was probably the truth all
along • but former generations of Austra-
lians were —luckily perhaps—blind to it.
Now blindness is no longer a merit and has
no further excuse. In Australian ships and
rifles and railways and c-ommonsense lies our
only hope of maintaining onrselves in this
continent. If we grasp that fact we have
still a chance.
* * * *
Unfortunately the habit of depending on
the relief expedition has worked itself into
the Australian character, and until we can
work it out again our national life can never
be the strong thing it might be. Our states-
men conduct all matters as if they were ex-
perimental ; and their actions are only the
reflection of the settled—-or unsettled-
thought of the people. Nothing is done as it
it seriously mattered, or as if all the future
depended upon it. Ia a word, we have not
yet learned to think nationally. It is the idea
of the relief expedition, always present with
ns that prevents onr full development. *' hat-
ever we do. whatever mistakes we make,
there seems to be always the possibility of a
road our. the possibility that somebody "
help ns. If we run short of cash. Cohex wi
send the relief. If we shamefully neglect our
industries, we can exchange a little wool or
mntton for something made in Europe o
America. Such a habit of thought is as
dangerous as the same notions applied 0
defence. If Australia is to have a tutur •
that fntnre mnst he of her own ma iog-
There is no country in the world as ray
ably situated for producing all that a nig
and complex civilisation demands ; and t
is. therefore, no good reason why tms u
monwealth should not be self-contained
"esting on the soundest foundation on " 1 •
a nation can rest. What we want to gt .
firmly is the idea that we are a nation,
we ought to be able to go alone without - •
Let us give np the notion of the relief I
dition and exploit our own powers. ■
the only line of safety either in Tl ™
l*eace or when the imminent danger thre

The Finances of German) .
The latest dollar dictator to get *“ s . js
views cross-headed in Australian da .
the son-in-law of James Hnx. * an -
can railway magnate." This blcatec P
states that “Germany is beaten owing
the **financial strain'* which will not 1
her to struggle through “another
campaign.” Persons of the Hi , as
of thinking talk of “banknapt
they talk of bankrupt railway «>inpa _.
ignoring the fact that a nationa ?' npV
ment manufactures its own •
and a railway company doesnt. 1 # or
truth is that Germany can keep go o


j ns t as long as it can produce its own food,
war material and clothing. If it can pro-
vide itself with those things it can, so far
as material matters are concerned, go
on for ever. And, helped by its occupation
0 f foreign territory, it seems quite as well
circumstanced in this respect as any of the
Allies. Hence the talk of the failure or alleged
failure of loans may be entirely disregarded,
except insofar as it may indicate a weaken-
ing of that “will to conquer” of which we
once heard so much. People get perplexed
by - this problem simply because it is so
vast. If they will reduce the number of
people involved to a hundred, who occupy
an island, they will be better able to realise
that the lot can hold out against an enemy
so long as there is food, clothing and mili-
tary gear. lOU’s given in payment by one
to the other may rise in a pile 1000 ft. high,
but that won’t alter the facts regarding the
islanders’ powers of resistance. We have put
our faith since this war broke out in a
hundred false gods. We have been urged
by our misleaders to always consider not
o ' U r own real weaknesses, but the hypothe-
tical weaknesses of the enemy. It is time
we ceased either to be dupes of others or to
dupe ourselves. Until we face the whole
facts squarely we shall never be likely to
make our utmost effort.

The Qualifications for a Government Job.
The essence of Justice Pring’s report on
what is known as the “petrol scandal” in
N. S. Wales is (1) that there is no evidence
that Griffith, the member of the Ministry
who was brought into the affair, was guilty
of corruption, but that there are some sus-
picious circumstances; (2) that Morgan,
the Government’s agent appointed by Grif-
fith, tried to work a crooked deal; (3) that
Heed, the officer of the Works Department
to whom Griffith had delegated the job of
handling the business, knew of Morgan’s
devious proceedings and didn't say anything
to Griffith about them. As Morgan has
since been arrested and there will be a fur-
ther attempt to get at the innards of things,
the matter can for the present be left to
stand where it is, with the certainty that
additional ventilation will do no harm. But
while the public is judicially holding both
its tongue and its nose, there is one phase
of the matter that is of considerable noli-
tical importance apart altogether from the
question of individual culpability. This is
the revelation of the manner in which the
State’s business is conducted. In this case
there was a presumably serious inten-
tion of embarking upon a scheme of
nationalisation involving enormous capi-
tal expenditure and expert administra-
tion. The evidence discloses that from
first to last the project was handled bv the
politicians with rather less wit and caution
than could reasonably have been expended
on the purchase of a mouse-trap. Griffith
was directed by Cabinet in 1914 to “inquire
into the establishment of a petrol monopoly”
and promptly directed Reed to do the in-
quiring, after warning him to be careful, as
charges of corruption might be one of the
fruits of his labors. Griffith knew nothing
about petrol. Reed’s ignorance of it was
profound. Morgan, who later on was
appointed by Griffith to assist Reed, but
without any arrangement regarding salary,
knew nothing about petrol either. And in
charge of these three persons, whose know-
ledge of what they were handling was fully
equal to their stock of information about
the extraction of radium from turnips, the
great scheme of nationalising the trade in
petrol drifted round like a sampan in com-
mand of a dead Chinaman. Governments
which conduct the taxpayer’s business in
this way can be relied upon to do only one
thing—make a mess. Taking the most
charitable view of the facts there is nothing
to see except incompetence and recklessness.
A suburban greengrocer who wanted to
acquire a sack of cabbages would show im-
measurably greater capacity for business.
If he didn’t and got into the Bankruptcy
Court, as he almost certainly would, the
Judge would suspend his certificate for a
year or two. Incidentally, he would then
have an excellent chance of being appointed
agent to assist the in the pur-
chase of locomotives. Griffith s agent had
proved his special qualifications twice over.
He had “failed” in Westralia as well as
in N. S. Wales.

The Practical Training of the Australian
Kid.
Wails about the youth who works in a shop
and doesn’t “prosecute his studies” at a con-
tinuation school by night were heard issuing
from the Education Department in Sydney
the other day. Assorted noises concerning
the need for more technical education are
frequently detected in Melbourne. f rom
Brisbane last week the Associated Chambers
of Commerce issued a dignified demand for
“the appointment of several Royal Commis-
sions” to investigate this _ business of indus-
trial training. Considering that about l
Commissions have already investigated the
subject, that a regiment of alleged specialists
have at various times been sent around the
world, at the public expense, to investigate
some more, and that the taxpayers'are finding
the salaries for innumerable persons whose
sole claim to the money is that they know
all about the matter, this incitement from
Brisbane to get rid of more cash in several
places seems uncalled for. Anyhow, what
do these people want? There must be. a
practical side to this business of industrial
training. Unless training leads somewhere
it is obviously futile. There is, for instance,
no point in erecting an expensive institution
and paying experts to teach young Australia
how to make beer-pumps if the country allows
beer-pumps to come in free or thereabouts.
Nor is there likely to be any useful result
apart from an increase in taxation from in-
structing the youth of the country in the
production of electrical and chemical goods
if vast quantities of these things are flung out
on the wharves ready made. But in ordei
that waste may be checked and the Austra-
lian youngster given a fair deal and public
policy regarding industry and education
reconciled, something certainly requires to lie
done. Australia is going on the wrong lines
new, admittedly. Such technical training as
is given is inadequate, and yet equips the
child with a skill that is not wanted. There-
fore The Bulletin suggests that under the
War Precautions Act the existing system be
up-ended and schools established throughout
the Commonwealth where lads may receive
instruction that will fit them for the kind of
job that is waiting for them, the curriculum
to cover training in :
1. Driving carts (bread, meat, fish and rabbit) ;
conducting lorries to wharves and warehouses; de-
livering bricks, stone, timber (imp.), lemonade,
milk, pianos, vegetables, millinery, new-laid eggs
and headache powders.
2. Digging post-holes, erecting fences, pushing
wheelbarrows, manipulating hoes, rakes and spades,
wearing bowyangs, making tea in a billy, cutting
lawns and trimming a mate’s whiskers, filling in
time and a hole in the road, repairing clocks and
fowlhouses.
3. Sawing up wood, killing sheep, carrying water,
going for the mail, finding Strawberry, putting slip-
rails in position, digging a well, hiding behind the
shed, having a yarn with the cook.
4. Extracting bales, boxes, crates, bundles and
sacks from the interior of vessels, whether propelled
by steam or wind; piling the aforesaid commodities
in sheds; wheeling boxes and bales about on trucks;
filling vessels with raw meat, greasy wool, butter,
cheese and luggage; waiting for more vessels.
5. Public speaking, getting into Parliament, run-
ning a newspaper, fixing the price of wheat, negotiat-
ing with shindykits, building the national capital,
planning strategical railways, interviewing Cohen,
acting as censor, manufacturing yells and develop-
ing our great National Resources.
6. Carrying a swag, looking for a job, studying
law and the “Wanted” columns of the daily rag.
It is no use letting things slide any longer.
The nation must be organised on the basis
of public policy. No other organisation is
possible, and as public policy is dead against
Australian industry, and all for loans and
bloat and shirk and the wood-and-water joey,
why not be honest about it all and give the
kids a decent show to make goodV
The Pursuit of War Profits.
The Bill introduced by Treasurer Higgs for
the Federal taxation of war-time profits is so
elaborate a piece of machinery that it will
help poor old Costs to face the rise in the
price of groceries with a light heart. It
bristles with “standards” and “allowances”
and complicated arithmetic. There is a
profit standard, a pre-war standard and a
percentage standard; also a statutory per-
centage and provisions for variations and
modifications. It is not likely that war
profits of any considerable magnitude are
being made in Australia except in metals
and shipping and by a few individuals doing
business with the Defence Department. Yet
it is a fair thing for the Commonwealth to go
in pursuit of all such excess earnings as there
may be. The result of the present Bill,
however, will be to take a big bite out of the
profits of some concerns that have yielded
poor returns and no bite at all from others
that have wallowed in fat. And there will
be other queer results. For instance, take
the case of the Hot Air Co., which made a
pre-war profit of £7OOO. equal to 7 per cent,
on its capital, and makes now £lO,OOO. From
the excess of £3OOO there will be a deduction
of £2OO, leaving £2BOO, which lliggs and the
directors of the Hot Air Co. will cut up.
Frofit to Higgs, £l4OO. The Wild Cat Co.,
on the other hand, on a watered capital of
£140,000, made a 5 per cent, profit of £7OOO
before the war, and now makes £lO,OOO. On
this excess Higgs allows the co. 2 per cent, to
make up the statutory standard for a private
concern, which means £2BOO. This leaves a
balance of £2OO. which goes in deduction.
Profit to the Wild Cat, £3OOO. Profit to
Higgs. Od. There seems something wrong
with this, especially as the Wild Cat people
may have secured the whole of their extra
profit from the war, while the Hot Air
brotherhood may have got their lift from
producing a larger number of pumpkins or
by employing more men. Moreover, the long
list of exemptions from the Bill is calami-
tous. Why should there be any exemptions
or any deductions? The idea of exempting
Costs, for instance, is the wildest folly ; and
why the Cow Cocky and the pastoralist should
be chased and the agriculturist let go free
is a detail that Higgs has not explained.
Holman and the Labor Party.
Dear Bulletin, —Whatever the faults and
the follies of Labor League Executives and
Party Caucuses (and the Lord knows
I never made the error of venerating
them), Holman has brought all his
troubles on himself. I have known him
intimately for 25 years, and long ago found
that his ability at getting _ out of tight
corners is only equalled by his stupidity in
getting into them. Starting his political
career with an alleged belief in a Socialism
evolved by Karl Marx and his own omni-
science, he engineered the “ironclad
pledge of ’93 whereby the first Labor
party was split into fragments. Only
a few of the original crew sailed
into port the following year, _ with 14
of the “solidarities,” minus tlieir leader,
who had fallen overboard at Leich-
hardt. Holman was beaten again in ’95,
end eventually reached Parliament in ’9B.
No sooner had the old parties amalgamated
and the Labor party become the direct
Opposition than Holman asked the next
Labor Conference to entirely abolish the
pledge which he had all along passionately
advocated as the Corner Stone of Laborism.
He was turned down. He then manoeuvred
to get the “restriction of borrowing” plank
whittled down till it meant nothing. _ In
this he was successful. His one consistent
policy since has been to secure the selection
of candidates with a chance of winning, re-
gardless of whether their principles were
sound or rotten. This accounts for the char-
acter of his following. Many of them are
mere camp-followers of the Labor army.
For two years he has held out the bait of
Upper House appointments to a crowd of
anxious aspirants, while those quite out of
the running for M.L.C.-ships have been in-
cited to see visions of inspectorships under
the many new industrial laws which were
promised. These things for delegates, male
and female, and constant work for the rank-
and-file per medium of a “vigorous borrow-
ing policy,” are the means by which Hol-
man has debauched the Labor movement of
N.S.W. His courageous attitude over the
Boer war, his honeyed eloquence and per-
sonal charm of manner, his undoubted skill
and resource in emergencies, and his bound-
less though frequently misapplied energy
were assets which made it possible for him
to succeed where most men would have
failed. A generous friend with many fine
qualities it is almost impossible to dislike
Holman ; yet, with it all, he has been
the evil genius of the Labor movement by
consistently side-tracking it from its true
objective. But, though he has dazzled Labor
with his brilliance and audacity, he has never
completely won its trust, and those who
could see through the glamor to the cold,
hard facts beyond have been on the watch
for years. The Initiative Referendum and
Recall are more really live issues now than
they ever were in the past, and while the
“Industrials” of the late N. S. Wales Con-
ference were hastily organised and used
their power crudely, they have at least
shown this “smart young man” that he can-
not fool all the people all the time. When
they weed out a few of their own weaklings
and consolidate their organisation, Holman
and his colleagues will either have to run
straight or walk the plank.—l am yours,
etc., Boomerang.
The Frightfulness of Canberra.
As the Australian taxpayer is going to
have the privilege of paying the bill for
another Royal Commission, this time in con-
nection with Canberra, it looks as if some-
body ought to rise to the occasion and
agitate for the appointment of a permanent
Commission to go round overhauling every-
thing in sight. ■ The time is far away in
the distance when the average politician
handled his job efficiently and without
extravagance. Therefore a Commission
leaping about the country promiscuously,
exposing muddle and waste, would exercise
a wholesome influence by announcing the
dreadful details before they commenced to
putrify and call attention to themselves. As
things are now it is only when a smell
becomes a public nuisance that a Commis-
sion is appointed to go and see what has
happened; but as a fresh aroma breaks out
every week, reinforcements are clearly neces-
sary. The smell from Canberra had of
course reached the dignity of a stink away
back in the dark ages. The whole story of
the wretched place is a blot on the nation.
Canberra was born in a squalid intrigue
and ‘ever since it has been the centre of
waste and muddle and perjury and loaf. The
latest evidence on the subject was tendered
by Postmaster-General Webster the other
day in the House of Reps.—about a railway
put in the wrong place, and the head-
quarters of a sewer where the Town Hall
ought to be, and a power-house decorating
the address reserved for something else, and
£BOO,OOO going up the spout, and more of
the dreary old yarn about let-’er-slide. Web-
ster, incited apparently by O’Malley, for
the purpose of giving ex-Home Affairs
Archibald one in the neck —which, by the
way, he deserved, as this paper showed 12
months ago—damned the whole show -from
beginning to end and incidentally damned
O’Malley and the Government and every-
one connected with it. Therefore, as the
Ministry is in a state of hopeless confusion
regarding Canberra and cannot make up its
mind what to do or what to leave alone or
bow to run the local wheelbarrow, there is
going to be another Commission of Inquiry.
The job of building the National Capital
seems to have been mistaken by Australian
Governments as a job at _ shovelling the
national capital down a drain.
The Discovery of Snowy River.
Although the details of the expedition have
not been made public, it is to be inferred
from the attitude of the N. S. UV ales Gov-
ernment that some party of heroic adven-
turers has at last succeeded in bursting
through dense forests of prejudice and howl-
ing wildernesses of official ignorance, and
found the Snowy River. Years ago, when
a worthy Federal capital for Australia
appeared to be a thing really desired, this
paper pressed the claims of the region about
Dalgety as offering the best site for a great,
clean, permanent city which might fairly
claim to be representative of all that was
best in the Commonwealth. But, though
the recommendation of the Government’s
own Commissioner was turned down and
the Canberra swindle was perpetrated, all
the time the Snowy River has been running
on and the opportunity to use it is still
there. If the State Government would take
up its use in earnest as a part of a grand
plan of decentralisation, it might yet redeem
a lot of its past failures. The temporary
capital at Canberra can go on eating up
cash until the real capital is finally estab-
lished somewhere else; but there is no
reason why the city by the Snowy with all
the features of a great national undertak-
ing should not proceed. At present the idea
is to develop electrical power there. And
the nearer the factories are to the source
of the power the greater the economy ; and
where the factories are there will the homes
be. also. So the city planning and the erec-
tion of power plants should go side by side ;
and to link up the whole into a sane and
complete proposition the railway from the
Snowy to its natural outlet at Twofold
Bay should be immediately constructed. It
would not be a long railway nor a specially
costly one; but it might have tremendous
significance in the development of the State.
In plain fact, the Snowy River scheme, pro-
perly tackled by men with and pur-
pose, might readily save N. S. Wales from
most of the ill effects of the after the-war
slump.

The British policy of suppressing news
likely to foment suspicion of the “loyalty”
of naturalised aliens has reached a point
where the safety of the public is clearly in-
volved. One of last week’s cables reports
an amazing case of Government persistence
in its policy of hush: —
In the House of Commons to-day Mr. H. J. Ten-
nant, Under-Secretary for War, replying to Sir A. B.
Markham, said that it was not in the public interest
to confirm or contradict the statement that telephone
and telegraph lines had been cut in a recent air
raid on an east coast town.
Sir A. B. Markham asked whether it was not in
the public interest to inform the country that alien
enemies were living in that district.
Mr. Tennant replied that comment was undesir-
able.
German residents of a Zep.-raided district
had endeavored to cut it off from communi-
cation with the defence forces, and the Gov-
ernment says, “Don’t mention any little cir-
cumstance of that sort.” Now, why?
Clearly not because the publication would
help the enemy. No! —but simply because
it might inconvenience some local aliens, by
leading to their internment.

The incorrigible Judge Docker distinguished
himself again during a case at the Sydney
Quarter Sessions by reproving a well-known
Sydney solicitor, who was a witness, for
putting his signature to a petition praying
for the release of a boy of 17 who had been
dealt with by Docker himself. The young-
ster was a resident of the Gordon district,
and his youth and the fact that he was an
orphan and had probably fallen under evil
influences were amongst the reasons urged
in favor of his release on license in a
petition signed by most of the leading resi-
dents of the Gordon district, including ex-
Premier C. G. Wade. Docker, having the
unfortunate solicitor before him in court
as a witness in quite another matter, took
the opportunity to give him a lecture which
practically implied that he had put his signa-
ture to “a tissue of lies.” It was a shock-
ing misuse of the judicial position—so obvi-
ously shocking that no argument need be
used on the point. Perhaps this last treat-
ment of a professional witness will do more
than the many injustices to prisoners re-
vealed by appeals from his Honor’s deci-
sions, and at length satisfy the authorities
that Docker is very old for his job and
that his years if not his services have earned
for him retirement.
Wit at appears to be a minor error on the
part of the legislators who drew' up the
N.S.W. Liquor Referendum Act might have
pretty big consequences if the six o’clock
proposal got through. It is specified that
“licensed premises” shall close at the hour
decided —not merely that the bars shall
close. “Licensed premises,” as interpreted
hitherto, include the dining-room, bedrooms,
billiard-room, stables, yard, fowl-house and
everything else connected with the hotel;
so the whole affair must go dead at the
specified time. Nobody, on the appearance
of things, will be able to get a meal, or a
bed at a country pub unless he is under
cover before the curfew rings. In the big
towns it doesn’t so much matter, because
there are other places besides hotels to eat
and sleep in ; but for the traveller on a lonely
country road the outlook is pretty cold.

The Shepherd Kings.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One of the tribes against which the Australian
troops are now fighting is supposed to be descended
from the Hyksos or Shepherd Kings—the most
ancient conquerors of Egypt.
Here where, in dim mirage, pale waters
shine,
Where all is sun-parched plain and ancient
death,
The foremost cohorts of Australia’s line
March to the testing-place with hard-held
breath.
The German guns are here, the Turks com-
mand,
But stauncher foemen hold the wastes of
sand.
These w r ho have stood in arms five thousand
years,
Stern desert warriors that no force might
bow,
Whose gleaming ranks of unaffirighted
spears
Before the Pyramids were strong as now—
These ancients of the ancients face the
guns
Of young Australia and her marching sons.
The Shepherd Kings, whose long-remem-
bered pride
Was seen in Memphis in a world so old
That savage Europe, with her strength un-
tried,
Yet crouched in caves, skin girt against the
cold,
Now bring the royal strength age cannot
tame
To face the newer battle and the flame.
And here the newest nation, whose strong
hand
Last of all peoples gripped the sword to
slay,
Is face to face upon the desert sand
With those whose thousand years is but a
day;
And Death, the first of things, must arbi-
trate
When new and old fling down the gage to
fate.
These who have seen a Pharaoh’s chariots
turn,
Who marked the rise of Nineveh and Tyre,
Who watched the fires of Alexander burn,
And Rome triumphant march to her desire,
Now, through the lightnings of the battle,
feel
They meet a foeman worthy of their steel.
N.S.W.
D.M.W.

Where Armageddon Stands Now.
At the beginning there were long accounts of
Italian advances, and the hauling of guns
away up behind the clouds —of the Austrian
being prized off remote snow-clad peaks and
being smitten with an avalanche on the "’ay
down. But there was nothing very definite
about it all except that what was going on
was horribly cold and strenuous, with the
Austrian getting the worst of the deal. Last
week there was a change. The Italian found
the other chap leaping back to where he was
before, rolling up infantry and artillery and
pushing his enemy oil to a new address.
Bulh sides are claiming to view the new
si f nation with confidence, hut as all situa-
tions are contemplated with optimism nowa-
days, this means nothing. The obvious
thing is that the associated Huns are hitting
harder and that the Italian js on the defen-
sive. Around Verdun the perpetual scrap is
being continued with unabated fury, and the
accumulation of corpse has not yet con-
vinced the German that it is time to knock
off. From Mesopotamia comes news of a
Russian cavalry force having junctioned with
llm Anglo-Indian army which went to relieve
Tow ns I-I END at Ivut, but didn’t. This may
be significant; it is impossible to say yet.
The certain thing is that on this Front the
Turk has great strength, and unless the
Russian can imneril the lines of communica-
tion with Baghdad, the troops operating from
the south have a hard job ahead, even if
they can save themselves. Stories _of
isolated engagements in Flanders and minor
disturbances along the Russian lines, with
some vague whispers about the German get-
ting ready for another attack via the Baltic,
make up the rest of a record which, in the
main, merely emphasises the long-standing
conclusion- —that next week is not going to
see the end of it, and that we cannot afford
to do anything but our very best. Mean-
while our Australian lads are in the thick of
it somewhere or other, and the frequent
information that Hughes has met some of
them at the Anzac Buffet in London is the
juiceless piffle served out in place of the news
which the Australian people would have of
their army if they were not afflicted with a
Minister who thinks the place of Australians
in the scheme of things is to “do as they are
told.”
Compelling the Grocer to Sell Rubbish.
The tied-house system which is making big
headway in Australia looks like the infan-
tile prattling of the big Trust or bigger
Honorable Understanding that is to follow.
Competition may or may not be tlie life
of trade, but it is certainly under a com-
petitive system that the public gets the
fairest deal in the choice of things on which
it must spend its cash; and while a lot
can be said in favor of monopolies on the
ground of cheaper output and distribution,
the advantage thus gained mostly goes into
the pockets of the few and rarely displays
itself on the breakfast tables of the many.
Take the case of the grocery trade. The
grocer, as his name implies, was once the
Fat Man of commerce —the grosser. He
it was who handled things in enormous
hulk and stepped with a proud foot among
the lesser tradesmen who peddled their small
wares. Now the grocer is being harried be-
tween an uncomprehending public and the
too-comprehending and too-comprehensive
suppliers of the goods that fill his shelves.
One agency secures control of a dozen lines
of goods. Of these three are popular, six
in fair demand, and three are not wanted at
all. To keep his business going the grocer
must sell the three popular articles and
might be prepared to stock the six others
which are occasionally asked for; but the
agency insists that be shall stock all twelve
or obtain none at all. To prevent absolute
loss, therefore, the man behind the counter
must make the most desperate efforts to
foist upon his customers three lines that
they do not want. The system works badly
all round. The manufacturer with a single
line which he lias been endeavoring to bring
to the highest perfection, and which in open
competition would gain first place in public
esteem, has no chance against those who
in addition to ten other lines offer a sub-
stitute for his manufacture of decidedly
inferior quality. The grocer is desperately
pushing that substitute because in failing
to sell it he must suffer direct loss. The
specialist is therefore forced to dissipate his
strength and diffuse his effort by taking on
the production of a host of things which he
would have preferred not to touch, or to
combine with a number of other makers of
a single product, in order to hold liis own
against the firm with a dozen strings to its
bow. The evil of the tied-house system in
grocery business would fie bad enough if
the manufactures were all Australian, but
the imported stuff is still with us and help-
ing to further complicate matters. Some-
body ought to get after these small
attempts at monopoly with an axe. If they
are allowed to grow they will soon be big
combines with half Australia in their
pockets.

“The Family Cupboard,” now trying its
luck at Melbourne King’s, is an interesting
study in the melodrama which combines
naturalism with impossibilities and leaves
all the conflicting elements of the story to
be reconciled by the performers. It should
be a “good acting play” for a very specially
selected company, but the finest company in
the world couldn’t give it more than a sur-
face appearance of truth. The co. at the
King’s, not being the best in the world, often
reveals the weakness of situations that are
strong in opportunities for emotional acting.
Thus pathos misses the ’bus, painful signifi-
cance takes the wrong turning, and a rather
dense audience is prompted to laugh where
it should be compelled to think. The success
of “The Family Cupboard” in New York is
as accountable as its failure in Melbourne.
Written by Owen Davies, it is a more
dramatic play than “What Happened to
Mary,” but more difficult to interpret. In
the first place, it is a sermon on the old
subject of the expensive ’Murkan matron
whose husband stays away earning dollars
whilst she gads around and gets into debt,
her debts having no direct connection with
the plot. She is just a ty 7 pe of Yankee sas-
siety7 woman of 45, with a sentimental,
waster son and an angelic sassiety daughter.
Maggie Knight is obviously too old and
rotund for the job. George Bryant is suffi-
ciently realistic as the low-spirited husband
who compensates himself for the loss of
home-comforts by keeping another girl in
clover and fine underwear. He penitently
sacks the vulgar minx when his waster son
tellg mother about her, and then he devotes
all his time to a new scheme of dollar-mak-
ing, instead of looking for a new girl. Bryant
is, in fact, a pattern husband and father in
temporary disgrace. Meanwhile the waster
son, who is an unprecedented ass, picks up
the minx his old man has dropped and pro-
poses to marry her; but when the boy’s
money has gone she prefers to bolt back to
music-hall life with a rorty pal. after which
the incredible simpleton talks of suicide and
his fond parents become re-united in order
to turn him from his praiseworthy intention
The prodigal son isn’t worth any of the fuss
that’s made about him : nevertheless Guy
Hastings attracts symnathy to the character.
On the other hand. Florrie Gleeson’s picture
of the wild, disorderly minx is an awful
injustice to the author. The character is
loosely put together, but an Ethel Irving
could give a striking impersonation of it.
There are numerous pegs of human effect for
an emotional, actress to hang her art upon.
Jeff Williams is admirably 7 matter-of-fact in
a servant part; and Nellie Bramley, Clarice
Ifardwicke. Laura Roberts and Bellairs are
more satisfactory than several other people
in minor parts, two of which —the Lawrence
Winthrops—seem unnecessary interruptions.
Three stage sets are better than good enough.
When “The Family Cupboard” comes off
“Just a Girl” will come on.

somersault of the season at Wirths’ Sydney
Hippodrome on Saturday night. Then after
the seals have sung the National Anthem the
circus will unostentatiously fold itself up

position of strategical advantage to the
ticket office. And then, before The Bulle-

the proceedings, the Wirths will have sub-
stituted for the lion’s roar a stupendous
“aqua-drama” called “Ivultur.” Vast

the darkness. The pictures, however, are
