Nothing has happened to impair the
notoriety of “So Long, Letty,” since last
week and that piece of good fortune is pro-
ceeding at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) as before.
The French Fighting Front film will be
screened for the last time at Melbourne
Auditorium on Friday, and then the violin
of Selinsky will be heard in the Hall.
Six nights of “The Irishman” are bring-
ing the Griffin season to a close at Mel-
bourne Princess’s. The play is a dishevelled
scrap of fiction featuring the rude endeavors
of a villain named Blake to make life un-
pleasant for a well-favored hero. Blake is
an old-established artist in will-snatching,
homicide and other crimes, and his only
novelty is that he carts around a degraded
assistant as a sort of mascot and omits to
force unwelcome attentions on the hero’s
cherished lady. For his part, the hero
(Vivian Edwards) spends a few months of
peace and obscurity in gaol, followed by a
period of hiding in a ruin, chiefly at tin 1
instance of the awful Blake, who easily
induces the Law to sool its available police
on to the poor young man. Blake, played
by Fred Neilseu, is a strenuous miscreant,
and only pauses in his career of blackmail
and perjury to strangle a baronet and pour
his drugged accomplice into a lake. The
latter victim (.T. P. O’Neil) is a tremulous
apparition whose lugubrious snivelling gets
a large allowance of publicity in compari-
son with Ethel Buckley’s mechanical glee
and (Jerald Griffin’s songs. Miss Buckley
is mostly off stage as Molly O’Flaherty,
and Griffin is frequently missing when his
strong right arm is required to show that
an Irish minstrel is not to hi' trifled with.
Once or twice, however, he gives the villain
a push and cheeks the pleece on the trail of
Vivian Edwards; so what wid his warbling
and his love-making he has a fair even-
ing. Next Saturday George Marlow stages
“The Spoilers.” described as a “thrilling
adaptation of Ilex Beach’s masterpiece.”
Illusionist Goldin continues to hold the
stage at Melbourne Tivoli. Goldin is adroit
in giving aged and familiar mysteries the
semblance of novelties. Indeed, he achieves
his success by appreciating the fact that
most of tin* elderly tricks of the trade are
cajiable of modern valuation. Immediately
preceding Goldin is a droll yarn-spinner and
songstress, Marie Iving-Seott, hilled as
“That Country Girl,” though she sounds
as if she had graduated in the Fniversity cl
Nuts (with honors). Cardo and Noll sing
with much feeling and lyric sweetness in
the first part of the programme, and Musette
is still there prancing to the canoodly
strains of her naughty violin.

piece is an expensively dressed mixture of
songs, dances and topical absurdities,

This week we commemorate the steady growth of
The House of Peapes, from the humble enterprise of
George Peapes and William Shaw in 1866 —since
consolidated, during 50 years’ mature experience,
into a paramount position as an exclusive house for
men’s wear.
The Jubilee catalogue details the history and expan-
sion of the firm, and contains several old-time illus-
trations hearing on the early days of the business.
It is, in addition, a comprehensive and most useful
guide for the selection of distinctive apparel, and
we shall gladly forward copies upon request.

Petrol is now used with excellent results
in turning the soldier’s most intimate enemy
out of the trenches. When the soldier can
get enough he petrolises the inside of his
vest, as well as seams and folds. So far. no
regular supplies are being handed out to the
men. They get the drips from the motor-
transports or whatever dregs they are able
to commandeer.
The law was a cheerful ass the other day
when it had to decide a claim under the
Workmen’s Compensation Act (Vie.). The
applicant, a ship’s fireman, left his job,
without permission, to go and get booze. He
returned paralytic, and celebrated the event
by falling down the hold and fracturing him-
self. Although if was his own misconduct
that had caused the injury, he was
“within the ambit of his employment” at the
time, and was therefore entitled to recover
damages. Verdict accordingly, with costs.
Justice Higgins, sitting in Federal Arbi-
tration. is wrestling with a voyage problem.
The Merchant Service Guild is asking for
an award, and the E. and A. Co. wants an
exemption. The co. is registered in London
and its vessels ply with passengers from
Sydney to Hong Kong. Japan and China.
The E. and A. people claim to be a “foreign”
company, explaining that their vessels com-
mence their voyages at Kobe and make a
round trip to Melbourne and back to Kobe.
The Guild argues that, practically, the co.
is Australian, that the vessels leave Mel-
bourne, make a round voyage to Kobe and
back to Melbourne. The co. chips back that
if it comes under the award so does the I’,
and O.—which argument would be all right
i’ the E. and A. steamers were registered
in Kobe, for then they would be going to
their home port. Higgins has confessed him-
self puzzled, and threatens to refer the
filing to the High Court.
AT POVERTY POINT.
"Masetto”: The playing of the Ver-
brugghen Quartet in Melbourne this week
leaves no doubt that no such perfect cham-
ber music has been heard in Australia since
the visit of the Mendelssohn Quintet Club
(of Boston. U.S.A.) over 30 years ago. The
perfection is. of course, the result of years
of association. Other worthy quartets may
have had as good player's ; but the Mendels-
sohn and Verbrugghen touch was lacking.
The only regrettable feature of the Ver-
brugghen concerts was the absence of most
of the violin students —the very people who
ought to have been waiting outside before
the doors opened. I was at the Goll-Ver-.
brugghen recitals six months back, and
could recognise the same faces at both sets
of concerts, but of the multitude of hoys
and girls who walk about with violins and
’cellos for company I saw’ only a few. Yet
these are those who, if they show a suspicion
of talent, want their parents or the public
to raise the wind to send them abroad for
further study, because “there’s no musical
atmosphere in Australia.” Possibly not as
much as we could wish, but quite enough for
most if they would look for it. If music
students in the past had availed themselves
of the notable opportunities of hearing the
best visiting performers when offered to
them, we should not have had the gibe
levelled at us that we are unmusical, as
proved by the financial failure of the Men-
delssohn Quintet, Wilhelmj, de Munck, the
Alva-Verne Company and Gerardy.
Genevieve Ward started her 80th year on
March 27, and received a big, encouraging
cheer from a crowded house at the St.
James's, London, where for a couple of
months she had been doing eight perform-
ances a week with Sir George Alexander
in “The Basker,” The sprightly old lady
had some difficulty in getting into her
dressing-room that night; it was (hockful
of flowers, letters and telegrams from
admirers all over the three kingdoms.
“F.R.”: There are times in Egypt when
we think we are home again. A stroll along
a Cairo street the other evening, and I was
gazing at a poster which announced that
Tom Dawson, Australia’s premier comedian,
was. appearing at a local picture-palace. In
front of the Kursal. Cairo’s leading play-
house, Violet Paget and Hugh Buckler
headed the bill. Lieutenant Hugh, who
only recently arrived from Malta after a
serious illness, was assisting at a concert
arranged for the usual war-purpose.

A quaint theatrical item is vouched for by
a man who was in Vienna at Christmas.
The most popular play then running was
“Mr. Wu,” the main reason for the popu-
larity apparently being that one of the hated
English is scored off by a Chinaman.

back to harness.
M. G. Fawkner, late manager of the Pav.
picture theatre in Adelaide, has been
acquitted after a week-long trial on a charge
of conspiring to defraud the Union Theatres
Co. One ticket-seller or taker after another
swore that tickets had been held back and
sold twice over, with the manager superin-
tending the plot and dividing the spoil. Fawk-
ner stoutly declared, however, that he was
not in the ring at all, and that his subor-
dinates, on being found out, must have
agreed to throw the blame on him and save
their own skins bv turning King’s evidence.
Josiah Symon put up one of his finest de-
fences, Judge Buchanan spoke of the danger
of accepting evidence from confessedly guilty
persons without corroboration on some
material point, and the jury, after retiring
for two hours, sa d Not Guilty. Fawkner
can therefore be i garded as the victim of
an unusually devilish gang of plotters.
There has been an odd sequel to the case
in which the late manager of Adelaide Pav.
was acquitted on a charge of conspiring
with the ticket-sellers to make a fraudulent
profit. Of the various employees who con-
fessed to wrong-doing only one was pro-
secuted, and against him the management
(Union Theatres Co.) did not push the
charge after the jury had said the manager
was not in the swindle. Judge Buchanan,
however, gave him 12 months ; on which the
Crown and the prosecuting co. both backed
up his petition for a pardon, and in four
days he was released.

“Melb” : “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford” is
suggested by (’barley Mathews's old char-
acter of Affable Hawk, in “The Game of
Speculation,” which he acted in Sydney and
Melbourne. The essential plots of the two
comedies are similar. “The Game” was
drawn from Balzac’s “Mereadet.” Webster,
at the Haymarket, was thinking of it when
Mathews, at the Lyceum, urged Lewes, after-
wards George Eliot’s husband, to hurry up
with a version for him. Lewes was dra-
matic critic of the Leader. Calling himself
Slingsby Lawrence, he wrote “The Game”
in a couple of days, and scored a great suc-
cess. The public insisted that the volatile
Mathews (then under the influence or the
extravagant Madame \ estris) was acting
his own self. The play was somewhat in-
debted to the experiences of Cbuzzlewit and
Tapley in the swindling American Eden.

A respectable claim to be the oldest Aus-
tralian native comes from Mrs. Bryant, o!
Middle Park (Vic.), who celebrated her 90th
birthday on May 8. She was born at 1 arra-
matta (N.S.W.) in 1820, crossed to Victoria
in the early ’so’s, and has enough descend-
ants to do her honor.
Binghi doesn’t often take an active part
in the work of evangelising himself, but
William Kropenyeri did. lie lias just died,
aged 80, at the Point Macleay (S.A.) mis-
sion-station, where he had been since it
started. 40 years ago. His eloquence made
its mark even in shearing-sheds, and he
backed it up with special powers on the
harmonium and piano. Incidentally he was
a first-class baker. He was one of the last
of the old Kropen or Meningie tribe, who
had the country about the lakes at the
Murray mouth; but a son who is a short-
hand writer —of reputed 120-words power—
carries on the race.
In the business of watching the cash-box
the Federal Parliament shows the States a
bad example. For the current year (ending
on June 30) the Estimates have just been
passed—that is, when the bulk of the money
has been spent and the country irrevocably
committed to spending more on the same
lilies. Beginning with July the Govern-
ment will go ahead getting rid of the loan
and the revenue in the same old way, and
roll up in about nine months for authority
to do what has been already done. Giving
politicians a blank cheque has been tried in
other countries, and Australian experience
is not likely to be any different from
theirs.
Higgs’s announcement that rather than
graft the doctrines of the professed Wealth
Conscriptionists on to Federal policy he
would retire from the Government need not
be taken as an indication of pending change
in the Ministry. He will not be called upon
to make the choice, because the people who
are yelling at him have nothing to offer.
Meanwhile some of the things that J. Bull
is actually doing in the way of “conscript-
ing wealth,” without blowing off hot air
and wrangling about impracticable notions,
are worth looking at. For instance, a citizen
with an income of £6OO not derived from
personal exertion pays £lO6 income tax :
one with £2500 parts with £625. And they
pay swinging local rates and taxes as well,
and are being pleasantly informed that a
larger lump will be cut off later on.
Lancelot Harrison, appointed entomologist
to the British forces in Mesopotamia, was
born 34 years ago at Coonamble (N.S.W.).
where his father, now deceased, was doctor.
Young Harrison captured and classified most
of the beasties in the bush round Parra-
matta (N.S.W.) when he was at King's
School, and the school museum is still proud
of his collection ; but on leaving school he
went to a clerkship in Sydney Mint, where
the gold-bug was the only insect on the pre-
mises. Later, he got a chance to go to Syd-
ney Uni., where he annexed most of the
prizes in his own line, and got his B.Sc.
with honors. A research scholarship from
the Uni. sent him a couple of years ago to
Cambridge to delve more deeply into bio-
logy ; and he was there when the new
appointment was made. Harrison’s wife is
Amy, one of the literary Mack sisters —a bit
of a naturalist, too, and authoress of a dainty
“Bush Calendar.”
“Barmitwah” : Du Maurier knew what he
was talking about when he referred to the
priceless value of a drop of Jewish
blood in Little Billee’s vital fluid.
Isaac Altson, jeweller and lapidary, is
just dead in Melbourne, at the age
of 88. On leaving Iris native Russia lie
founded a big synagogue in Yorkshire, and
after coming to Australia occupied the third
seat on the Jewish Ecclesiastical Board (a
unique distinction for a layman). He had
nine children. Of these one is B. 11. Altson, a
most prosperous tobacconist. Others are
Dave, of Bourke-street, whose saddlery and
horse upholstery are known all over Aus-
tralia ; and B. Altson and Meyer Altson,
scholarship holders of Vic. National Gal-
lery, who, in their London studios, are
painting reputations for themselves in the
temple of art. Versatility’s other name is
Israel!
PA[?]EM ST OGHEIENSES.
(A Lay of Ancient Rome.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the invitation of Mr. ('. E. Liardet, a visit was
paid to the peanut farm at Frankston yesterday.
Harvesting was in full swing. About 1 A ton to the
acre will be secured, and this result is considered
very satisfactory. The plants make excellent hay,
and the nuts are larger and of better quality than
those grown in China. Under some of the plants
100 nuts were counted, but the average per plant is
about 50. —Age, 17/5/’l6.
Bring forth the lyre, Horatius, with all the speed
ye may,
The peanut farm at Frankston’s the subject of our
lay.
Though in Ausonian orchards the apple-crop may rot,
Though grain, for lack of triremes, remain upon
the spot,
Ihe plump, plebeian peanut is safe from trade’s
rebuff,
Because for home, consumption we cannot have
enough.
M hile “Sundry Shows” still flourish, while yet the
“gods” are kind,
And to their staple diet return with even mind,
We shall not need, by export, nor trading oversea,
To spread beyond the Tiber the peanut industry.
Ho, trumpets, sound a war-note! Ho, lictors, clear
the way !
The day we planted peanuts shall be our brightest
day.
We scorn the snarling baker, though bake he soon
or late ;
Our harvest to the acre is thirty hundredweight.
There’s joy wdthin the mansions, and in the shearers’
huts—-
'Neath one small vine we counted full half a hundred
nuts,
Exceeding those of China in every single way—
The peanut-stalks, moreover, make unexampled hay.
The Hun would be exultant had he so fine a feast —-
A noble entertainment alike for man and beast!
No more within the forum is tumult and affright;
Our food-suply is certain from morning unfo night;
For. “Panem et Circenses” no more the Romans
pray—
The slogan that succeeds it is “Peanuts add the
Play !”

THOMAS BABISTON MAUALLAY KETTLEDRUM.
HOW TO TELL IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In stage affairs I’m not u dunce,
I spot the comic man at once!
He wears weird hair upon his head ;
It is a wig, and it is red.
A cue for risibility—
The wig denoting Comedy !
I’d know if I were deaf and dumb
The funny man at last had come ;
You can’t tell always by his face,
But there’s a hall-mark of his race —
A red wig! That infallibly
Shouts: “Here, wake up—l'm Comedy!”
And so l roar the moment it
Appears—what drollery, what wit!
My laughter is infectious quite;
All those around shriek with delight!
For when a rufous wig we see,
We’ve got to laugh—it’s Comedy.
A red nose in the long ago,
Proclaimed the “comic” of the show ;
Red noses now are infra dig,
Which means they’re ousted by the wig.
That’s what unsettles you and mo -
iled hair. He, he! It’s Comedy!

THE INTERNATIONAL LANGE AGE.
BILLJIM (at a loss for the French): “Oh,
well! 'Ere's lookin' at yer!”
“Are you good to your wife?” asked the
satirical prosecuting lawyer of defendant in
a Melbourne matrimonial case the other day.
“I am,” replied the defendant husband. Then
he added unctuously: “The kind man is
kind to his beast.”
Whenever the aching Hun sits down in
the mud that doesn’t belong to him, and
indulges in a few doubts about Gott, reli-
gious comfort of the right sort is
always at hand. The tribal witch-doctors
of Germany haven’t the slightest doubt that
justice is on their side, and, whenever occa-
sion offers, they distribute spiritual conso-
lation. Parson Zoebel, of the Lutheran
Church in Leipsic, recently boomed this :
It is the deep consciousness of our mission that
permits us to congratulate ourselves, and rest con-
tent with a heart full of gratitude, when our guns
beat down the children of Satan, and when our mar-
vellous submarines —instruments to execute the Divine
vengeance—send to the bottom of the sea thousands
of the non-elect. We must fight the wicked with
all the means in our power; their sufferings should
give us pleasure; their cries of despair should not
move German hearts. There ought to be no com-
promise with Hell, no mercy for the servants of
Satan. In other words, no pity for the English,
French and Russians, nor, indeed, for any nation that
has sold itself to the Devil.
Professor Rheinold Seeby is more snuffle-
bustrous. Says he :
We do not hate our enemies. We obey the com-
mand of God, who tells us to love them. But we
believe that in killing them, in putting them to
suffering, in burning their houses, in invading their
territories, we simply perform a work of charity ... .
Germany loves other nations, and when she punishes
them it is for their own good.
What a beautiful Censor he would make!
“Bill B.”: The Gippo makes money in
many ways out of Billjim by the Nile. He
collects old new’S sheets and sells them to
the butcher, while magazines and books in
good order are resold in the streets. Meat
left over in the mess huts is sold- to Arab
small-goods vendors, who do it up with olive
oil and sell it to the natives as rissoles. The
Arab who has been keeping late hours
carries home a parcel of these mince balls
with as much exhilaration as we felt when
we bumped home the midnight cray. Cigar-
ette butts are eagerly sought for and are
sold to some unknown fag manufacturer in
the city. Later we buy them back again in
the shape of fags done up in gaudy packets
labelled, “Australian Glory,” “Anzac.” etc.
They are half-castes (Egyptian and ordi-
nary ’bacca mixed) : for the Gippo is too
tired to even sort his fag-ends after he has
picked them up. Have an “Egyptian”?
From The Bulletin’s horse-doctor :
The Australian horse is superior to the Maori-
land article in Egypt. For one thing he is much
harder. Yet, peculiarly, the Maorilander stands the
sea and its atmospheric changes better, and generally
lands very “clean” and quite free from catarrh. In
most cases the Australian lands “stinking” ; but he
“cleans up” promptly when he is dropped down to
the hardships of the desert and Remount camp,
whereas the Maorilander takes the land conditions
badly and is difficult to treat. All round, the Aus-
tralian can give even the Arab a long start in trials
of hardship, and is far gamer in sickness than any-
thing of his species in this country, which just now
is simply littered with horses from all parts.
“One of the Lambs”: Help, or I’ll be
strangled with red tape! While on week-
end leave in Melbourne from Seymour camp
I desired to have a slight fault of vision
seen to, so went to the base hospital and
asked to see the eye-doctor. So help me
Heaven, I was informed that I would have
to returu to Seymour and get an order from
the regimental doctor. Then I could return
and be attended to. Isn’t it gorgeous!
Made to travel 120 miles and waste three
days—all at the expense' of my bleeding
country—sooner than be permitted to see
the doctor at once. He would probably have
put me through in three minutes !

MR. HUGHES AMONG AUTHORS.
Mr. W. M. Hughes, Prime Minister of Australia,
spent an hour with Miss Marie Corelli, the famous
novelist, at a recent week-end. The interview took

Mr. Rudvard Kipling, poet and author, called on

“Billy T.”: Here in Melbourne they are
again making earnest appeals for funds for
the Lost Dogs’ Home, and piteous little
paragraphs invite us all to consider with
sobs the condition of the derelict dog, the
dog who has seen better days, cast upon the
streets, fatherless, motherless, and deprived
of all the comforts of a home. The high
price of butcher’s meat has resulted in an
extraordinary increase in the number of
waifs and strays, and an excessive strain on
the resources of the Lost Dogs’ Home, which
institution may have to close its doors if the
great heart of the public is not touched.
Laws against enemy trading forbid the
Home selling out to the Germans, and it
seems indecent to waste money on sore-eyed
tykes at a time when money is urgently
needed for 500 better purposes. What I say
is if dogs can’t go to the home, let the home
go to the dogs.
“The Religious Editor”: The stay-at-
home woman who works hard tidying the
house and kids and getting Dad’s dinner
has a pathetic fear of the brassy-haired bar-
maid who works almost as hard at cleaning
the bar and getting Dad’s drinks. If these
two women could only get together once in
a while and talk hats and operations, they
would find that they were sisters under
their clothes and agree that Dad was a hard
man to please—especially when drunk. But,
life being all watertight compartments, they

meanest of men.”
SHE: “Egotist!”
never do get together, and (to the house-
wife especially) distance lends suspicion. It
takes a poor sort of husband to play on
that suspicion ; but the right sort of noble-
man turned up in a Sydney police court
last week. He was charged with assaulting
his missus, who confided to the Bench that
he, had first bragged voluptuously of “drink-
ing with a fine fat barmaid.” In truth she
was probably a thin, dignified woman who
just tolerated his offensive presence for the
sake of the house ; but in the narration of
the loud man of 54, she became a sort of
beery sultana.

“Catkin” from behind a gun :
We have just heard the result of the big Aus-
tralian loan. It’s a beauty. It cheered the chaps
up more than all the letters sent to “our good, brave
boys at the Front” by dear, well-meaning old ladies.
Nothing makes them more uncomfortable than being
slobbered over like that. But they do like, when
they dip their hands in their new socks, to find a
little note saying, “Bill, please drop me a line and
tell me if they fit.” There is nothing Bill likes
quite so much as getting letters from home; the
next-best thing in many cases is for Bill to join up
with home by writing a letter to someone there.
And the note in the sock gives Bill the correspondent
he’s often hungry for. •
* * * *
“Dalriada”: The noblest conception yet
supplied for a statue typifying Australia at
Anzac is given in a story told by Clifford
Browne, pioneer military secretary of the
Y.M.C.A. with the Australian Imperial
Force. On 12 acres of cleared land 12
entrenched Australians, cut off by the
Turks, were gasping for water. One man
volunteered to get it. Returning after a
successful journey, with five filled canteens,
he was shot down. He half sank to his
knees, leaning slightly forward, and his com-
rades imagined him about to spring to his
feet anew. Twenty Turkish rifles rang, but
the kneeling soldier did not move. And
there, as if at orisons, the riddled body
stayed, the canteens at its side, until, after
a week had passed, it bowed and fell. Surely
there will be sculptured marble to make
imperishable that story of a dauntless Aus-
tralian !

“Mimosa”: I thought the rooster-feathers
swank had been done away with by the
practical, get-down-to-it Australian, but
apparently I was w T rong. In the
S’African unpleasantness the emu plumes
gave a smart touch to the Queens-
land mounted infantry, I’ll admit, and
so did the black cock’s decoration to the
N. S. Wales Lancers. But anybody seems
to be able to suit his individual taste here
in Egypt. I saw two infantry officers the
other day wearing the foliage of a crateful
of fowls on their headgear. Loud “coeka-
doodle-doos” greeted them, and one Arab
howled, “Baksheesh ! Give it egg!” ho
runs the millinery branch of the Defence
Department?

A FEW CONFIDENTIAL REMARKS ON THE
RECEIPT OF A LEGAL DOCUMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear one, when our flirtation first began its pleasant
course,
I little thought a breach of promise writ would be
the end ;
I innocently dreamed that you would be the harm-
less source
Of many joyous hours which I might otherwise ex-
pend
Wasting my substance in those haunts where drunk-
ards congregate.
I chose you partly as a shield against so grim a
fate.
Intentions, whether good or ill, I altogether lacked.
My notion was to wander on along love’s rose-strewn
path,
Guarding you with my strong right arm in case you
were attacked
(A very outside chance, this last, I feel compelled
to say).
And my reward ? A passing hug; an intermittent
kiss.
Such was my blameless scheme of life—and it has
come to this!
However, dear, despite my disillusionment and grief,
And all the aching anguish of a lacerated heart,
1 find in one reflection an astonishing relief—
I never wrote a line to you, from the delightful start
Of our great love, to this, its end, so harrowing to
me,
Which all the jurymen on earth would lack my leave
to see.
Nor, though pumped hard and often, did I ever,
sweet, allow
A soul to know the passion which I entertained for
you.
I did not take this cautious course because I feared
a row;
I merely, in my simple way, decided not to do
The sort of needless thing which later on might make
one mad—
A fact for which, as matters stand, I am profoundly
glad.
And so I raise my stricken brow to make this one
remark:
If, when you think of all the food I bought for you
of old,
And all the shows I took you to by daylight and by
dark,
And all the splendid lies I told, you still feel hard
and cold—
Well, sweetheart, fire ahead! Cut loose! Make all
the fuss you will.
Your chances, as I work them out, are absolutely nil.
N.S.W.

“Billy B.“: The rabbit-trappers are
amongst the few whose Union delegates
have not yet been invited by Lady Strick-
land to afternoon tea at Government House,
but they’re on the list. And they’ll do the
mob proud. Along the South Coast of
N S. Wales some of the boys are making
£8 and £9 a week, and any trapper who
doesn’t hold out his hook for at least £5 is
looked upon as only fit to associate with the
working classes.
Bill was full up, and when she gushed,
‘‘And were you in any bayonet charges?”
lie grunted, “Two.” “How lovely!” she
fluttered ; “Oh, tell me about them.” “Well,”
says William, “one was to get me on the
boat and the other was to get me off.”
The gift of tongues is not always
profitable. A Prahran (Vic.) landlady
refused to tell Arthur Sostley, a carter,
whether one of her boarders was at home.
“Are you deaf and dumb, or a Venus, or
a n oil painting?” was his comprehensive
sally. This pearl of eloquence was quoted
in evidence against him on a charge of
trespass and insulting words; and it cost
him £7, with £3 Is. costs.

“Mere Pounamu” : I inherited certain old
scrapbooks full of newspaper cuttings care-
fully collated by an English grandfather
who was in his prime in the ’thirties. They
had, I suppose, a certain antiquarian value,
and’ were full of repetitions of the aged and
comfortable boast that one Englishman was
worth three frog-eating Frenchmen. This
was before the flames of Verdun had lighted
up for us the French warrior of to-day.
But observe how the old brag, now adapted
by the Hun, still persists. In New York is
published for 5 cents The Fatherland,
whose object is announced as “Fair play
for Germany and Austria-Hungary.” It
sports the Stars and Stripes on its title
page, is the organ of the hyphenated
Americo-German, and is edited by George
Sylvester Viereck, whose poetry, before the
present war made him foam at the mouth,
was favorably noticed by the Red Page. In
its issue of 16/2/’l6, The Fatherland
extols that smart feat of German seaman-
ship, the capture of the Appam, in these
words: “This story, that shall live so long
as the salt spray dashes into the nostrils of
seamen, and while the ocean runs its course,
was not received with loud acclaim. The
world had awaited it. These men were
Germans Forty-two Germans against
375 Britons. It is the average odds. One
German is the peer of ten Englishmen.
There is not boast in this. Let us face the
fact.” Grandpa is dead these 20 years, and
cannot retort. But three of his grandsons
are captains of Anzacs in the French firing-
line. His reply is safe with them. Two of
them proved it in Gallipoli.

tralia there is a lighthouse known as North-
West Cape which is 120
miles from the nearest
inland telegraph station
A few months ago a
keeper named Butler and
his family developed
ptomaine poisoning, the
worst sufferer being an
18-months-old child. Af+er
exhausting their own re-
sources the parents de-
cided to take the mite to
the nearest doctor, 250
miles away, at Carnar-

they reached Maud’s
Landing, a distance of
104 miles, in four days.
From there Butler re-
turned to his station, and
his wife and child left
for Carnarvon (150
miles) by motor. But
the car broke down when
they had gone only a
quarter of the distance.
After the usual delay,
they struck inland and
borrowed another car
from the owner of Boola-
thanna station. In this,
two days later, they
arrived at Carnarvon.
The doctor there recom-
mended taking the child
to Perth, and after a
wait of five days Mrs.
Butler got a passage.
Twelve days after the
start of her journey she
carried her baby into
hospital. With mothers
like that you wouldn’t
need to wonder if + he

Recently in Egypt the Maorilanders made
a list of the names of the men in their force
who were in the famous landing on April
25, 1915, and who remained on the penin-
sula continuously until the evacuation. There
were only 16 of them.
“Hamer”: Something like a Wagnerian
selection gone mad may be heard amongst
the musicians very soon. Of recent years
their Union has grown to be a very power-
ful body—so powerful that it has been able
to (1) secure agreements from very unwill-
ing employers as to working conditions, (2)
force practically all professional musicians
into its ranks, and (3) demand an entrance
fee of £5 or so from new comers. Now
breakers are sighted ahead. A prominent
official and some others have broken away
and formed an Association alleging an
objection to the highhandedness of the
Union. The Union countercharges them with
having been instigated by some of the em-
ployers, However, the bright new Ass. is
asking the Federal Arbitration Court to
cancel the mother Union’s registration on the
ground that its rules are ultra vires. The
application will be heard when the Mer-
chant Service Guild’s case is finished. The
Union, which was perfectly calm about the
loss of registration under the N.S.W. State
Industrial Act, will kick hard against the
loss of Federal registration, because its
awards have to be registered in the Federal
Court; and if the hostile Ass. pushes it
out, the full power of the united fiddlers
will be used to make a discord. In the
meantime, in order to give its members
something extra to fight for, a new “log” has
been fired at employers, the demands being
a considerable advance on existing rates.
Paying the piper is going to be a serious
problem presently.
* * * *
At the annual meeting of the Tasmanian
Synod Tryon Wilson fluttered the clerical
birds by denouncing the way they were
lavishing money on people already well-paid,
while squeezing, little by little, the clergy
who were doing the work of the diocese in
the country. If he had a million, he said, he
wouldn’t give a penny of it to the Diocesan
Council or the Church Trustees, but would
endow a number of poor country parishes
and tie the money up good and hard so that
the central authorities couldn’t burgle it.
While clergymen in the country were starv-
ing on less than £3 per week, out of which
they had to keep horses, the diocese spent
£2360 a year in “administration,” and only
£2OOO was left to help the poorer parsons.
No one contradicted all this; but the money
was allotted in the same old discreditable
way.
“D.F.” : Talk about the city taking over
the dirty and disgraceful old graveyard of
St. David’s, in the midst 'of Hobart, and
turning it into a park, has always called
forth protests from the Anglican Church
authorities who control it; and a lot has
been said about sacrilege ajid disrespect to
the departed dead. But the real nigger in
the wood-pile showed his nose at Synod the
other day, when it was stated that the
Church must keep hold of this ground be-
cause in years to come it would be worth
£30,000 or £40,000 for building allotments.
Even as it is the Church lets part of the
ground for an oil-store and timber-yard,
and there was a strong kick against a pro-
posal that the rent should all be used to
put the dangerous ground in some sort of
decent order.
The old, two-storey Black Bull at Bathurst
(N.S.W.) has gone the way of all worn-out
pubs. Built in 1837, it was for a couple of
decades the swell kiosk of “the West,’ and
for some years the home port of the Sydney-
Bathurst coach. The Bull had an inevitable
horn in the bushranging pie, and the yarn
goes that Ben Hall used one of its cellars
as a dug-out for three days, while the police
scouted for him over the neighboring land-
scape. The early licensee was the late
sport Johnny Tate, whose horses won a good
share of what lay on the right side of the
winning-post those times.
The Crown Prince is reported to have
said: “At Verdun I will build an imperish-
able monument.” He is still building it, and
so far there are only about a hundred thou-
sand dead Germans underneath.
“Bill Bowyang” : I want to put in a word
to the credit of the son of Abraham. At
Cape Ilelles the Zion Mule Corps, recruited
largely from Palestine, did most excellent
transport service. Strange it seemed when
one asked a war-cobber where he came from,
to hear him claim the Holy City as his native
place. With these Jerusalem chaps Arabic
was the easiest language for conversation.
They were very earnest in their support of
the great enterprise that brought them to
Gallipoli. Also we had Australian and
Maoriland Jews in various battalions who
would yield to none of us in the depth and
energy of their patriotism. I helped to rescue
a Sydney Jew who had a bullet wound in his
chest. He fought a most plucky battle for
his life, and finally was moved to the hospi-
tal ship, suffering gladly for the only home
and fatherland he knew.

THE OLD CANDLE ROW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On Anzac Day he took part in a united service in
his parish. The minister was having pictures ex-
hibited in his church, and he was striking trouble
with some of his parishioners over candles.
—Scotchbyterian parson.
The war has come with fire and steel to make the
world anew,
And endless fields are scarred with graves and wet
with bloody dew ;
Prone fighting-men cry out to God, their faces to the
sky, .
But Echo only answers them and so they faint and
die.
I buzz at home while these souls pass, or have a
cassock-fight
With holy fools who see all Hell in candles that I
light.
A shred of rag will serve me well to stir my sheepish
flock ;
A pictured ghost upon a screen will give them quite
a shock.
For oh ! they live by rote and book, and what their
fathers feared
They also fear, with threat and sneer, each in his
goatish beard.
The living world beyond our hole may shake to
devils’ tread ;
We crouch inside and mope and moan with old things
damned and dead.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Hats off to Mrs. Teresa Baxter, of West
Brunswick (Vic.), mother of 20 (18 living),
of whom five are in the trenches. And as
that lot didn’t exhaust her mother-love she
reared eight of her sister-in-law’s youngsters.
EDWIN STOOKE, who represents "The
Trade” in the N.S.W. early-closing light.
Three of the six Australian State Pre-
miers are natives of South Aus., the State
in which they have just been cackling—
Crawford Vaughan, Jack Scaddan and
Edward Theodore (Acting Premier of Q.).
Theodore, in his 32nd year, is the youngest
of the bunch.
The death of St-. Clere Evelyn Liardet, at
Auckland (M.L,)* aged 82, erases the last
of that historic family-. He was the youngest
eon of the late Captain W. F, Evelyn
Liardet, the founder of Port Melbourne* St.
Clere was five years old when lie .arrived
at the Port (then So nil ridge) with his
parents in the ship William Metcalfe in
1839, The Captain dumped his goods on
the foreshore, which for long* years was
known as “Liardet’s Beach,” The Pier
Hotel was built by him in 1843, on a site
now occupied by the tramway engine-house.
He had the first mail contract and drove all
mail matter to and from early Melbourne.
And he also ran the first steamer to
Williamstown. Altogether a most enterpris-
ing man.
This is one story of the late John
Murray’s reformation. It began in a
city bar one night, long after trading
hours. There were
four men in the
dim religious light,
and Jack had been
very jovial. Sud-
denly liis eye fell
upon his own re-
flection in a strip
of mirro r. He
stared at it like a
man who sees his
own ghost. Then he
said: “My God,
boys, I have been
telling myself for
the last hour that
at any rate I
didn’t look as bad
as you do. I’m
never going to
look like that
again.” John
walked out. and
never went back.
Next day the pub-
lican had that
long mirror taken
out of his bar.
MARION,
N. S. Wales’s

Six O’Clocker.
Oodnadatta is not an ideal place for a
honeymoon, but that is where Johii J. Wal-
dron, of Parkville (Vic.), has taken his
bride. He was married on May 10 to Mi*s
Florence Webb, a North Melbourne girl, and
soon afterwards set out on his fourth visit
to Centralia, where his job is to make sur-
veys for the External Affairs Department.
Waldron, by the way, speaks highly of the
camels which have been bred in Centralia,
and says that with their help the Common-
wealth could form a camel corps that would
astonish even Ahmed.
•Timmy Carroll, one of Maoriland’s poli-
tical delegates to England, has never before
travelled further than Sydney—lie languidly
drifted over to Australia when the Common-
wealth was christened. He has sat con-
tinuously in the M.L. House of Reps since
’B7 and has the record of having been longer
a Minister in point of unbroken service than
any other man there. Jimmy held a job as
Minister from ’92, when Ballance took him
into the Executive, until Ward went to
pieces in 1912. As a boy he fought through
the Urewera campaign and earned a medal
and a grant of cash and special men-
tion in despatches for his services with Ham-
lin’s troop against Te Kooti. He is a boy
of 60 now—a good sportsman, a very fine
speaker,, stout and lazy. His father was an
old-time whaler; his mother one of the
Ngatikahunguru.
The two oldest Anglican gaiters in Aus-
tralia. and Maoriland respectively kept their
birthdays this month. On May 17 Bishop
Langley, of Bendigo (Vic.), finished the
eightieth lap; and Bishop Nevill, of Dune-
din (M.L.), entered it four days earlier.
Nevill was bishing before Langley was or-
dained, having tried on his mitre for the
first time in 1871. in which year he was im-
ported to Dunedin from the Cold Country.
He is easily senior to any other Austral-
asian bishop, either Anglican or R.C., and
lias been Primate of Maoriland since 1904.
Langley is a comparative novice in the
bishop class, for lie lias worn the gaiters
only nine years, though he had an arch-
deacon’s spats for 12 years earlier. He was
born in Ireland, blit came to Australia as a
youth, and was a bank manager at New-
castle (N.S.W.) when the Church called to
him in the early seventies of last century.
For 25 years he looked after the collectioii-
plate at the Australian headquarters of
“Low Church” Anglicanism, Sydney’s old
St. Philip’s—which ought to be spelt “St.
Phillip’s,” for it was called after the first
Governor of the Ma State, not after the
Saint.
Fifty-six years at sea and still going
strong is the record of Captain Crane, of
the sailer William Mitchell, which blew into
Hobart from Maryport (Eng.) the other
day with 8000 tons of railway iron. Born
at Singapore he went to sea at 15* and has
been there ever since. He once commanded
the tea clipper Yangtze, which hurried
from London to Sydney in 71 days and
made some other line runs in the tea race
from China ports to London. Crane has
never been wrecked yet; but the very trip
after he left the old Yangtze she was piled
up on a rock In the China Sea.
Adelaide’s real, indisputable centenarian
has given up the tight against Time at 108.
Surgeon-Major Hinton was horn two years
before Waterloo, and was a qualified Lon-
don doctor before South Aus. was founded.
Joining the Bengal army, he was in all the
big Asiatic fights of a period ivhen Europe
was fairly peaceful—Maharajapore against
the Mahrattas, Aliwal, Sobraon and Mool-
tan against the Sikhs, many Mutiny battles,
and the Taku forts in China. When he re-
tired he brought a lot of experience and a
batch of medals to Australia. After giving
Tasmania a trial he settled near Adelaide
in 1870, and has put in nearly half a cen-
tury of peaceful enjoyment, always in splen-
did health till he broke his leg at 98. He
never smoked, took alcohol only as medi-
cine, could read without glasses till almost
the end, and set a good example by insisting
on being sent to Adelaide’s too-little-used
cremation house,

“Jacky” Evans, one-time master mariner
and Premier of the Speck till _“recohstruc-
tion” squeezed him out, has been appointed
Speaker of the Tasmanian Assembly, in suc-
cession to the scholarly “John Drayman”
Woods, who is on the wrong side of the
party fence. “Jacky” had the- job for a
while some years ago, but dropned it in a
vain attempt to help the Lib. party out of
a hole and dish the Labs. As he is now
Master Warden of the Hobart Marine Board
and manager for Huddart, Parker, as well
as Speaker, he is on a pretty good wicket.

His business was started in Swanston-street.

shipmate Henry Parley, the butcher who
afterwards made his name as a missioner of
the grim dry-tack school.

HECTOR
GOLDSPINR,
Melbonrne Tenor.
There is one part of the world that has
sent its last man off to the war, anyhow,
and is only restrained from despatching the
last shilling because it hasn’t got one.
Campbell Island, 200 miles south of the
Bluff, a resort of old-time whalers, had a
population consisting of seven brothers
named Norton, and when a ketch put in
there the other day the whole community
climbed aboard and sailed off to Maoriland,
where it immediately flocked to the colors.
Retired-squatter David Stodart, formerly
of Mooramong, Skipton (Vic.), was rounded
up the other day by the Boss Musterer.
Besides engaging in the sheep-business in
the Western District, the old man had also
tried the Upper Goulburn beef-country, and
his experiences enabled him to spend his
declining years in the odor of social sanctity.
Stodart married a daughter of the late
Charles Willis, also a Western District
grass-expert, and there is a fine bunch of
Stodart sons at the Front.
There is hope for us yet. When the Inter-
State Trade Union Congress met at Hobart
the other day it appointed as its secretary
J. B. Griggs, who in his spare time is secre-
tary to the local Trades and Labor Council,
but makes the laying of bricks the serious
business of life. An hour or two later he
told the Congress that he had just got word
that a job at his trade, which has been slack
of late, was waiting for him. and he reckoned
that that was more important even than sit-
ting as secretary in “the Federal Parliament
of Labor.”
The wanderings of an Australian family:
Some years ago there was at David Jones’s m
Sydney a stylish Irish shop-walker known to most
as Jack Robinson. He had a.Scotch wife and two
pretty daughters, Madge and Nancy. Jack had pre-
viously worked in China, and both his children were
born there, but were sent south to he reared and
educated in Sydney. After Jack Robinson’s death,
Hans Meincke, who was on the staff of the German
Consulate at Sydney, married Madge Robinson. Soon
afterwards Meincke was recalled to Europe and
attached to the German Embassy in London; and
sister Nance and Mrs. Robinson, the mother, went
to London, and lived with the family at Brixton,
where Mrs. Robinson died. After this Meincke was
transferred to the German Embassy at St. Peters-
burg ; but with his eldest son he was in Berlin, con-
sulting with the Foreign Office, when the big trouble
started. Nance Robinson was then in Austria, using
certain medicinal waters for her health. Madge
Robinson, of the enemy Embassy, got two hours to
leave the Russian capital. Nance, who was a Biit-
isher and an enemy subject, was ordered to leaw
Austria within two hours. She got to England and
sheltered with some relations. Then, growing anxious
about her sister Madge, she became .- a naturalised
German subject, and went to Berlin, where she is
now living with the Meinckes. And in between rear-
ing about glorious victories, the Sisters wonder what
they really are. Born in China of an Irish fathei
and a Scotch mother, reared and educated in Sydney
one driven out of Russia because that country was a.
war with Austria and the other driven out of Austria
because that country was at war with England, an
now both sheltering in Germany —it is a tane *
indeed.
WHEN WE GO OUT TO WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Me most all be prepared to go, irrespective of
age and station. —Melbourne paper.
I’m waiting now to go to war,
For sure the need is great;
We all know what we’re fighting for,
And now we shan’t have long to wait.
It’s better late than not at all (this has been said
before),
Though some of us are fifty-eight, and some of us
are more.
George Houston Reid, a general he,
John Forrest bv his side,
Will lead us on to victory,
And somehow soon we’ll turn the tide.
There’s King O’Malley girds his loins like fierce
Jehoshaphat,
While bold Bruce Smith sits stiff astride his charger
—think of that!
Sir Ronald Mungo—hang it all,
The name escapes me quite!—
Puts on his spurs nor fears to fall:
For war he has an appetite.
The fatted editor puts down his shears, no whit
upset
To know that he must leave his desk and seize his
bayonet.
Ten grey reporters, very tough,
Prim judges twenty-three,
With blown-out ladies' men (hot stuff!)
Will go to war along o’ me.
George Blatk will join the cavalry, and gay his
plumes will toss,
Although his horsemanship may prove distressing to
the hoss.
I’m glad that we shall go to war
To fight for King and State,
For Empire and tor Faith, and for
Matilda Jane and Tot and Kate.
Me're sixty-five to eighty-six, but loud our martial
song
Shrills out to prink the doodledoos, and now we
shan’t he long!
x.s.w.

Owing to the War.
With regret we are compelled to announce that
all lines of “Austral"’ Crossbred Wool Rugs, includ-
ing our famous “BOSKER” RUG, have sold out,
and will be unprocurable for some time to come.
On the outbreak of war the Military Authorities
not only requisitioned all reserve stocks, but, in
addition, secured the whole of the output of Vicars’
Mills, and until their requirements are satisfied
nothing may be manufactured for Civilian use.

Westralia has been having a bad time; and,
though things have improved, they are still
only middling. The Northern cattle-raising
business has been wilting under a long
drought; and the industry will take a few
years to recover. The wheat harvest, though
showing a record of 16 million bushels, wms
much under early estimates; and it looks
as if Westralia has still a lot to learn about
wheat growing and where not to try to do
it. Incidentally, the experience will pro-
bably cost a good deal of money, and mort-
gagees will have to do some of the paying.
The gold industry will not flourish for ever.
The cream of the Ivalgoorlie field has been
already skimmed; and Westonias and the
like are a poor substitute. Altogether there
have been and still are fine chances of mak-
ing pretty big banking losses in Westralia ;
and no doubt the need to write down
doubtful assets has a steadying effect upon
the profits of this bank.
As disclosed, the profits are by no means
as dazzling as the dividend. The share
capital is £250,000 and the visible reserves
£707,840, together £957.840; and 6 p.c. on
that lot amounts to £57,470, whereas the
total declared profit for the past 12 months
was only £55,962. Further, the bank has
over 2\ millions of advances. That is to
say, it has invested over million of other
people’s money and guaranteed the principal
and interest. Considering the inevitable
risk there is in that, shareholders can’t say
that the bank, in earning less than 6 p.c.
for their money, is doing anything to call
for three cheers. But if there really isn’t
much in banking profits for shareholders
(and there are plenty worse figures than
those of this bank) there is a good deal in
it for the scattered public. This concern,
- for instance, carries on its operations in no
less than 60 branches, all in Westralia,
whose population isn’t much over 300,000.
And it can’t do that without heavy expense.
There have been no movements of much
consequence during the year:—

approximately to £2,600,000 wherewith to
make good that balance.

“Although,” the directors say, “the depres-
sion in business resulting from the war has
extended over the full year, it is satisfactory
to note that the net profit is sufficient to
pay the usual 12i p.c.” But has there been
any “depression in business resulting from
the war”? So far as Australian business is
concerned the war has meant the boom
conditions that spring naturally from the
lavish expenditure of many millions of bor-
rowed money. Not a factory or mill that
was previously in operation has been closed ;
on the contrary, new ones have been estab-
lished, and old ones have added new lines.
And if some of the old brands of goods have
been off the markets, new ones have been
forthcoming, and they have needed publicity,
of which this co. has got the benefit. The
investor, then, who believes that the war
has produced a depression that will pass
away when peace comes is likely to fall
into serious trouble. Peace may possibly be
immediately followed by something in the
nature of a frenzy; but after that will come
the real depression, when borrowed money
will be no longer lavishly spent; when, in-
stead, we shall consider the bill and be
brought face to face with the painful pro
cess of paying up. Certainly, business con-
cerns such as this will have some set-off
against the diminished revenue of the days
to come. They have been hit during the
past year by war prices for materials and
war freights, which in some cases they have
not been able to pass on. But the extra cost
last year was probably more than counter-
balanced by the extra revenue resulting from
the boom conditions beforementioned.
In spite of the heavy borrowing in which
both Commonwealth and State have been
engaged, this co.’s profits have shrunk
steadily since 1012. There are two visible
causes: (1) increased cost of production,
the result of wages boards’ awards, etc.;
and (2) the fact that throughout this period
a large and increasing sum of money for-
merly invested at interest has been put into
a building which has only just been com-
pleted. The new building account as at
December 31 stood at £103,383, and averaged
for the whole year about £75,000 ; and that
meant a loss of £3OOO in interest by com-
parison with, say, 1912 and 1913. The
£103,383, by the way, is exclusive of the
freehold, £28,019. The old leasehold pre-
mises stood in the books at £30,000. This
has been provided for by a sinking fund,
which has involved an annual charge of
about £1000; and the co. will now be free
from that.
Many investors unquestionably made a
great mistake about this stock, which they
valued on the basis not of the dividend but
of the dividend plus the market value of
the bonus shares which were then being
regularly distributed ; and the market value
of those bonus shares was based chiefly on
the continuance of the distribution. It was
overlooked that, owing to the issue of the
bonus shares, the dividend alone now con-
sumes more than twice as much profit as it
did 10 years ago. That there will be dis-
tributions in the future is very probable;
but they will be irregular. The present paid
capital is £266,670; and if we assume a
divisible profit of £40,000, the co. could pay
the present 12J p.c. on an additional capital
of £53,330 —equal to one share for every
present five. That is the utmost limit until
the profits which can safely be divided
exceed £40,000.

This is an offshoot of Elder, Smith and Co.,
Ltd., S. Australia’s old-established pastoral
finance and agency co.; and it has the
biggest pull of any concern in the Central
State. Moreover, it dates from so far back
that many of those interested in it are old
men ; and that’s no disadvantage to a trustee
co. formed under its wing. Partly no doubt
for that reason, partly on account of the
transferred executor business, the co. is
already controlling estates valued at
£1,731,954; and a 6 p.c. dividend is very
comfortably paid. Given the expert manage-
ment which the directors were wise to secure,
the co. takes very small risks. Its capital
and reserves are practically all in gilt-edged
securities, and in a couple of years they will
be bringing in enough to pay a 6 p.c. divi-
dend. The profits of the business will be a
sort of bonus on top of that. And the trustee
co. performs such excellent services to the
community that there is first-class reason to
believe that the whole tribe will go on stead-
ily expanding.

This was the outside showmen’s reply to the
big combine; and the latter, knowing that
hard things were being threatened just then
against rings and combines, was probably
very glad to see a decent co-operative com-
petitor in the field. The co. started off with
a solid connection ; amongst others, the late
A. F. Russell, the born showman who
founded Hoyt’s, was a director. In the first
year the gross profit on hirings and sales ran
to £5533; in the next year to £11,448 ; in the
past year to £13,867. Exes, have, of course,
moved up, too; but there was enough left
over last year to show nearly 25 p c. on the
capital, which now stands at £11,645. At the
outset £5439 of the capital—then barely
£lO,OO0 —was represented by goodwill and
other intangible assets; but since that date
practically an equal amount of profit has
been accumulated, so that, without going
seriously into debt, the co. is able to carry
double the old stock. Total liabilities are
£3854; on the other side there is stock (at
valuation) £7330, advances (to secure films!
£4483, book debts £3839, and a few sun-
dries. Bad debts in this business, by the
way. are heavy ; this year, after writing-off
what are reckoned bad or doubtful (amount
unspecified), a further £5OO is put by to
provide for contingencies.

This co. has its troubles too: the Govern-
ment has prohibited the export of coal to
places beyond the Commonwealth ; and this
and the difficulty of getting freight has cut
into the output and the profits. But a coal-
mining co. has this consolation, at any rate:
what it doesn’t hew out to-day will be wait-
ing for it to-morrow. In that respect a mine
isn’t like a farm or a block of shops. The
life of these is not made longer by idleness.
The life of a mine is. The co. hasn’t any-
thing to complain of, anyway. Ten years
ago it had worked out its old mine and
had just secured land at Cessnock, where
it hoped to make a new mine. Since then,
though it has got in less than £6OOO of new
capital, it has not only opened up the new
mine—the Neath—but has worked the fol-
lowing changes in its finances :

Once bit, twice shy. The co. practically
did nothing towards writing down the book
value of the old mine while - the coal was
being worked out. In other words, though the
co.’s capital was being exhausted, it wasn’t
being repaid and written down. Now, how-
ever, as the mine is drawn on, money is
being accumulated either to buy "and
develop a new mine or to pay back the
capital if it is thought desirable to go out
of business. The capital, by the way, is
£20,000 in 12| p.c. pref. shares and £90,000
in ordinaries, all fully paid.

Some of the figures in the last line are esti-
mates ; and there isn’t much on which to base
the guess at the bonus to policy-holders. In
the Accident department policy-holders who
are shareholders get back 40 p.c. of their
premiums, and policy-holders who are not
shareholders get back 30 p.c.; but nowhere
are we told what proportion of the total each
represents. By the way, these rebates have
to be remembered when you are told on
behalf of the new Government office that the
public has been so badly fleeced that the
State can do the job for about half the
money. As a ~matter of fact, it was much-
better to put the premiums fairly high and
make a refund of the part that was not
needed. There isn’t much experience to go on
in connection with this accident insurance ;
there was so little, in fact, a few years ago
that the English concern with which the
manager of the Queensland State office was
then connected lost £47,000 in one year at
this business —not this manager’s fault, of
course, for he was in Australia and the in-
sufficient premiums were charged in Eng-
land.
This little co., established primarily to do
the accident (employers’ liability) business
of its shareholders, now finds its chief occu-
pation gone—or, at any late, very seriously
threatened. The State office claims to be
the only concern authorised to do this spec a 1
brand of insurance, though the claim is, of
course, contested. Still “notwithstanding the
loss of’ the Accident department, the directors
are of opinion that the fire, marine and nlate-
glass business can be safely carried onA
These are, however, only small lines thus far:
last year the Accident premiums were £2256
and the total of all the others (excluding re
insurance) was only £662. If the law finallv
decides that the State office lias been given
a monopoly there thus won’t be much left
for this little co. to do. Nevertheless, if the
worst comes to the worst, it won’t have to
hold a sacrifice sale in order to pay its credi-
tors. Including provision for current risks
it owes only £1044, and in cash and good
saleable stocks (such as Union Banks and
Commonwealth Loans) it has £9llO, less the
current dividend. The actual paid capital is
only £1512 ; so for each £ paid up the share-
holders can see at least £4 10s.

Poor old Block 10 has lately been so much
in the habit of winding up on the wrong
side that, though, taking no account of depre-
ciation, it made a’ profit of £155 last half-
year, the directors’ report says that there
was a net loss of that amount. The sohi
written off for depreciation is £4262; so the
final result is the loss of £4107 shown above.
Block 10 is next-door to the B. H. p r0 p„
of which it was formerly part; and it was
in the B. 11. Prop, that the Broken Hill
lode, which is in the form of an inverted A',
came to the surface. Therefore, both there
and in Block 10 the owners got at once on
to rich ore ; so also they passed through it
first. On the other hand, the more remote
mines, like the South and the North, were
for years struggling propositions, whereas
now they are in the midst of affluence.
Being very near the end of its ore re-
sources Block 10 was not invited into Asso-
ciated Smelters, Ltd., the more or less co-
operative concern which recently took over
the B. 11. Prop.’s smelting works. Hence
it wasn’t able to get concentrates treated
when the war closed the European markets;
but as the smelter capacity Las now been
enlarged a conti act has been made with the
Associated Smelters, ancl both the mine and
the mill have been restarted. It is impos-
sible to say how long they can keep going—
it is wonderful how yon can potter
about in these big mines picking up scraps
of ore here and there. Twelve months ago
the reserves were said to be 229,000 tons, and
that was where they stood 12 months pre-
viously, though 38,000 tons had been beaten
out in the interval. But there isn’t much
profit in rummaging for sulphides in a
worked-out mine under ordinary market
conditions ; so Block 10 has been exploring
a property at Misima (New Guinea). Gen-
eral-manager O. B. Ward says the results
have been satisfactory:—
The shaft is now down 141 ft., and a plat is being
cut for No. 2 level. The exercising of the option
is dependent on results obtained at this level. Should
values and widths be equal to those obtained in
levels and tunnels- above, the property will be an
attractive one.
Meantime the money spent on the properties
under option is being treated as an asset:
six months ago it was £4853, and now it is
£974(3. The co. has still £52,443 of liquid
assets in excess of liabilities.

This co., which has fallen farther short of its
own promises than any other concern at
Broken Hill, has had a turn of luck. Its l
mine being almost exhausted, it decided
to become a treatment co., and bought a large
quantity of slimes, hoping to reduce them
by the new “selective” flotation method an
extension of the oil and acid process in
common use. Then the war intervened, and
nobody wanted that grade of concentrates
which can be produced from refractory
slimes. 8o things went on until a few
weeks ago, when the output of zinc concen-
trates until April 30, 1017. was sold to
Japan. There was also a stack of lead con-
centrates- —about 1300 tons —and these the
Associated Smelters have taken off Junction
North’s hands. The arrangement (if any
for the future is obscure: the directors sa' •
“We are arranging for the treatment oi
sale of our lead concentrate from slime,
while general-manager Palmer says in an un-
dated report: “Associated Smelters will----
take a portion of our future output as soon
as we are able to commence deliveries. Ine
quantity to be taken will gradually
as smelting capacity is added to. , u '"
the Junction North ought to be able <>
make a profit out of metals at presen
prices. What will happen when the bottom
falls out of the markets after the war this
paper doesn’t know. But it seems quite
safe, on the experiences of past -J
years, to say that the Junction North wui
be making a loss while two-thirds of the
others are still able to show a profit. I* ie

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.

Vislok is a single, self-contained lock nut, consist-
ing of two sections hydraulically forced together.
Vislok has a solid, continuous thread throughout, and
when locked gives a regular and positive hold on the
thread of the bolt. Vislok is so constructed that the
energy of the working load and vibratory force
exerted upon it tend to increase the locking action.
Vislok can be used as an ordinary nut, and is easily
applied in confined spaces. Vislok will not injure
the bolt-thread, and is quickly locked or unlocked
with an ordinary spanner. At the present time
Vislok is proving its usefulness on the Government.
Railways and at the Cockatoo and the Naval Dock.
Yards/ Agents: B. J. Ball, Limited, Sydney and:
Brisbane; Elder, Smith and Co., Melbourne and!
Adelaide; Elder, Shenton and Co., Fremantle and!
Perth; Colin J. Shields, Launceston. — Advt.
