Agents and Correspondents throughout the World,
financial position continues weak. There is a
snrnlus of £97,188 of “floating assets”
Sets £136,858; liabilities £39,670); but
£O6 519 represents concentrates on the
floors £86,771 slimes purchased hut not yet
treated, and £10,051 stores.
,\.t the meeting the chairman made
matters with regard to the smelting contract
‘little clearer. The co. has arranged “for
the disposal of 250 tons per week [of lead
concentrates 1 over a period of .»(> years,
commencing almost at once, and gradually
increasing to the full production of 750 tons
oer week.” Now all that the co. has to do
is to find the ore to make the concentrates.
Counting in all there is in the mine and
the slimes in the dumps, it can’t see ahead
beyond seven years.
B. H. Block 14 is a wonder. Ten years
ago it was all hut worked out: there was
just a bit of picking to he done hero and
there. It has been picking ever since, and
last half-year made a profit of £10,874. The
fact is, iii the early days they were in such
a tremendous hurry that they couldn’t be
bothered with trifles; they wanted to get
down. And when they got down they wished
they hadn’t, because the sulphides soon cut
out. The pot has been kept boiling since
by rooting out carbonates —rich, easily-
smelted ore for which there is alv-eys a
demand. What sulphides there are in the
mine have been left practically untouched,
because it didn’t look as though there was
enough to justify the erection of an expen-
sive mill. But “in view,” the directors
say, “of the satisfactory prices of metals
the'question of resuming the mining of sul-
phide ore is having close attention, and it
is hoped that an arrangement may be pos-
sible for the concentration of the ore by
some other co. on joint account.” The
Junction North is handy. The co. paid
£l5OO to prefs. and £5OOO to prefs. and ordi-
naries during the half-year, and still has a
nice little £02,997 in excess of liabilities.
The assets are very liquid ; and the liabili-
ties are only a few hundreds. The co. for
some years chased around for a new mine,
but after a disheartening experience at
Torrington (N.S.W.) seems to have given
up the hunt.
Following the lead of the old Continental
Spelter Combine the Commonwealth Govern-
ment has successfully promoted the Zinc Pro-
ducers’ Association to handle practically the
entire output of Australian zinc concen-
trates. The present idea is to smelt 40 p.o.
in Australia and 60 p.c. in England, with a
proviso that the output in excess of the
capacity of the Australian and English
works shall go to Belgian and French
smelters. But that won’t solve the pro-
blem. Costs will do that. If we go into the
world’s market with our metal we must, in
order to get the trade, be able to put the
stuff there cheaper than our competitors.
If on the other hand, we aim only at
supplying our own and the Allies’ markets,
then there must be a Customs duty to keep
the foreign metal out, or an equivalent
bounty.
The C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), is raising
a few tons of 40 p.c. to 50 p.c. lead ore
from the 457 ft. level and a winze. It is also
raising fairly large quantities of first-grade
and second-grade copper ore from a zone of
secondary enrichment above the 500 ft. level.
During the fortnight the mine produced 70
tons of first-grade and 264 tons of second-
grade, and is shipping rather larger quanti-
ties than usual to smelters. Reserves have
been recently calculated at 150,000 tons of
7 p.c. ore, and compared with that the pre-
sent output is trifling.
Plucky, unlucky little Mt. David, the
most persistent gold-miner in N. S. Wales,
is at last knocked down on its knees, and is
begging the Warden to give it nine seconds’'
rest from labor conditions.
* * * *
Edna May Deeps is down 425 ft., and sink-
ing is slow—the management feeling very
carefully for the heavy burst of water which
will arrive sooner or later. A plat has been
excavated at 405 ft. from the surface, and the
management announces that a crosscut has
been extended from it for a distance of sft.
If it is intended to crosscut for the reef at
this depth the co. is entering upon a, most
interesting programme. From the history of
the bores it is apparent that there is very
slight hope of the reef being located in the
Deeps at so shallow a depth as 405 ft. Then
there is the water problem to be considered ;
and in view of Edna May’s experience it
seems certain that Deeps will be flooded out
before a ton of ore is made available for
treatment There is also the theory that it
was the Edna May hanging wall reef, or
some even less important ore body, that
the Deeps bore disclosed. The 10-head mill
is approaching completion, and no doubt the
plant is intended to deal with ore reserves
which may be discernible by the management,
but are certainly not visible to the eye of
others. Anyone who' considers the present
outlook for Deeps a pleasing one is an
optimist in a class all by himself.
A bomb has landed in the midst of the
tidna May Central management, which has
lately been all smiles because its affluent
neighbor, the Edna May, by draining its
own property, performed a similar service
for Central. Now Central is said to have
heard whisperings that its benefactor is at
work calculating the proportion of exes,
it should charge its neighbor. Several Cen-
tral barrackers have hastened round to
ascertain the legal position, but about all
they have been able to discover ,is that most
verdicts in past mine-drainage compensation
cases have been retrospective.
* * * *
Managerial explanation of Edna May Cen-
tral’s poor return for April:—■
When opened up at the 75ft. level the ore body
varied greatly in width, and for the first few feet of
driving values ranged from 17dwt. to 20dwt, per ton.
Both the south and east faces of the boomerang-
shaped ore body became poor owing to a large per-
centage of valueless kaolin characteristic of the
Central and experienced to some extent also in the
Edna May. It was impossible to separate the mul-
lock from the quartz, and everything was put through
the batterj'.
Well, in the name of honest mining, why
wasn’t this reported at the time?
* * * *
A clamor for a Royal Commission has
been raised in Perth by some of the victims
of the recent Westonia boom. There, is
grim humor in the proposal that a Commis-
sion should discover what has become of
the one-, two-, three- and four-oz. ore men-
tioned in the official despatches of the Edna
May Central, and the whereabouts of the
valuable veins of quartz alleged to have been
exposed in the Consolidated and neighboring
blocks.
For some time past the Lake View and
Star has been worked at a loss, and the result
of the first month’s run of the reorganised
treatment plant isn’t brilliant. Revenue
amounted to £15,162, but expenditure (inclu-
sive of development £930 and capital £743)
exceeded that figure by £1351. When the
mill is working at its full capacity a better
showing is predicted, but the co. is never
likely to equal its past performances as a
div. payer.
* * * *
The Augusta (W.A.) is to be sold, lock,
stock and barrel, in a week or so. Years
ago an English co., the Golden Rhine, got
some good returns from the show and then
gave it up. . Later on a local syndicate
took out some rich crushings, and believing
that it had Unearthed everything worth hav-
ing, sold out to the Angusta co. The latter
was always reporting good values, but some-
how the battery failed to keep pace with
the reports.
Black Range Pinnacles (W.A.) has got
nearly all its plant up or on the ground, and
expects to start crushing shortly. Practi-
cally the whole of the development done in
the property is at the 100 ft. level, where the
lode, which has been driven on for over
800 ft., is reckoned to have an average assay
value of 15dwt. The show is regarded
locally as a good thing.
Blair Athol colliery (Q.) made a net
profit for the half-year ended March 31 of
£3413. The output was 29,300 tons, so the
net profit per ton was about 2s. 4d.
MacDonald’s molybdenite show in N.Q.
is to be known as the Kitchener. Some 4000
tons of 3i p.c. molybdenite have been proved
in a 4Jft. lode over a length of 130 ft. In
the winze the lode is 6ft. wide, and gives 4
p.c. by assay. The flotation process with
oil is to be used to recover the molybdenite,
and the equipment is up to-date. The little
show is within a mile of the Etheridge rail-
way, so there will be no difficulty in mar-
keting the concentrates.
Gorella (Q.) resumed smelting on May
15, and every effort is to be made to buck it
up by vigorous development work. At the
Rosebud mine the 200 ft. level is to be con-
tinued to the south to pick up the shoot of
ore which at the 300 ft. level has for the
last 12ft. averaged 6 p.c. for a width of
48in. Reserves are limited, but the Rose-
bud is practically a self-fluxing proposition
producing ironstone and calcite, and with
copper at its present price lower grade ore
than usual can be made to pay.
Gympie (Q.), . crushing 9272 tons in
April, claims an average of 345. per ton.
The Scottish Gympie, though by far the
largest producer, is still very low grade,
recent extractions averaging hardly sdwt. per
ton. The North Smithfield has a large body
of crushing stuff above the 615 ft. level, and
the stone is carrying “fair gold.” No. 2 N.
Columbia Smithfield is collecting a few
specimens from a winze below the 1500 ft.
level.
Hampden Cloncurry (Q.) has been re-
porting low copper values from the Duchess
recently, so the development of a big make
of pyritic ore at the bottom level (300 ft.)
of the Trekelano is very opportune. The
south drive is in 57ft. and the last 10ft.
has been in heavy basic ore worth 8 p.c.
copper. A crosscut has still to prove the
full width of this ore body, which is showing
7ft. at the end of the drive, with no wall
disclosed on the western side. The April
smelting return was 096 tons copper, 2040 z.
gold and 59110 z. silver from 6,928 tons ore.
What looks like a big deposit of nearly
pure baryta, a mineral used in making white
“lead,” has been unearthed in the Frankford
district. (Tas.). A trial consignment of
five tons is alleged to have yielded 99 p.c.
of pure baryta, and 50 tons is now being
got out.
There’s a proposal on foot in Laun-
ceston (Tas.) to start again the Tas-
mania mine, at Beaconsfield, which closed
down last year after running since 1877.
The mine turned out over £3,000,000 worth
of gold (and incidentally paid £772,071 in
divs.) ; but while there was still plenty of
gold in it when it shut up (or so it is gener-
ally supposed) there was also a terrible lot
of water, and the cost of pumping took all
the gilt off the gingerbread. But there is an
idea that if hydro-electric power can be got
from the State scheme the cost may be
knocked down to about a quarter (some
optimistic persons talk about one-eighth) of
what it was with the steam plant; where-
fore there may be money in it still. It
depends a lot on what other demand there
may be for hydro-electric power in the
north of the Speck; a special transmission
line for the Tasmania mine isn’t likely.
* * * *
After a two years’ hunt for it Major
Dunn, a British Army officer turned pro-
spector, reckons he has found the “mother
lode” of the alluvial gold deposits in the
Lisle Valley (Tas.). It is a big dyke on
the northern slope of Mt. Arthur, from
which it is said that coarse gold can be
obtained. A Launceston syndicate has gone
into the show and proposes to spend a few
thousands in preliminary work.
* * * *
The success of a new process for convert-
ing Taranaki (M.L.) iron sand into pig
iron at a profitable price (the process is
the invention of two Melbourne men) is
said to be assured, and a plant capable of
turning out 200 tons of pig iron per week is
to be erected. The sand is mixed with coal-
dust and treated in coking ovens, and the
product is then ready for the blast furnace.
* * * *
Bradstreet, the prospector of Mount
Maroumba (Arnheim’s Land), is again mak-
ing for the wilds in Northern Queensland.
This time the lure is molybdenite. Though
a young man, “The Shadow” is already well
known, having made two trips right across
Australia and any number of shorter ones
in W.A., the Northern Territory and the
Peninsula (N.Q.).
A.W.8.: (1) This paper was never asked
to “withdraw and apologise” for what it
said about the Australian Amicable. It
wouldn’t have withdrawn one ivord if it
had been asked. > (2) If any canvasser is
obtaining people’s business on that repre-
sentation, or on the representation that
“those connected with the paper have since
tried to purchase shares and insure with
the company,” he is a rogue.
Last- year 28,314,539 tons of ore were
milled on the Rand for an approximate
return of 40 millions sterling. Working costs
mopped up £25,000,000 and shareholders
got nearly £8,000,000 of the balance. The
field now produces 40 p.c. of the world’s gold
output and 67 p.c. of the Empire’s produc-
tion. Colored labor employed totalled
211,000, and whites about 23,000. The
S’African Govt, has intimated that it wants
a bite of £500,000 in addition to usual taxes
on the industry this year. Of course, mine-
owners are grumbling, but all the satisfac-
tion they have got so far from Botha has
been a blunt request to pay up and look
pleasant.
* # * *
Plymouth Consolidated, the Californian
resurrection into which Loring and others
induced the Sons of Gwalia to put £250,000.
has issued another annual report. In all
129,500 tons were treated for an average
of 255. 7d. per ton. Three Is. divs. were
paid during the year, and another of the
same amount was declared a month ago.
Loring is acting as a sort of local director,
and may, it is whispered, sever his connec-
tion with 8., M. & Co. and remain there.
The flotation process is becoming a big
thing in U.S.A. In south-eastern Missouri
practically the whole output of 200,000 tons
of lead last year came from plants with
flotation equipments, and it is estimateu
that 17,000 tons were due to flotation alone.
The average lead recovery since flotation
processes came into vogue is 85 n.c., com-
pared with 77 p.c. before its adoption. And
it is now considered that very large sup-
plies of low-grade oxidised zinc ores can be
made payable by coating the stuff with a film
of -cofiper sulphide, done by treating it with
a dilute solution of copper sulphate, and
then capturing the zinc by flotation. This
looks like having a considerable bearing on
the future of the zinc market.
*"** * ‘ *
Philip Argali, the great mining engineer
and metallurgist of Colorado (U.S.A.), says
his biggest achievement was to introduce the
eight-hour working day into mills in his
State, in 1899, without asking his directors.
He' claims that he got better work and, in
time, a better class of workman, elevating
the workman, and lowering the costs. The
system the Australian miner has worked
under has had much to do with the fact
that he has for many years borne such a
high reputation in distant countries, which
always grab him as a man who is surpris-
ingly willing and competent to tackle any-
thing. Having had time to develop his
brains, he can use them in a manner sur-
prising to people accustomed to the unintelli-
gent products of long hours and short wages.
In no class of Workmen is initiative better
developed than in the Australian miner, and
especially the gold-miner —bad to drive and
good to lead ; quick to size up a boss who
tries to disguise his own incompetence by
nigger-driving and cheeseparing tactics;
quick to give even a disliked autocrat the
credit of being “a smart bloke at his work”
if he deserves it.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A Good Corn
Remover.
Those who suffer from corns and hard skin
on the soles of the feet w'll find it a great
boon to apply a little Atla Resin to the corn,
which, when applied to the corn or hard skin,
and a little covering of cotton wool on the
the top to prevent it sticking to the stocking,
will remove the corn in a day or two. The
Atla Resin seems to act by getting thor-
oughly into complete contact with the corn,
and thus being able to penetrate it, and not
to act on the surface only. It is certainly
the latest cure for these troublesome and
painful structures, and, according to all re-
ports by those who have used it, one of the
best.* [2

A WOMAN'A
LETTER
Sydney, May 22, 1916.
My Dear Arini, —
The town is full of Anglican bishops. The
shepherds have come in from Outback runs
—where bishops are supposed to live in
their saddles—and from inter-State cities.
Adelaide sent Bishop Thomas —-and really
didn’t mind a bit. Bendigo’s contribution is
fine old ex-banker Langley, whose arch-
diaconal whiskers were once the joy of Syd-
ney’s Church Hill. With him is .the severe-
looking Pain of Gippsland, who also used
to expound the Word in Sydney— at St.
John’s, Darlinghurst. Riley of Perth and
Donaldson of Brisbane have been buckling
up their archgaiters under the Stricklands’
roof.
Sheila Loughborough (formerly Chis-
holm) and Mrs. Clive Dangar and her small
family, turned out of Egypt, went off to
England in the same boat as the ex-Indian
viceroy Hardinge. Bridegroom Lough-
borough accompanied his pretty Sheila, so
his valuable health is evidently not quite
restored. Clive Dangar was a major in
the Light Horse. Before the war he used
to adorn the 13th Hussars, but left the
dressy regiment when father Abby died. With
Mrs. H. Chisholm as fellow-voyagers (all
pyjama’d and buttressed with lifebelts) went
Kathie Osborne, Audrey Chirnside and Nor-
man Brookes. Most of the Australian
“Society” set have left Cairo. And “a good
job too,” says a lone Kangaroo who wants
to get on with his work.
The Sydney Needlework Guild, which dis-
tributes its goods amongst certain hospitals
and crowded tenements, paraded before the
Strickland lady at Government House dur-
ing the week. It works all the year round
by stealth, and when it is discovered it
flutters a list of presidents and vice-presi-
dents long enough to terrify any critic. The
company fell back on the refreshment-tables
after exhibiting its seams and gussets.
The modern Aide gets off lightly on these
occasions, and generally appears only at the
climax behind a plate of cakes. In former
years it was otherwise. When the then
Lady Mary Lygon ruled over the youthful
Beauchamp’s household, she was closely
attended at sewing shows by the polished
Victor Corkran, who used to blush behind
his silk hat on the intimate garments of
childhood being thrust under his aouiline
nose. Lady Rawson cut the silken thread
that bound an A.D.C. to Dorcas meetings,
and in Sydney he has been a free man ever
since.
“Jarvisfield” Antill is now Brigadier-
General, with the 3rd Light Horse to assist
him in his search for the enemy. After the
Gadlipoli gruelling the illimitable desert is
this smart soldier’s present lot. Or lately
was, No one—except the enemy—really
knows where any Light Horseman is just at
the moment.
That pluckiest of little women, Gertrude
Tait (now Lewis), had a few hurried days
of packing-up last week, and then carried
baby up the plank of the Osterley. Father,
who had been ordered off to Gallipoli before
the infant was born, has since been sent on
to France; and there is going to be a
great, exciting time during a promised fur-
lough. He is one of the big, tough men
whom the Northern Territory has sent to
the Front; she is the astonishing little
woman who became matron of the polyglot
Thursday Island Hospital when she was
hardly more than a girl, and afterwards, as
superintending nurse to the Papuan Govern-
ment, dealt single-handed with the awful
outbreak of dysentery among the natives
and others at the Lakekamu gold rush. No
white woman had ever been to the awful
place before ; I doubt whether another has
been there since.
. But if there’s a bright time ahead there
is_ a dark cloud behind Mrs. Lewis. A fort-
night ago she helped to bury Lieut. Tait, her
brother—a son of old Archdeacon Tait, for
so lopg of Parkes. The fine youngster, who
was in the Dental Corps, had the misfor-
tune to strike a camp suffering from an
epidemic of virulent tonsilitis. He con-
tracted it, and literally bled to death after
a . slight operation, in circumstances which
simply shriek for a thorough inquiry.
Genial Bobby Luckham, whose smile had
f° r 30 . years warmed The Bulletin’s
advertising department, slipped quietly into
eternity, by the long, familiar wash of
Manly seas, on Sunday morning. “Died in
his sleep” was the easy manner of his leav-
ing. There are none of his kith in Aus-
tralia, but there are two sisters in England.
And until a couple of years ago there was a
venerable father—Bobby himself was 57 —
with whom a dutiful son had a habit of
eating Christmas dinners, though it was
more than a tram-ride there and back.
Rider Haggard drops in for a day this
week on his way to Maoriland. He has
been in Westralia considering the poison-
plant how it grmvs.
At last the Tom Dibbs gift home, beauti-
ful old Graythwaite, is to get into business,
with a full house. It has been more or less
empty since it was opened with a flourish
in the summer. The V.A.D. can’t wield
the same authority as a trained Nightin-
gale. So the latter treasure is now being
searched for.
The Youngsters’ Exhibition was opened
by Governor Strickland to the accompani-
ment of an interminable list of Gnashional
Anthems. Some mercy really ought to be
shown to the old folks. They are expected
to stand to the bitter end even if they faint
away at the finish.
The Exhibition itself was admirably man-
aged. It was the genuine drama of the
Child. Many of the youngsters came from
the Free Kindergartens of crowded city
areas, and went through their “demonstra-
tions” as artlessly as if they were playing
round the home doorstep. The Alexandra
Hospital “exhibits” were attended bv Night-
ingales. The little invalids remained calm
while the public harried their guardians with
questions sacred and profane. John Mackay,
who presides at Kindergarten meetings,
shadowed the infant hair-bows every day.
The “comforter” was one of the thrills
of the pictorial section. The microbes in
attendance were quite as large as bumble
bees.
The concert in aid of the sth Artillery
Brigade and the A.M.C. comforts fund was
a surging success, in which the holders of
unreserved tickets were prepared to engage
the haughty reserved seats in mortal combat.
Elderly citizenesses pleaded for half a step
to sit on, and perched on the stairs that
climb to the Conservatorium gallery. Mrs.
Herbert Maitland, Mrs. George Lane Mul-
lins and Miss A. C. Cook organised the
show, and Gertrude Palmer arranged the
programme. Society preened its plumage,
which has rather moulted since the war, in
something like its old decolletd form.
The Conservatorium, with its sheltei-less
front patch, is a cold proposition when
sudden storms meet the crowd that pours
out after a show. The other night dam-
sels in thin slippers had to patter over
flooded paths, and stout citizens unpre-
pared for rain splashed through the open
to the distant tram.
Still another
Kindergarten lady
who has been
pushing the
Kiddies’ Exhibi-
tion—the vivaci-
ous and youthful-
looking Principal
of the Sydney Kin-
dergarten Train-
ing College—Miss
Harriet Dumolo.
Passing through
the education mill
Miss Dumolo,
after some train-
ing, went to New-
castle to direct the
Free Kindergarten
there. At that
time it was largely
financed by Mrs. Essie Jenyns Wood.
Later, the Union recognised the Syd-
ney woman’s special gifts as a teacher,
and sent her to Europe for about two years.
After picking up Froebel crumbs here and
there she took up the serious duties of
school marm in the Kindergarten College—
once the home of John Rae, who built it in
the davs when Darlinghurst was considered
“the bush.”
Sydney’s jumping-jack comedian W.
S. Percy, lives of late at West Hampstead.
Rehearsing to himself his part, and mutter-
ing unconsciously something about being a
spy, he found that the tube passengers were
concentratin? their attention on his move-
ments. Now, he says, he chooses a more
retired spot for his memorising—Hampstead
cemetery, for instance. It recalls another
story of a Colonel who left with a consider-
able knowledge of nearly everything except
soldiering. Luckily he had a first-class
adjutant, upon whose mercy he threw himself.
Before any parade the crowd used to watch
the colonel and the adjutant in deep consul-
tation. Then for the next 10 minutes the
colonel would march to and fro repeating
his lesson: “Advance in column of sub-
sections, advance in column of sub-sections,
advance in column of sub-sections. Advance
in battery column, advance in battery col-
umn, advance in battery column.” I sup-
pose they’ll give him the V.C. for it.
In St. James’s Church, Margaret Middle-
ton, daughter of G. Middleton, of Bondi,
promised to love, honor and (in moderation)
obey Lieut. Harold Le Gay Holthouse. The
petite bride, who came up to her tall sol-
dier’s shoulder, was in foam white, with
layers of tulle in excelsis, edged with silver
gauze. A tulle veil encrusted with seed
pearls fell over her dark hair. Bridesmaids
Marie Middleton and Ruth Holthouse wore
evening frocks of azure blue, garlanded with
pink posies. (No getting away from that
dash of pink!) Instead of mere hats, the
bridesmaidens put their heads into long pink
tulle veils, with a picturesque Smile of Asia
effect.
The Evening Sun (Noo Yark) gives
itself elbow room in an interview with the
Australian artist, Mrs. Montagu Marks. The
Royal Miniature Society of England-—a
haughty body which only sometimes opens
its doors to strangers —has elected Stella
Marks a member. A portrait of Marjorie
Williamson, eldest daughter of J.C., is
from her brush. By way of introducing an
Australian artist of note to its readers the
Murkan begins thus : —•
When you think of Australia, does your mind’s
eye conjure up visions of endless sheep ranches, and
innumerable black sheep, younger sons of English
families, or do you think of Art?
They really do say nice things about us in
N’York !
The news that Elsie Greene, eldest
daughter of the late George Greene, of lan-
dra, is engaged, comes through this week.
The Greenes migrated to England some
time ago, braving the rigors of the absentee
tax. Since the war Elsie has been here
and there, making soup in soldiers’ can-
teens, and flitting from England to France
on war work. Possibly that’s where she
met her fate, Colonel Kelly, who has been
fighting side by side with Papa Joffre’s gal-
lant lot.
This year the Uni. Undergrad, let himself
go in a very mild way as host at a garden
party. Afternoon tea was handed round in
the great stone sarcophagus, where the
pictures of dead-and-gone Chancellors peer
through the gloom from the walls. Gov-
ernor Strickland with his Eldest Treasure
and Aide Firth got in early, but the august
Uni. crowd was ready to welcome them. In
the evening some of the grads, let them-
selves go a bit more. Good old dad will
pay the bill as usual.
F. M. Lindley, 114 a Pitt-street, is pre-
pared to take the name and address of vol-
unteer workers for War Chest Day. The
rally is dated June 30.
The Indian summer we call winter has
hit the furrier’s business fairly hard. Only,
fairly. In spite of golden days of languor-
ous warmth, when the domestic cat basks
on the back wall, there are female Stoics
who wear fur coats and carry muffs. The
cold snap before Easter saved the fashion-
able tailor from despair. So things are not
so bad as.they. might be, in spite of the war
and the lingering summer.
Magpie plumage is promised for the
spring. It’s a thrifty device, for everyone
has something black and something white to
toss together. Only let us pray there won’t
be a revival of the pre-war craze for even-
ing wear, one half of the lady being uphol-
stered in white, while the other half turns
inkily to the astounded spectator.
Infant prodigies gathered together the
other afternoon to take their Conserva-
torium prizes, given by the brothers Grace.
The admiring crowd of friends and relatives,
to say nothing of infant sisters who took
piano solos in bad part, filled the concert
room at Broadway. A tot named Phyllis
McDonald, all violin and brown windmill
hairbows, won the gold medal in the fiddle
section. I recall the bowing of this young-
ster chiefly as the episode of a small
dimpled arm. T. J. Gibb, a boy tenor-
winner of two gold medals—piped sweetly of
“Angels ever bright and fair.” The juvenile
winner of the piano scholarship, Ada Brook,
was another pleasing circumstance. Regis-
trar Warwick handed over the trophies on
behalf of Yerbrugghen.
The Domain Soldiers’ Buffet has had its
measurements published. The news that its
counter is 45ft. long will help the by-law
which tells ladv assistants that they must
remain inside the »ervery. The Ameliora-
tion Committee having months ago chris-
tened its show the Anzac Buffet,
there’ll be switches on the green if the
Domain contingent insists on duplicating
the hard-worked name.
After a long illness, Mrs. Montagu
Rothery closed her tired eyes the other day.
The frail old gentleman who survives used
to be the chief pillar in the tabernacle of the
Society for the Protection of Animals, and
is still a battler for the preservation of wild
life in our bushlands.

Under the stimulus of sock competitions
many a woman knits her best. If the
referees are occasionally buffeted by unsuc-
cessful candidates—well, football ‘umpires
can extend their sympathy. Now, the
French Australian League of Help comes
along with its carnival. Prizes for the best
heeled and toed specimens will be adjudi-
cated for on French National Dav. Even
the lift that shoots up to the Macdonell
House eyrie is placarded with details as to
the length of leg that must meet the judge’s
eye on July 14.
Mrs. George Black—who goes to Eng-
land to see a doctor —was sped on her way
by the usual Australia tea party. Wishes
for her safe voyage and other expressions
of good-will were voiced by Mrs. Holman.
Chief Secretary Black had hoped to go too;
but clouds blew up, and he stays here to
help his friend Holman out of the troubles
that that young man is bound to fall or
wriggle into.
Mrs. Henry Solomon’s friends mourned
for her on May 10. Tireless in Good Works
in her younger days, the kind old lady leaves
a sheaf of pleasant memories behind, and
stalwart sons who have done their bit at the
Front.
Dr. Lucy Gullett and Miss Constance
Carson have gone to England after doing
war-time work in Egypt.
Director Le Souef won’t march the
animals two by two from their old ark in
Moore Park to the new one at Bradley’s
Head until the rigors of the winter are over.
The Works Department could have the
attractive place ready by July; but so many
of Le Souef’s pets have an objection to new
houses that they won’t venture into them
for months, perhaps ; and while they insist on
sleeping out the only thing to be done is to
either provide them with umbrellas or wait
till the summer is setting in. It will be
September, therefore, before the key is for-
mally turned. Meantime the population of
the monkey and bird departments in the
new premises is growing steadily. They
don’t mind new quarters.
The North Sydney Comedy Club plays
“The New Boy” for the No. 1 Pack Wire-
less Signal Troop fund in the Repertory
Theatre on May 27 and 29.
The infant citizen is at last getting his
share of public attention, a great war hav-
ing sent un his value about 50 per cent.
Two new clinics, where efforts will be made
to instruct mother in the right manner of
feeding the aforesaid citizen, will be opened
shortly. Mrs. George Black sets the Chip-
pendale house in order on May 25.
Have you noticed how early- Victorian the
1916 girl is? Instead of sandal shoes she
wears high boots, but the spats give the
white stockinged effect of the days of sylla-
bub and croquet. Small round hats, of the
pork-pie species, wide skirts and capes
flapping in the wind frock the Girl of the
Moment. But so long as she stops short
of crinolines the tramway people won’t have
to alter any by-laws.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Alelbourne, Alay 22, 1016.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Alalvern shed a farewell tear last week on
the heroic shoulder of General AlcNicoll,
D.S.O. The shedding happened at the mia-
mia of Airs. Leon Jona and her A.AI.C.
captain. AlcNicoll’s peace-time profession
was chiefly represented bv the weighty
Adamson, of Wesley, who is nowadays equal
to any other two school principals. Alel-
bourne’s Alayoress Hennessy and Aiayor
Weller (Alalvern) and his wife each con-
tributed a parting sigh. Alajor Dunhill, of
the A.AI.C., called in for a few minutes to
see that the General's temperature was in
the right place; and a tea oblation was
poured by Airs. John Alonasli, who also has
a General husband, by Airs. Presently-Pre-
mier Hagelthorn and Mrs. Rogers Thomson,
and by Airs. Norris, hack from seeing her
Dr. commence drawing his salary at the
Rockefeller Institute.
At beautiful Hethersett, Dr. Ramsay
Alailer’s voluntary hospital at Burwood,
miracles have been worked since it was
opened a few months ago—mostly with
massage. Sergeant-major Alullens came
there from Gallipoli partially paralysed. Now
he is a recruiting-sergeant, and very forceful
on the platform. Alassage did it. The capa-
city of the hospital is 28 soldiers, and usually
there is a full houjse. It has a billiard-room,
a croquet-lawn and golf-links, and a lot of
lesser nerve-restorers. Dr. Aleares attends
daily to bestow his blessing ; nerve-specialist
Alailer gives a large share of his time; and
there is a staff of girl-volunteers under Sis-
ter Alice Rowand, who gained her best
experience among the miners and other
hard-doers at the Ovens district hospital.
Altogether, as an establishment financed
entirely out of a few private purses, Hether-
sett is the sort of enterprise which deserves
all the encouragement it gets.
Honorary Lieut.-Colonel Norman Brookes
is now “somewhere in France” with the
rest of the boys. When Red Cross affairs
in Egypt had been more or less set in
order, Normal! went to England to give a
hand in straightening up Australian Red
Cross matters there. That job attended to
he crossed the Channel and is doing the
same thing all over again. Airs. Brookes
parted with her husband in Egypt, just
before he sailed for London, and recently
came back to stay with mamma Emmerton
at South Yarra. As soon as an expected
cable arrives she is going to hop off again,
this time to the scene of action in France,
or as close as may be.
The soldier son of Commercial Bank
manager Heron has come back from Egypt
after losing an eye. He is not down-hearted
about that, but would feel more cheerful if
he could see an early chance of getting
back to the riot in some capacity. Before
Armageddon he plied a pen in Dalgety’s. But
what soldier wants a pen when the guns
are still muttering?
Louis Foulet, the youthful oil-painter who
tore back to France and a Lebel rifle when
the Huns broke in, has been wounded a
second time in the leg—very badly. Louis had
a habit of winning medals for painting and
swimming, and now he possesses also the
French Alilitary Cross. He hopes to hobble
back here when the Hogs go home.
Widow Ross-Soden’s son Gordon been
dealt a commission in the British Flying
Corps.
Alajor AValter Berry’s wife farewelled her
husband on the way to his troopship, then
took the first boat for Canada to look in
upon her relatives ere she offers herself as
an army nurse. Professionally, she was a
ministering angel in her pre-matrimonial
days.
The ninth nursing-sister to be married in
the Garrison Chapel at Abassia, Egypt,
Florence St. George Sproule, agreed on
Alarch 27 to take orders (according to the
law in such cases made and provided) from
Captain (hold your brea'th!) Walter Green
Ivor Frederick Steele Hewitt, of the 2nd
(N.S.AAh) Light Horse. She was attached
to the Ist Australian General Hospital, and
so is her uncle, Lieut. Col. W. E. Summons,
A.A.AI.C., who gave her away—discreetly.
The chapel in which the incantations were
made is part of the hospital building, so
everything was under one roof. Florence
bewitched beholders in a mess uniform of
grey silk. She is one of the clever Alel-
bourne family of which the beauteous A iva
St. George Sproule, M. 8., Ch.B. (“now,”
according to Aledical Register, “known
as Viva St. George Summons”), forms a
striking unit.
The able-footed Alelbourne young men
who advertise their absence from the war
by slithering fit the Glaciarium are now
doing so under appropriate conditions, the
color scheme of the electric lighting being
red, white and black.
Airs. Archibald Alenzies has trundled in
as her war contribution a Rolls-Royce car,
which, is to be shipped straight away to
the Front.
Arts and Crafts Aubrey Chomley and
the Fisken sisters are at the Chelsea Hos-
pital, London, teaching raffia-weaving and
knitting to wounded soldiers.
The Ben Nathans are doing a roaring
business, exhibiting their dreadfully valu-
able conservatories of orchids to the public
on Saturdays and Sundays at a shilling a
head. The cash goes to a branch of the
Patriotic League just formed at Rippon
Lea, Elsternwick, where shirt and sock toil
is done twice a week.
The A'ictoria Leaguers will pour into the
Alasonic Hall on June 7 and 8 and gaze at
Patriotic tableaux representing war car-
toons. Proceeds go to buy books and com-
forts for troopships and the Red Cross.
The responsible women in it are Joan Brett,
Aliss Austen and Airs. G. Barlow.
The “Girls AA'ho Stayed at Home,” the
pretty company of amateur pierrettes who
raised stockingsful of cash for the Red
Cross last year, are powdering up again.
They gave their first show of the season at
Canterbury last Thursday, for the benefit
of the Rest Home out that way.
Adelaide tunes up her genuine Strad :
For a couple of days the Exhibition Building
hasn’t been the Exhibition Building at all, but a
mock-turtle Elizabethan village with Plymouth Hoe
and Drake’s historical game of bowls thrown in, and
stalls that took their title and the dresses of the
saleswomen from various nursery rhymes. The
Returned Soldiers’ Association and the Cheer-Up
Society are both heavily engaged, and the profits will
be theirs. The Galway dame said the opening words
while his Ex. was at Glenelg presenting the Cheer-
Up colors to the 43rd Battalion. Amongst the
stalls at the Exhibition fair was a pocket-edition
art gallery, arranged by Mrs. W. L. Strachan, where
you. could get anything except genuine Rembrandts
for sixpence.
The Art Gallery has been showing signs of anima-
tion. There has been a rearrangement of the pic-
tures, and an attempt at arranging in the limited
space available the new 1 possessions that have been
acquired under the Way bequest. Meanwhile the
Agent-General, Will Ashton, and Percy Arnold have
successfully recommended that £9OO of the £2OOO
entrusted to them should be spent on a landscape by
H. Hughes-Stanton, A.R.A., and an animal study by
Arnesby Brown, A.R.A.
A lot of the friends he made here are mourning
for Lieut.-Colonel Patterson, who died at the Base
Hospital in Melbourne. When invalided from Gal-
lipoli, he made his home here till a few w T eeks ago,
when, with his wife and children, he moved to
Victoria.
The death is reported, from Canada, of Mrs. Allan
Baker—a South Australian, whose home had been
for some years in the Dominion. The husband is
a brother of the late Richard Chaffey Baker, first
President of the Senate.
The Red Cross Society has granted £5OOO out of
its South Australian funds for a convalescent hospital
at Woodville, and a special button day is to be
arranged on its behalf soon. Better start saving
your small change now.
Dr. Ennis and Mr. Bevan have gone in plain
clothes as the Adelaide University’s ambassadors to
the Melbourne conference that is to discuss a
Universities’ scheme for public examinations in
music.
The Frightfulness of these Six-o’clockers! The
South Aus. Caledonian Society has just indulged in
a quarterly meeting without whisky. The Thistle
can get along without much water, but !
One of our Most Select titled dames cast a billy
full of comforts into the trenches, with a written
request that the gift should be acknowledged. In
due time the acknowledgment arrived: ‘‘Thanks, old
girl, for the billy.” The impudence!
The Reaper’s swathe for Alay included
Airs. Laurence Doyle, a prominent member
of Malvern’s social congregation. The dig-
nified Laurence is alleged to have retired
from active service in an ancient 'whisky
firm with a neat fortune of £60,000, which
permitted his genial lady the luxury of con-
siderable benevolent expenditure, handled
in a manner which brought her no advertis-
ing. Julia, the only Doyle offspring, married
Gibney Roche.
The widow of the late Dr. Tom Hewlett,
and her sister-in-law, wife of Dr. Fred Hew-
lett, passed out last week within a few
hours of each other and were buried on the
same day. Dr. Tom, who earned many good
fees in his day, was noted for his charming
manner and well-groomed beard —both good
assets in his profession.
Parson Ernest Hughes, of St. Peter’s, has
always shunned the currant-bun and cold-
tea entertainment indulged in by the usual
blackcoat. He and his handsome wife
lately treated the choir boys to “So Long,
Letty.” There’s something in being a
chorister at Hughes’s church.
The Bowes Kellys’ six-foot daughter Edna,
while staying at the Australia with grannie
and auntie ABllar, tried a short cut across
the glass roof of the lounge, with terrifying
results. The loungers heard a crash over-
head, and looking up, observed a shapely
leg, booted and silk-stockinged, dangling
from the glass overhead. They surged up-
stairs shouting prescriptions for help ; but
before they had decided on anything a rescue
had been effected and Edna found that she
had escaped with a fright, a hole or two in
her stocking and a scratched leg.
The athletic Mrs. John Sanderson
threatens to become an arc-light m _ the
musical world. Bast week she entertained
the Verbrugghen Quartet in her music-room,
and the Quartet filled the quart-pot of
melody to overflowing.
The A'erbrugghen string foursome at the
Athenaeum last week drew all the best fur
coats and hair ornaments of Melbourne as
well as the musical dervishes. Both sets
of vice-regals attended fairly regularly, and
the John Maddens came along to get what
social limelight was left over. The Stanley
dame shrouds herself in a black veil when
she goes out o’ nights and spends quite five
minutes unwinding the mummy-wrap when
she gets to her seat. Lady Helen manages
to secure cloaks with a Royal Family cut
about them emphasised by a very Alexan-
drian-Mary style of hair-building and a kind
expression. Front seaters included a big
rally of Belgians led by Consul-General
Lauwers and the handsome A anderkelen.
Fresh from hospital, tenor Kirby shook his
auburn locks at the show, and somebody
remarked aloud that AA T alter isn’t dead after
all.
The Lady Northcote Orchestral Trust
Fund should shorten its title and lengthen
its artistic knowledge of platform decora-
tion. Good music deserves a good setting,
and though A'erbrugghen and his little party
tried to look as though they were unconscious
of the crude screens, green crinkled
paper, dusty palms and half-dead flowers,
they weren’t quite successful. They looked
about as if they half expected to see some
china dogs and w 7 ax flowers.
The first concert of the Verbrugghen
Quartet gathered the music-drinkers to-
gether and enabled Alelba to burst on her
native city afresh. The undistinguished look-
ing parties from both Government houses
ambled in the usual tvay down to the usual
easy-chaired reserve. Then, like a genuine
A'elasquez come to life again, Nellie glided
along looking like a Spanish queen. Right
up to her chin line she was clad in rich
night-black velvet and furs, matched per-
fectly by her jetty hair and eyes.
Unstarved Lena Kingston, having been
occupied for years in training her voice to
perform in public, has lately made several
appearances on the concert platform, with
popular approval. Papa Kingston, who for
the past century or two has been selling
umbrellas, did the fair thing by his three
girls, w r ho all showed early signs of being
able to entertain an audience. He gave
little Olive an opportunity to ripen in the
Marches! nursery; but she presently came
back to Australia and married into the
Clark family, which floated to the surface of
the financial sea during the YVestralian
bcom. Ruby, too, glowed briefly in the lime-
light as a serpentine dancer 1 ’ore throw-
ing a coil or two around Rollo Thomson and
setting up her own kitchen. Art is long
and time is fleeting, yet Lena may still get
in some good work if she keeps moving.
“Rosna’s” note from London: —
Hymen is still busy. He is just getting his
torches polished for the wedding of Patricia, daughter
of Mrs. Alexander Brown, of Melbourne, and Lieut.
James Phillips Jones, a Welsh doctor who is with
the R.A.M.C. At the tail end of March he did
his best (old Hymen, not the medico from Wales)
for Nora Somerville, grand-daughter of the late
John Charles Lloyd, one time well known in the
Liverpool Plains of the Ma State. Her benedict
is Capt. Cecil Fox, R.N., of the Powerful, who has
helped to capture four Hun torpedo boats and done
sundry other valorous things in the chilly blasts of
the North Sea. The Powerful provided the cleric
for this wedding and the Naval Air Service the next-
best man, and the wedding cake had a model of the
old Victory in sugar on the top. But the bride
didn’t wear a sou’-wester.
These are parlous times for some of the
lesser charities—kindergartens, for instance.
So Airs. Robert Hooke, who superintends the
free kindergarten department of the
AV.C.T.U., makes a desperate appeal to the
public to send along its worn-out silver
thimbles and damaged jewellery, so that the
useless articles may be boiled down and fed
to the flock at Richmond.
The false-alarm queens are running to
and fro, and standing on their dignity and
sooling on their retainers and men-at-arms.
Airs. Woolcott put in her claim for the
monarchy at the outset, and then retired
from the struggle. Whereon Irma Caron
was persuaded to come in, and for the sake
of the St. A r incent Hospital cause the little
songstress will go to the poll without a hope.
All the other “Queens” have got three
weeks’ start of her in the humiliating, banal
business.
Artist Ambrose Patterson writes from
Honolulu that he likes the place so well and
so many commissions have been projected at
him that he intends staying there for some
time, instead of going on to N’Yark as he
orminallv intended.

A mild example of military swank is ti le
effort of the lately-hatched lieutenant to
avoid looking fluffy in his new uniform He
does it by bashing his headgear hard and
often, until it appears as though it had been
through a strenuous and bitter campaign
Then he puts it on again and looks the whcb
world in the face for the first time since he
climbed into his beautiful outfit.

"J.G. : Does anyone know anything of the
keawe, or algaroba tree? lam recommended to grow
it in Australia. All over the Hawaiian Islands it is
said to be flourishing in places where other trees,
shrubs or glass could not exist. It is a bean, and a
forest of it is said to be capable of providing enough
tucker for countless droves of cattle and pigs, its
fruit being very rich in sugar and protein. In addi-
tion to having this fodder value I am assured it is
one of the best honey-yielding trees in the world;
one of the best for firewoods; ditto for tannin; ditto
for gum; excellent for vinegar; ditto for piles owing
to toughness and ability to resist the toredo; it
enriches the ground because it is a legume; and is an
exceedingly handsome shade and shelter tree. Now
what’s the matter with it ?

"Pick : Here s my record in spread-
eagling. I flavored with strychnine a bear I had
skinned on a Bibbenluke sheep run, Monaro
(X.S.M.). Next day 14 eagles were spread out to
help me to the P. P. Board’s ss. per beak. The
wing stretch of one fellow went 9ft. 4in. Two
others bettered Bft., and a chicken spanned oft. 3in.
I have seen the Monaro mountain eagle attack a
sore-backed horse and a cow with a young calf, and
I once discovered two of them hovering suspiciously
near a small boy. I was the boy.

'D.I-." : One .T. T. Johnson claims to have
seen and tested the world’s record pumpkin. He
says it was exhibited years ago at a show at Camp-
bell to« n (Tas.), where the wrinkly merinos grow,
and that it turned the scale at 2601 b. After, this he
has no time for degenerate modern vegetables that
only weigh a cwt. or so.

BILLY: “Not that, Boss! You see, I chop all pheller wood for Ah Chong, the cook

“G.L.H.”: “Stingo” (B. 20/4/’l6) is
wrong when he says that a high-velocity bullet beats
a Martini-Henry or Snider at giving the knockout.
The high-velocity bullet is the most accurate and
will hit at a far greater range, but the big bullet is
all on its own at short and medium range. I have
had one Mauser bullet into me and two through me,
and I was right again in a few weeks. I saw a man
hit behind the neck, the Mauser bullet passing through
the tongue and taking two teeth out with it. He,
too, was soon right. But if the slug had been from
a Martini there would have been a different tale to
tell. A high-velocity bullet is good if it is of the
expanding or soft-nose breed. Would not “Stingo”
sooner have a Lee-Enfield bullet go through him at
100 yards at a velocity of 2000 ft. per sec. than a
Martini-Henry at 1300 ft. per second? I know I
would.

"North Coast" : A violent eruption in the
shire council had thrown a promising young man
into the mayoral chair, and his native town of Two
Bridges indulged in a banquet to celebrate the
triumph. -After is M orship had been well buttered,
Hairy Bin, the local butcher, rose and waved his
brimming glass in the direction of the star-guest's
beaming father. “Gennulmun,” he cried, “I ask
you—charge your glasses an' drink health of our
dear oF fren’ Joe Wirefence! Joe Wirefence, gennul-
mun, deserves well of this town—'e sired its mayor!”

"Too”: I will guarantee hot water as the
cure for Portuguese man-of-war. Have seen washing-
blue, soda and other antidotes tried, but it was my
hot water that always did the trick.

"Eddyson : It was at about a quarter to
eight, before the morning shift went on. that Gaire
drew our attention to the man on the fence, opposite
Mrs. Irwine s. “I noticed him sitting there last
night,” said Gaire. “Now, what's his game?” “I
dunno," Cobb replied, “but if his intentions towards
the old girl ain't honorable I’d. like to be there
when she gets at him with a fire-iron.” The man
on the fence across the road from Mrs. Irwine’s
home did not look formidable; he was small, and
thin with it. and the frayed fragments of his pants
fluttered in the wind. Mrs. Irwine was a lone, lorn
woman, Willie Irwine having mysteriously disap-
peared three years earlier. At about 11 o’clock
Gaire noted that the suspicious stranger had crossed
the road and perched himself on Mrs. Irwine's own
fence. By noon he was sitting on the grass-widow’s
side fence, his face towards the window. At three
P-m- he was on the woodheap. Towards six o’clock
the unknown was dumped on the block beside Mrs.
Irwine’s kitchen door, twirling his tattered hat be-
tween his sharp shins. And still there was no appear-
ance of Mrs. Irwine. “I don't like it,” said Gaire
“I'll just run along, and see if the old girl’s all
right.” Gaire strolled down to the house and
entered by the front door. Mrs. Irwine was sitting
m the parlor, with an air of expectancv. 4s Gaire
entered at the front the tattered man stole in at the
back, and seated himself on the form in tlje kitchen.
‘T suppose you know there's a suspicious-looking
man hanging about your house?” said Gaire. “I
do,” said Mrs. Irwine. “Would you like him
shifted?” “No, no, no. Just leave him to me.”
She went into the kitchen. The intruder arose
humbly and faced her. He retreated before her
glance into a corner by the fireplace. Mrs. Irwine
took up a broom handle and poked him in the ribs.
He soughed like a punctured balloon and went down.
She hit him nine times with the broom handle and
then broke a dinner-plate over his head. Then she
paused, and at last the man spoke. He picked a
fragment of delf out of his scalp and smiled wanlv.
“It’s good to be home, Annie,” he said. “I was
afraid I wouldn't be welcome.” Gaire went back
to the huts and reported. “Willie Irwine has come
home,” he said.

"Oh Mali": Mick Ellis was out with four
others on the Thompson River (Q.) getting a break-
fast of yellow belly for Good Friday. It was 4 a.m.
and moonlight. Just as a dozen wild duck were fly-
ing past Mick made a cast, the line twisted round a
drake's neck and Mick landed the meat. It was the
best bit of line work performed in Bananaland since
Tom Dooley caught an emu the same way.

THE SEVENTH WAVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is an article of faith with inaccurate observers
that the seventh wave of the sea is of special bulk
and volume. —Nature note.
It was a slightly peevish night.
The moon was in the sky,
But all the brightest stars in sight
Were just a hint awry.
A cloud or two with measured tread
Paced like policemen overhead.
This sort of night, as. all men know,
Disgusts you at the start,
Then sets its merry pipes to blow
And tangles up your heart
And tries your fretful wits to craze
In all sorts of delightful ways.
The moon sent Elsie down a wire
To meet my soul at eight.
The eastern wave was all afire,
The surf was out-of-date;
So cold the heavy water felt
It seemed to shiver in its pelt.
I saw her standing all alone,
Star-mazed beside the sea.
I really rather liked the tone
I n which she spoke to me;
She said the Seventh Wave had not
Until that moment reached the spot.
“Dear girl,” I said, “the Seventh Wave
Is sleeping in its bed
Deep in some silent ocean cave
With weeds about its head.
I know it well; it does not care
On such cold nights to take the air.”
She smiled a very pleasant smile,
Her laughter rippled free;
Her eyes were brown and free from guile—
The color of strong tea —
Her soft chin set in whitest fur
Strangely attracted me to her.
I said: “Although that wave may sleep,
We both are wide awake;
And here beside the shining deep
A long salt breath to take
In fine companionship is good
For people in our soulful mood.”
I took her arm. We wandered long
Upon the wrinkled sand.
Her heart appeared attuned to song
And she could understand
How mine a happy beat might miss
Each time our dreams provoked a kiss.
So things were going very well
And all the night was kind.
One star that we were watching fell
And did not seem to mind;
And one, that seemed remote and dry,
Winked at us with a heavenly eve.
“Young star,” I said, “it is not meet
That you should mock our bliss!
Go seek the moon on shining feet
And if she deigns to kiss
Your cynic mouth I do not think
I shall be rude enough to wink.”
But Elsie softly only said:
“Don’t worry over him.
His taste is bad. The life he led
On earth has made him dim.
He sees the whole bright atmosphere
In terms of ribaldry and beer.”
1 kissed her for her gentle thought
Twice on her happy lips.
She leant to me as white sails caught
By winds that blow tall ships
hen from the isles of tropic spice
They carry chocolate and rice.
Just then the star above went out,
A wind began to blow,
And little wavelets seemed to sprout
Out of the undertow.
We little thought that wicked star
Had started earthward in his car.
We wandered on a mile or two;
The time went lightly by.
The song of ocean swelled and grew,
White foam began to fly.
I said, “That Seventh Wave, my dear,
Is likely shortly to appear.”
We watched for him, but ere he came
A gentleman we saw
All clothc-d from head to foot in flame,
Whose set and angry jaw
As:ured us beyond hint of doubt
He was the star we talked about.
“I do not like him,” Elsie said,
And I must here admit
He filled me with a sort of dread,
Though I dissembled it.
“Don't worry, dear,” I said, “I know
These devils are all sparks and show.”
But fiercely down the sand he strode
And we with haste withdrew.*
The flames within his whiskers glowed,
His eyes were brimstone blue;
The rising sea began to roar
And we retreated more and more.
Along the very edge we went
Where all the sand was wet
And every roller, whitely spent,
Rushed back with cold regret.
The fiend that was a star came on
And Elsie screamed when hope seemed gone.
Before us stood that awful thing:
The deep sea rolled behind....
Then flames about us seemed to spring,
The hot smoke made us blind ;
If we retreated further yet
Our feet were certain to get wet.
Then suddenly the sea upheaved
A mighty crest of white
(This thing will scarcely be believed)
And Elsie gripped me tight—
The Seventh Wave with shout and yell
On us and on that devil fell!
It put him out. Our laughter free
Was heard-amid the splash.
Like corks upon the angry sea
Our souls were soon awash.
The waters tossed us to and fro
And drowned us in the undertow.
Beneath the wet, bone clasped in bone,
We lie where pearls lie deep
And simple fishes of no tone
Glide o’er us as we sleep.
My Elsie since we put to sea
Is really very fond of me.
But when the winds are soft and low
And all the deep is kind,
The placid weeds that round us grow
Shift like a window blind ;
And looking to the heavens afar
W e note one spot where shines no star.
N.S.W.

“G. Mac.” : I worked for some years in a
big factory at Yarraville (Vic.) operating a bagging
and weighing machine, and saw some artists with
the needle. I and my sewer usually kept an hourly
gait of 10 tons, or 120 bags. Had a friendly go with
him once for half an hour. The machine won with
seven unsewn bags, but as the register showed 102
bags for the 30 minutes there wasn’t any disgrace
in the defeat. His best in my time was 1400 ewt.-
bags (no tags) in 8J hours, a small boy assisting
by threading his needles. This sleight of hand
artist drew 1 Old. per hour, and when a kindly Wage
Board weighed out another three ha’pence, he was,
for economy’s sake, superseded by two boys at 7 Id.
each, because, so said the foreman, the firm wouldn't
pay any sewer a whole bob.

“Tibia”: The pigs that scarified their
throats while swimming must have been common
pigs without a telephone number or a day at home.
The prize porker has an instinctive knowledge of
deportment and swims as if she (or he) had learnt
it at a business college. Some years ago the river
that runs beside a Victorian country showground
rose rapidly and flooded out the exhibits. Some
aristocratic porkers had to swim for it, and did it
very successfully, landing without a scratch, although
each of them had a swollen throat like that of the
tenor who has been overfed on cream puffs and
praise in a thousand drawing-rooms.

“Murrabit”: “Te F.” (B. 20/4/TG) states
a fact when he compares whale-flesh to good quality
beef-steak. Even the flesh of any part if soaked in
vinegar and water for several hours has an excellent
flavor. On the Japanese stations the entire carcases
are most profitably disposed of for food, and in
Norway a considerable amount of whale-meat is
utilised by the butchers. It is usually salted.
“Homo” : This is, briefly, the Vic. Board
of Health’s recipe for snakebite, and I haven’t seen
anything in the Abo. chronicles that seems better
designed to thwart the undertaker :
(1) Ligature. (2) Cut out. (3) Antidote (per-
mang. potass.). (4) Warmth—hot coffee, smelling-
salts, artificial respiration.
Ligature.-—When bite is on any part of a limb
apply between heart and bite, near bite, tight enough
to stop circulation at the part.
Cutting out. —Pinch up skin at bite and cut out
pinched-up part with one clean sweep. If impractic-
able to cut out bitten part, make small incisions
across and around bite, and (unless bleeding very
freely) suck (if no antidote available).
Antidote.—Powdered permanganate of potassium
rubbed well in. Repeat at short intervals. Encourage
bleeding by rubbing limb downwards (away from
heart) and squeezing wound with fingers.
If an antidote has been applied, remove or loosen
ligature 15 minutes after cutting out. When no anti-
dote has been used, after 15 minutes loosen the liga-
ture for about five minutes and then re-apply. On no
account leave the ligature more than 45 minutes with-

“8.8. T.”: For the benefit of “A.G.It.” (B.
30/3/’IG), who asks about weevils, 1 have trans-
lated from “Les Ravageurs,” by J. 11. Fabre, some-
thing about the wheat weevil, which I conclude will
be his barley pest. Fabre
explains that the insect
with its pointed nose cuts
the skin of a grain and
therein deposits an egg,
which she fixes by means
of a. viscous humor. Then
she passes on to other
grains, which she treats in
the same manner, until
her store of eggs is used
up. When the eggs hatch
the iiny worm pierces into
the farinaceous portion by
an almost invisible hole.
There it makes a hog of
itself. In five or six
weeks the flour is finished,
but the husk remains, and
within it the larva becomes
the perfect insect. It
then leaves the husk and
goes on the wallaby to
explore the pile of grain,
deposit eggs and supply a
fresh population - of ravag-
ers. Fabre mentions a
remedy which seems hardly
practicable for large quan-
tities of grain. Fill large
barrels with the affected
seed, and to every ton of
cereal add about a pint of
sulphuret of carbon. Fasten
up, roll about to make sure
that the liquid penetrates
throughout, and leave for
24 hours. When the cask
is opened it will be found
that weevils, larvae, nymphs
and eggs are all destroyed.
The grain, which should
then be exposed to the air
and agitated, will not
suffer in any way and the
odor will pass almost immediately.
* * * *
Talk of old pubs, and their queer names
sets “The Oldest Inhabitant” going:—
The Baldfaced Stag was on the Parramatta-road;
The Crown and Kettle at the corner of Barrack-lane
and York-street, with a Free and Easy and The Three
Jolly Settlers out Petersham way. The Punch Bowl
was in Cambridge-street, and the Red Cow at Parra-
matta. The first bowling-green in Australia was
formed at The Woolpack, in Parramatta. The Dun
Cow was at Bathurst. The Golden Fiddle, in Wool-
Joomooloo, was kept by a well-known professional
violinist. Speed the Plough was afterwards The,
Plough Inn on Parramatta-road. The Spinning Wheel
was in what is now George-street West. The Hole in the
Wall was in the city, and the Little House Under the
Hill in Judge-street, Woolloomooloo. The Black Dog,
on the Rocks, was built by Sam Terry. Help Me
through the World had the repute of housing an
expert banknote forger, sent out as such. He is
alleged to have followed the craft here and sent the
flimsies to England. And on Brickfield Hill some
years ago was The Honest Lawyer, kept by Daniel
O’Connell.

“Spraggs” : Bill told me there was a
bonzer bees-nest in a big gum-tree, on the creek,
about five miles out. So we got a couple of axes and
walked out to chop the weed down. Bill said he’d
been watching the hive at work for a over a week, and
had noticed the bees in swarms around the hollow.
He thought it would have been the proper thing to
have brought out a 400 gal. tank. We were half a
day chopping the tree down, and, ’strewth ! the bees
were blowflies, and the hollow where thev were buz-
zing contained a very dead ’possum. Bill was
slightly put out about it.

“Had Australian” : Long-distance rides and
endurance tests—bueno 1 Away back in 1906 I was
camped at a little oasis called Toconao, i n the
Desert of Atacama, just near where the frontiers of
Bolivia, Chile and Argentina meet, when I received
a “bush wire” that I was wanted in the worst kind
of hurry at Calama, on the Antofagasta-Bolivia rail-
road, which was (and still is) 130 kilometres awav
Half an hour later, at 7.30 a.m., my rec.ado. which
is saddle and “nap” in one, was cinched on one of
my toughest saddle-mules, and I was off at a sobre
pa-io (amble), a favorite gait on the Great Table-
land, over a “road” that was mostly alkali plain and
saltpan, to San Pedro de Atacama, 37 kilometres
where I rested and fed and watered my mule before
making a bee-line for the railroad, 93 kilometres
across the most awful, barren, waterless stretch of
stone and gravel'in South America, a howling desert
of shimmering heat-waves and fantastic mirage by
day and almost Arctic cold by night. The exces-
sively cold nights are due to the altitude— 10,000 ft.
above sea level. About 3 p.m. I rested in the
shadow of the mule for 15 minutes while I ate a
handful of charqui and parched maize and took
another pull at my water-bag, but there was neither
food nor drink for the vehicle. Two hours after
sundown the temperature was down about zero, and
at 10 p.m., when I reached the railroad, I was “all
in” and as cold as a seal on an icefloe; but the
mule was as chipper as a spring chicken, and to all
appearances good for 25 or 30 kilometres more.
After stripping him and turning him loose, I had
two words with the man I had come to see, got out-
side a jorum of hot rum, and turned in all standing.
I woke about sundown next day, and from what I
A FAR-SIGHTED NEPHEW.
“You might let me have the car for an hour.
Uncle.”
“All right, my hoy, you may have it.”
“And, I say, can you let me have the price
of a couple of fines or so?”
learned later the mule had got back to Toconao
about the same time. Now I don't claim this as a
long-distance performance to skite about, but I do
claim it to be something as an endurance test, con-
sidering that it happened about the same parallel as
Rockhampton, at an altitude varjing from 7000 ft. to
10,000 ft. above sea-level, over a stony, waterless
desert, under temperatures varying from lOOdeg. down
to zero in a few hours, and that my riding weight in
full marching order was a shade under 15 stone.
But the mule is born to endure, otherwise the Lord
would never have given him over to the tender
mercies of the half-breed Indian. I forgot to men-
tion that 130 kilometres equals approx. 81 miles.
FORETHOUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Truscott was a youngish man
He bought Bright’s farm, and straight began
The work of following the plough,
And studying the cult of cow.
'the land was cleared, there grew no gum,
But Truscott thought of planting some ;
One sapling he with proper pride
Set in his acres long and wide.
Bill watched his single redgum grow,
And saw its honeyed blossom blow.
The years slipped by. At length stood he
An old man ’neath an old, old tree.
One day as Truscott tottered round
His splendid stretch of pasture ground,
His white bull shook the firmament,
And went for Bill on murder bent.
Bill ran, but age and gouty feet
Had hobbled him ; he was not fleet,
Yet up the gum contrived to pull
One inch ahead of Prince the bull.
As Truscott sat upon a limb,
There came a curious thought to him:
Remembering he'd set that tree,
“There’s forethought, if you like!” said he.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

“Harrison O.” : “Here Come the Married
Men,” sung—or, rather, noised abroad —by
'Connie Ediss, is easily the biggest hit in “So
Long, Letty.” Why? Certainly the ditty has
a swing and Connie gets the last ounce out
of it, but I suspect the real reason is that it
tickles the vanity of the tame and bald by
encouraging them to regard themselves as
devils of fellows. “If the married men,
throughout their married lives, were as true
to their country as they are to their wives—
Heaven help the country then !” sings Connie,
and every gardening housefather roars with
laughter and glows with satisfaction at the
thought of his purely mythical wickedness.
In such matters condemnation is the sincerest
form of flattery.
“Avon” : I have just been walking through
the freezing works of Recollection & Co.
picking out the best Australian impersona-
tions of a handful of Shakspearian parts.
Here they are, and please note how well the
old-timers keep their end up: Caliban,
Rignold; Fauleonbridge, Sullivan; Hamlet,
Montgomery; Falstaff, Asche; Henry V.,
George Rignold; Lear. W. E. Sheridan;
Macbeth and Romeo, Never a Great One;
Mark Antony. Miln; Lady Macbeth, Gene-
vieve Ward ; Malvolio, Titheradge ; Bottom,
Jefferson; Othello and lago, Brooke; Rich-
ard 111.,. Sullivan; Rosalind, Nora Kerin;
Shylock, Wilkie, barring a few stupidities.
“Masetto”: How these mummers do love
one another was shown in the arrangements
for the special performance of “Julius
Caesar” at the Sliakspeare tercentenary, at
Drury Lane. The original committee, scent-
ing jealousy, handed over the cast to a sub-
committee of leading actors, and the latter
behaved just like an amateur theatrical club.
Sir Herb Tree cannily escaped to a Shaks-
peare festival in New York, and so dodged
the discussion. As a tribute to the success
that Asche has made of the bard’s plays in
the West End he was given considerable
authority, but eventually stepped out, and
at the same time selected for himself the
small part of Casca —not because he wanted
it. but because he thought no one else would.
The struggle was fiercest around the part of
Caesar, which was eventually secured by F.
R. Benson ; Bourchier was put on to play
Brutus; and Henry Ainley, who did not
press his claims, got Marc Antony.
“8.J.”; “The First Mrs. Tanqueray” is
the title of the film version of Pinero’s play.
A good deal of attention is given to the lady
whom Cayley Drummle spoke .of as "all
marble arms and black velvet,” and the
glimpses of Aubrey’s life with her are said
to make him seem a more reasonable charac-
ter than in the play—which, Heaven knows,
is needed! The film also shows Aubrey s
first meeting with Paula in a theatre.
Pinero is alleged to have bestowed his appro-
val on the affair.
“R.” : Saw a movie of the “Stingaree” series
the other day. All it proves is that America
must be hard up for film subjects when it
takes IJornung’s impossible “pommy” and
makes of him an Australian bushranger,
clean shaven, dapper as a draper, and eye-
glassed as of Piccadilly. “Stingaree” was
never Australian, and that’s why he appealed
to the English, who like the sham in notion
and are frightened of the real. Similarly
the un.Australian Captain Swift of Hadden
Chambers, who never knew Australia, pleased
them by reason of its unreality. The “Stin-
garee” picture nevertheless gives the story
some slight actuality, whereas in the book
it had none; and even this bogus thing m
such a realistic medium as the picture-show
proves that the real Australian story wil
give the world a new thrill. It will have o
be done, of course, in Australia, which P er "
mits of the finest photography on earth, ant
for the reason that it will be produced m
this despised country it will need to have
better construction and production than an
imported film —for Australian audiences a
least. The other countries of the world wu
be easier to please, because the Australian
picture will come to them from afar, and tnai
which comes from afar may lie boldly.
