“S.S.” : I thought I ought to mention it.
Everywhere else Monday is Monday, but at
our boarding-house Monday’s Stewsday.
* * * *
“Snell” : These are hard times for beggars
—ordinary beggars or city solicitors, I mean.
The blind, the halt and the lame rattle their
tins to a deaf generation, and the old-estab-
lished mendicants are turned empty away.
A Collins-street doctor tells me as a positive
fact that he was interviewed recently by a
derelict with only one eye and one leg—he
had lost the others in a blasting accident
many years ago. “See here, doctor,” he said,
“I want to know what’s your very lowest
for takin’ off an arm.” “What’s the matter
with your arm?” asked the M.L). “Nothin’,
but I want to have it off. I’m gettin’ my
livin’ beggin’, and I find the people want a
of a lot for their money nowadays.”
“Dry Shave” : This in a country that pro-
duces over 20 millions’ worth of wool a
year. I went into one of Melbourne’s big-
gest rag warehouses under orders to get some
woollen undervests for the missus. They
hadn’t seen a woollen undervest for months
—had a nice line of cotton, though. I was
off them and sought pyjamas for myself.
The shopman hadn’t seen woollen pyjamas
for years, but he had a nice line of flannel-
ette (all cotton) machined up in Australia.
I was off them, too, preferring to sleep in
my dungarees until someone starts to think
about making Crimean shirting and woollen
yarn in sufficient quantities to clothe me and
"the old dutch. I got a shock, incidentally,
to learn that the rare Australian manufac-
turer is under no obligation to mark on Ins
goods whether they are wool, cotton or just
tripe. Of course this suits the dishonest
retailer splendidly.
“E.M.W.”: My experience in the matter
of Australian-made leg gloves is similar to
that of “Billjim’s Sister” (B. 27/4/’l6). As
usual for the past four years I inquired for
home-made woollen stockings at a leading
Melbourne drapery store, but was told that
the Government had commandeered the mills
and Australian stockings were off. I there-
fore bought English-made at 2s. Gd., and they
showed very thin places after one day’s wear.
However, I am now in possession of some
which are manufactured in the Common-
wealth and sold at 2s. 3d. a pair. They are
the finest quality I have ever encountered at
any price, soft, thick, well-shaped and seam-
less, extra long, and of good, durable color.
There is another quality at Is. 6d., the only
perceptible, fault of these being that they are
somewhat “prickly.”
* * * *
The Aberdeen clipper Pericles, built in
1877, and in her day equal to the best sailing
ships trading to Australia, has in her _ old
age received a charter to a South American
port at 140 s. a ton, and is looked on as a
first-class insurance risk. Still the old ves-
sel —now a Norwegian—is a mere chicken
compared with two barques built in 1760,
which appear to have been found in some
Scandinavian marine graveyard and had
their old bones greased for the raw, salt
track again.
* * * *
“Ngatik”: We repatriated about 220
Caroliners and then set out in the Pukaki
to get 200 new boys. No luck resulted until
Ponape was reached. Here the boys literally
fought with one another to sign on. They
reckoned the Japs were not giving them a
fair deal. On January 28, when 15 miles
from Kusaie, the tail-shaft carried away.
Over 300 people were aboard, and during
the 43 days of drifting, hordes of sharks
were on the lookout for us. When we were
400 miles from Rabaul a Jap steamer came
alongside and offered to tow us to that port
for £15,000; but our skipper said he didn’t
have that much on him just then. So the
engineers went on amusing themselves by
fitting a new shaft. They did not mind
working up to their necks in water, with
the sharks nosing around, but reckoned it a
bit hot when five-eighths of an inch had to
be taken off the end of the new shaft before
the propeller would fit. However, they
accomplished it by chipping with cold
chisels. There were still a couple of days’
food left when we arrived at Rabaul, and
the language of the sharks was something
impious.
* * * *
“Avon” ; Maoriland breeders of high-class
jumbucks who competed at the ’Frisco Exhi-
bition are paralysed at the way they have
been treated. Originally it was announced
that gold medals would be awarded to win-
ners, but after the M.L. wool kings had
bagged a lot of the prizes they were informed
through Unfcle Sam’s nose that the baubles
would be of bronze. The infuriated merino
monarchs promptly leapt roofward with loud
protests and Sam relented. He said that
anybody who had won a medal could have
it gilded, if he preferred it that way, at his
own expense. When the mail left restora-
tion of some speechless exhibitors was still
being tried.
“F.N.T.”: In Australia the making of
basketware, cane-chairs, brooms and brushes
has provided a permanent but rather pinched
income for the blind. It is not exclusively
a blind people’s industry, for Private Enter-
prise also makes these articles, while a
whole lot are imported. Still, the asylum-
product has a reputation for being an ex-
tremely sound article: Melbourne “blind”
millet-brooms are unapproachable; and the
hair and bristle brooms and basketware are,
value for value, ahead of the sea-borne
goods. Yet the blind worker gets a poor
wage and his goods are little known. If
Australia were to bar all American and
other imported products in these lines the
Australian housekeeper would build up a
solid business on a very sound basis —the
basks of quality for which the asylum-people
are mainly responsible—and there would be
a better wage for the blind, with no need
to pay invalid-pensions to sightless broom-
makers, while the returned soldier who has
lost his feet or a leg could have a cut in,
too. It is only a small industry at present;
yet it could be an immense one —for
the most down-at-heel house in Australia
has some sort of a broom kicking about the
premises, even if its only use is to beat the
dog with.
* * * *
A small factory is being built at Sunshine
(Vic.), where Harvester McKay flourishes,
for the manufacture of chinaware. The clay
will be brought from Bacchus Marsh, where
there is a supply pronounced to be equal to
anything our European friends are shipping
to us. Good Australians will wish the young
industry the best of luck and an uncracked
future.
“Moana” : The most astonishing ship deal
on record is that connected with the steamer
Hoyland—a vessel of only 2996 tons. Her
original cost in 1898 was £30,060 and she
was sold in 1910 for £15,000. The other day
her owners agreed to part with her for
£117,000 —nearly four times what she cost
when new 18 years ago. More remarkable
still, she was immediately grabbed on charter
for 12 months at £9300 per month! At the
end of a year, her present owners will practi-
cally have received her recent cost in the form
of hire-money, and—if the submarines miss—
will still have the ship. Lord only knows what
the old ruin won’t be worth by then. In 1930
or so the last rusted rib of her will probably
be handed over in exchange for the Atlantic.
I wish I had saved my money when young
and bought a masthead lamp or a binnacle
or something of that sort.
* * * *
Melbourne will never again ride a horse-
’bus home from work —not as a regular
thing, anyway. The East Brunswick ’bus,
the last of its kind, has gone out of
business for good, owing to the building of
an electric line, and the Melbourne Tram
and Omnibus Co. no longer lives up to its
old-fashioned name.
* * * *
“Taffy” on Martha Washington’s griev-
ance :
“Longy’s” nibful of scorn re the payment of
country school-cleaners (B. . 27/4/’l6) encourages
me to put down my broom. Three years ago the
cleaners were receiving something like 7cl. per hour
for a week of 15 or 16 hours, and out of that they
had to provide their own brooms, buckets, etc. After
much writing and many appeals to the Minister, the
rate was raised to lOd. per hour. Subsequently the
Department had a spasm of generosity and decided
to provide the cleaners with tools and utensils.
Forthwith a most wonderful and wasteful kit of
brooms, buckets, mops, rakes, dustpans, shovels,
chamois leathers, wir.dow-dusters, etc., were deposited
at each school. Only one or two of the most
useful of the collection were kept out; the rest were
shoved into any old cupboard, and the schools were
cleaned without frills as before. Now the Depart-
ment has had a reverse spasm and tacked up new
rules entailing a lot of hard and unnecessary work
while cuttting down the wage from 12s. 6d. or 13s.
4d. per week to 9s. 4d. per month. If somebody has
to pay for the Holman Government’s extravagance
it’s not going to be me. Thank Heaven, I’ve still
enough beauty left to go on the stage.
* * * *
The South Aus. wheat crop is now offi-
cially announced to be a record—34 million
bushels against the previous best (in
11K)9-10) of 25. The harvest is dazzling
when viewed against the background of last
year’s failure: average 124 bushels to the
acre, against 14 ; total 34 millions, against
34. Yet the State had on the whole rather
a dry year in 1915; but all the rain there
was came from April to September, just
when wheat needs it.
* * * *
“J.G.” : I’m laying the odds that the most-
sought-for timber in Australia for cabinet-
making and such fancy work will be wild
cherry. The tree somewhat resembles a
cypress in foliage, and it produces the
prettiest-veined and -figured wood that I
know of. It is superior even to the best
fiddle-backed blackwood. It is much lighter
in color than blackwood, and will polish up
beautifully.
“Moana”: Somebody was asking recently
whether a new sea luxury canned in U.S.A.
was clam or the giant mussel of Maoriland.
It is toheroa, a large shell-fish found in
great quantities near the Kaipara Heads
(M.L.). One Skipwitli, who has been ex-
perimentally canning it, recently rushed away
to Wilson’s country with a quantity, and was
received by epicures with howls of delight.
Toheroa is one of the daintiest seafoods ob-
tainable, and it is expected that collecting it
and exporting it to tickle foreign palates will
soon be a flourishing industry in Loan Land.
For years small quantities have been tinned
by a M.L. firm, but for a very limited market.
Those who managed to get an occasional tin,
particularly in Australia, guarded it with an
automatic mantrap until they had swallowed
the contents and chewed the label.
THE BLESSED VEGETABLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From vegetables we can obtain all the alcohol we
need. Potatoes give the best results. It would pay
to grow them, not as food, but specially for the
manufacture of alcohol. — Melbourne paper.
What gleams is not invariably gold ;
And he whose eyes are red, whose footsteps swerve,
May not have touched a drink. So censure hold
In strict reserve.
Although ’twould seem an easy thing to guess
That such a citizen was on his ear,
He might have merely eaten to excess
The spuds that cheer.
A vegetarian saint may sometimes reel,
E’en though he ne’er the sinful pint-pot drains ;
He puts a carrot in his mouth to steal
Away his brains!
Look not upon the beetroot when it’s red,
For it may prove a mocker worse than wine.
And if you would avoid a drunkard’s head,
Leeks you’ll decline!
So roysterers will set choice spuds in rows ;
In growing alcohol no time they’ll lose;
And home they’ll rush each eve, and with a hose
Water their booze!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
HIS DISCUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On one occasion he divested himself of his minis-
terial attire and went to a play, which absolutely
disgusted him. — A saint at an Assembly.
They said the play had most delightful thrills,
Smart turns that almost took one's breath away.
The very name upon the hoarding bills
Suggested things no play-bill ought to say—
Things so disgusting, intimate and sweet,
I felt I really had to book a seat.
The Devil, always stalking in this town,
’Twere vain to meet in frontal, bold attack ;
It wants a lot of skill to get him down,
So I arranged this thing behind his back.
It was a play—Lord, keep thy servant meek! —
In which temptation seemed to simply reek.
The theatre I bar; yet here I must
Get a look in before the season’s close.
So full it was of scenes that would disgust,
And awful sin, as I could well suppose.
I put away my black and holy coat
And went in mufti for a great, glad gloat.
The play exceeded expectations quite.
Choicely indelicate I found it all.
1 never can remember such a night;
Spell-bound I watched to the last curtain’s fall.
Oh, wicked far beyond my power to tell—
-1 left the place as one who fled from Hell!
N.B.W.
GEORGE STREET.

BUSINESS AS USUAL.
The Management of the “Australia”
Hotel are gratified to state that, not-
withstanding the upset caused by the
recent tire they are able to announce
“business as usual.” The service in
the Grand Dining Room, on the
Balcony, and in the Winter Garden
is being conducted in the same style
that has made the “Australia” Hotel
so popular with its patrons, while the
arrangements for Private Dinners and
Suppers are working with their accus-
tomed satisfaction.
Throughout the holiday season
arrangements for Afternoon Teas will,
as usual, be carried out in the Winter
Garden, which has been running con-
tinuously throughout.

POLITICAL
POINTS
After the recent Trades Conference at
.Melbourne had damned compulsory military
service and announced that voluntaryism
would give equally good results, it called
loudly for an increase in the tariff “to keep
out the enemy’s goods.” But why use com-
pulsion when voluntaryism will give equally
good results? In keeping out the imported hat
why not allow our dislike of the exotic lid
to find voluntary expression? The distress-
ing fact is that, in the opinion of a demoral-
ised Labor conference, compulsion should be
applied to the protection of industry, but
not to the protection of life and liberty,
those things being of less consequence.
“The most unkindest Kut of all.”
— Shakspcarc.
Did the British Government want an out-
break in Ireland? How otherwise explain
the utter disregard of the flood of newspaper
warnings. Here’s a typical hulloa from
London Referee (12/3/T6) :
Are we squandering the staying power of the
Empire in Ireland by ignoring treason and the
growing influence of the Kaiser’s Irish secretary, Sir
Roger Casement?
Originally negligible, the Sinn Fein party is now
well organised, and is growing in County Cork at
an alarming rate. The party’s mission is not only
anti-English but pro-German.
The leaders of the movement should be imprisoned
for the term of the war, and' the nest of Sinn
Feiners near the “Seven Heads” interned. Sinn
Feiners are not playing for notoriety. Their
treason is earnest, hut they are not like the old
patriots of ’9B. Their one thought is money-
making, and their promise of untold wealth and
prosperity “when the Germans come” has caught on.
It would appear possible to find a proper
job for a firing squad on the eastern side of
the Irish Sea.
During the Leg. Council elections in
Westralia the other day one of the argu-
ments used to induce the electors to vote in
a particular direction consisted of a mass
of figures relating to the trading and busi-
ness enterprises carried on by the
State. Every line was a fraud.
There was a statement of revenue
and expenditure in each case: but
no mention was made of interest,
depreciation, renewals or sinking
fund, and bad as the figures look
without such charges they would
look fit for the morgue with them.
This sort of thing, the direct result
of party government, is flagrantly
dishonest. Either the administra-
tion can be defended with honest
figures or it cannot be. In any
case the public is entitled to a fair
statement on the subject. When
the alleged friends of public enterprise mon-
key with the facts, they immediately come
within the category of its enemies.
This paper will have doubts presently
whether Billy Kaiser hasn’t actually done
more than Billy Hughes for Australian in-
dustry. The latest development for which
the German William is responsible is hap-
pening on the Parramatta, where James
llardie and Co., hitherto known as importers
of fibro-cement slates and sheets, have
secured land for a factory wherein a sub-
sidiary co. will make goods of the same kind.
The new co.—which has got the Treasurer’s
blessing—intends .to make asbestos slates as
well. But while the German William has
thus set’ the new business going, it is out of
bis power to keep it going. That’s where the
Wales-always-best William should come in.
Unhappily in regard to Australian tariff
reform the Welsh William has never shown
any disposition to come in with enthusiasm.
The price of the bait under the voluntary
system. At its last meeting Brunswick
(Vic.) Council talked with misgiving of the
expense of the present ulan of recruiting,
but nothing was done. The only motion,
which suggested asking Pearce for a refund
of the money spent, lapsed for want of a
seconder. Still, the fact was brought out
that each recruit costs Brunswick 255. or
30s. before he even began to be a soldier.
Davies, the Commonwealth’s new Director
of Navigation, has told Tasmania to get to
work and build iron ships. He points out
that it has the Blythe iron deposit, one of
the richest in the world, along with cheap
hydro-electric power and more deep-water
facilities than it knows what to do with.
But he forgot something. It also has boom
conditions, the result of a borrowing
orgy, and a large capacity for knock-
ing off work frequently, and not do-
ing a fair thing between times. A
Director of Navigation who takes
the plus factors into account and neglecis
all minus quantities is likely to arrive at
some results more amazing than helpful.
A delegate at the recent N.S.W. Shires'
Conference put into shape a notion held
by a lot of people in a more-or-less vague
fashion, by moving that eligible single men
who don’t enlist should be barred from the
Parliamentary and Local Government fran-
chise. After "some talk the motion was with-
drawn, which was just as well, because the
idea is all wrong. It proposes to force men
by indirect means to do something which
the Law tells them directly they can please
themselves about. If voluntaryism is the
correct thing it shouldn’t be sandbagged
in the rear; if it isn’t the correct thing it
should be punched in honest, straight-for-
ward fashion on the nose.
Otto Sachse, who is asking for re-elec-
tion to the Vic. Leg. Council, is being
opposed by the persistent Frederick Daw-
born, who in 1908 very nearly pushed
George Swinburne out of Hawthorn.
In his daily appeal Sachse refers to his
“strenuous recruiting campaigns, the wel-
fare of my native land Australia, and the
best interests of our great Empire.” Daw-
born, in his brief four-line counterblast, asks
the North-Eastern Province to “vote for
Dawborn, Victorian native J_Britisher].”
Sachse is unfortunate in having a name
which isn’t Australian. Still, the other
man’s attempt to suggest that he is the bet-
ter Britisher doesn’t prove him the better
Liberal. For instance, Sachse has shown
himself a firm believer in an importing
policy, with no bigoted leanings towards
British imports. There was, c.g., that little
matter of the proposed purchase of German
pianos for Victorian State schools. What
Dawborn should do, instead of attempting
to get ahead of Sachse on the grounds of
his ancestry, is to show that he wouldn’t
support an importing policy, but would
battle for a few of the urgent reforms advo-
cated by advanced Liberalism, which he
claims to represent, in the days when Lib-
eralism stood for something.
FRIGHTFULNESS IN THE NURSERY.
THE FAMILY DOCTOR ( manipulating the
doctored dose): “Well, well, you are
a funny hoy—why this sudden dislike
for jam?”
THE FAMILY TERROR: “ 'Cos I b’lieve it’s
mined!”
State secondary education in Tasmania
doesn’t promise, as far as it has gone, to
relieve the curse of congestion or keep the
numerous middleman from getting thicker
on the ground. Of the 127 boys who left
the State High Schools last year 45 joined
the collar and cuffs brigade by taking up
“commercial pursuits,” 29 started to learn
a trade, 10 got a Government job, six went
into the military service, and only nine took
to farming.
An Adelaide magistrate has knocked a big
lmle in the new Licensing Act, which pre-
scribes six o’clock closing, by simply punch-
jng il with tlie words “for profit” in clause
]34 ll.' “cannot understand what the
legislature meant” by putting them in;
hut there they are. So the police witness
who gets whisky at a haiushop has to show
whether there really was any profit on the
deal; and the odds are he hasn’t the know-
lodge to do it. Tlie section says “sells
or supplies for profit,” and the question
seems to be whether “for profit" has to he
TOUGH.
ENRAGED DINER : “Waiter—a piece of paper—if you please."
WAITER: “A piece of paper?”
ENRAGED DINER: "Yes, sir: a piece of paper, to wrap up this steak in — I wish to take it
home and have my hoots soled.”
applied to both verbs or only to the second
one. If the Act said “sells, or supplies for-
profit,” there wouldn’t be any doubt about
it —the man would be an offender. But the
Law has a stupid aversion to commas and
other marks of punctuation ; hence this ridi-
culous muddle.
Now things will be all right! B. It.
Wise, N.S.W.’s Agent-General in London,
has, so the cable says, secured new offices,
with “two large windows facing Trafalgar
Square.” Think of it —two large windows!
In future the Ma State’s apple will have
ample space to turn the other cheek, and its
spud will be in a position to throw a glad
eye at the late Admiral Nelson. Incidentally,
the Commonwealth’s fine new building—Aus-
tralia House —will soon be looking for
tenants. But, of course, it would never do
for the “oldest State” to have the Common-
wealth as its landlord. What a farce it all
is ! And what expensive humbug!
The War Economy Championship lias been
won by the Hobart City Council, which has
decided to save £4OO a year by collecting
the garbage once a week, instead of daily.
But why once a week V Why stir up the
contentious dustbin at all while the enemy
is at the gate?
“•T.F.H.” reminds us of,an old visitor in
a corded velvet coat: —-
Billy Hughes has found out a thing or two since
he arrived here in England. Among his most humor-
ous discoveries is that he owes all to Ben Tillett.
That dreadful little posturing person tells London
Daily Chronicle that when he went to Australia in
1897 he found Andrew Fisher “secretary of a small
and struggling miners’ union at Gympie,” and Billy
“managing a waterside workers’'union at Sydney.”
Ben addressed meetings under the auspices of both,
and the burden of his speeches was the advantage
the Australian workers possessed in their democratic
system of government. “I urged them to make use
of these advantages by sending their own representa-
tives to run the legislative machine. They were apt
pupils.” So you see Ben Tillett is the Father of the
Australian Labor party and the political progenitor
of the Prime Minister and the High Commissioner.
Indeed, it isn’t certain that there wouldn’t have been
a ’orrible tragedy but for Benny’s little velvet coat.
When he arrived at Gympie he found Andy “rather
despondent,” but after Andy had listened and looked
he bucked up and “started on the line which led first
to his return to the Queensland Parliament and then
to the Commonwealth Parliament and Premiership.”
The trifling facts that in 1897 Fisher had been mem-
ber for Wide Bar- for four years and that Hughes
had been member for Lang for three years are, of
course, of no consequence whatever.
“Melb lam greatly impressed bv
item in a picture show just seen. \ Vatl
load of uniformed soldiers is on the wav t
vote at an English by-election. There k
tremendous significance in this. The soldie!
vote in England will be up to four million'
at least—and what camaraderie there will
he among them! No danger of the veteran
being neglected as the Balaclava heroes
were. Here in Australia we’ll have at least
a quarter of a million khaki votes, with all
their sisters, cousins and aunts and a host of
cronies.
Cheerful appropriation by Federal Parlia-
ment : Pay of censors and their staffs, etc..
£33.000. But why not be frank about it and
label it “Agency for Suppressing Unpleas-

£33,000” ?
Even while the war is raging there are signs
of weakening amongst the loud patriots
who declared hotly that the trading German
was rubbed off their lists for ever —and left it
at that. A few months ago the S. M.
Herald was the most enthusiastic never-
againer of them all; now, discussing
whether a citizen with £.">000 to spare should
keep it to invest in the next war loan or
use it to start a factory for the manufacture
of goods formerly obtained from Germany,
the S. M. Herald remarked: “When the
enemy is forced to his knees there will be
time enough to make us independent of him
in the matter of trade, if that he desirable.’’

chances of doing so when peace is restored
are poorer than Buckley’s. This paper can
already hear in the distance the argument
of the local foreign-trader: “In these days
of national distress, when the burden
taxation lies heavy upon our shoulders, we
certainly cannot afford to turn down out
late enemies’ offer of cheap goods. I‘ H ’
time to make these sacrifices will be when
we have healed our wounds and prosperity
is restored.”
A NEW VERSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Because we rallied him upon his haste
To hurry home, and leave behind the lights,
The pots of liquor it is good to taste,
And other things that gladden festive nights',
Brown, in fine scorn, this proverb at us hurled:
“The tongue a woman cradles rocks the world.”
And Brown is right, although he did not mean
To tangle up the ancient phrase like that.
When Blog-on comes to town, brim-full of spleen,
To sack his clerks, and kick the harmless oat
That on the office rug is snugly curled.
The tongue a woman cradles rocks our world.
Kings have made wars and statesmen gone astray,
Men rushed to battle glad to seek for peace,
Explorers towards new worlds have sailed away
Because of clacking tongues that would not cease.
Brown was inspired. All discord’s flags unfurled
Yell his remarks about a rocking world.

ANOTHER triumph for Columbia recording! Vladimir
de Pachmann, prince of pianists and greatest exponent
of Chopin ever known, has joined the ever-growing
number of Master Musicians who now record only for
Columbia.
Wonderful, amazing, astounding, these Pachmann Columbia
Records give a new meaning to tne reproduction of pianoforte
music. Here at last is true piano tone, impeccable in purity,
and free from that dull twang which characterised pianoforte
recording till the advent of the new Columbia, process.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Highland Band was a long time making
his pa and ma whinny with pride. The old man
was Tartan, and ma was the imported Playaway,
who once cleared out with a Newmarket Handicap.
What Tartan did is history. The disappointing off-
spring won his first race at Sydney Tattersail’s meet-
ing last Saturday, when he exhibited his tail to a
bunch of moderates. Little Foley, who piloted the
nag, took no risks, and won rather easily. That
boy is a fine jockey, who apparently doesn’t relish
flash finishes, for the same afternoon he gave Lady
Minnie no peace until she had won the Winter Stakes
by five lengths from the Maorilander Toa Tere.
Lady Minnie, a very lanky filly, had always been
going to do something big—according to her track
work. Had she been in a betting stable she would
have cost a mint of money. The young female had
been backed on many previous occasions.
Nombi’s success in Sydney Tattersall’s
Flying Handicap on Saturday was the sixth in that
nag’s career, four of them having been for his owner-
trainer, F. J. Marsden. Before Marsden got him
Nombi belonged to McDonald and Wills-Alien, but
after landing two races for the partners he contracted
the T.M.S. habit —too many seconds. It would be
safe to say that Nombi has been in that deplorable
position over a dozen times. Marsden always
trained the prad and got him for £5OO. Within a
month the brute was first past the post on two
occasions. Any punter who studied Doncaster
Handicap form must have been on Nombi on Satur-
day. In that race Nombi was a good fourth.
Apropos of seconds, it might not be out of place to
casually mention that Royal Laddie was there again
on Saturday.
* * * *
Racehorse-owner Fred Merton before leav-
ing Sydney the other day to catch fish in Queensland,
directed that Aurofodina was to be put into work
again. The old ’un —he is rising 10 —has been
doing stud duty for some time, and the first of his
yearlings appeared in the sale-ring at the recent dis-
tribution. As they did not bring loud prices there
is a suspicion that some of them may turn out
champions. Aurofodina won any number of good
races, but his name will always be associated with
one of the big tragedies of the Australian turf.
The late W. 11. Smith, than whom there was no more
honorable horseman, was riding Aurofodina in the
Rosehill Cup when he received the injuries which
killed him. His mount was running in the centre
of a packed field, when Gracedelle, a one-eyed
mare, crossed her legs and toppled over. Several
other nags hit the ground, including Aurofodina.
That accident was the cause of the introduction of
the A.J.C. rule which prohibits the running of one-
lamp nags in this State. Victoria soon followed
suit. It is only in portions of Queensland that
they are now permitted to compete.
Morven Lass (Brac-ken-Dobine) has been
a little gold-mine on Sydney pony courses lately.
In eight starts, the 14.1 champion had six wins,
with a second under a mountainous list. 31b.
Besides bulging the owner’s wallet, Morven Lass has
given Ikey a lift by upsetting red-hot favorites.
Adelaide Cup meeting was held at Vic-
toria Park, the “old course” on the city park-lands,
with a free Flat; Morphettville, the proper home of
the S.A.J.C., is still in the hands of the military.
The totalisator handled £54,000; which is much
better than last year’s £49,000; but the three years
before that were all £63,000. The Jockey Club
takes 7$ per cent, of the investments, which means
£4OOO towards the stake money ( £6300 odd). The
State Government gets a “sweetener” of £l3OO, and
90 per cent, is divided among backers of winners
and seconds. (Tickets on the second horse receive
one-fourth of the money whenever there are seven or
more starters.) The biggest dividend of the three
days was £23, but comfortable sixes and sevens
were plentiful. As already mentioned, the feature
of the w'eek was the success of imported horses.
Blague won the Goodwood (paying backers £7 65.),
Cyklon the one weight-for-age race, and St. Spasa
the Cup ( £l2 Is.). S.A.’s modest “classic” events
were won by Keily’s Ptami (two-year-old), and
Lynch’s Pretty Bobby (St. Leger) ; P.B. was also
second in the Cup. Tait, a South Australian, was
the only four-figure winner (the Cup was worth
£1200) ; then came “Rawdon” Syme, Lynch, Keily,
Goldhill, Mrs. Hawker, Mostyn Owen, Scobie, Hamil-
ton and Hawker. Harrison (4) was the only man
to ride more than two winners, Lynch (4) the only
one to train more than two, and Bobadil (Blague and
Pretty Bobby) the only sire with more than one.
The A.R.C.’s Birthday Cup meeting, with a similar
programme, follows, on the same ground, almost at
once.
* * * *
The way the totalisator works was well
illustrated in a small race at the Adelaide Cup meet-
ing. In a field of 19, only three were really backed,
others being nibbled at on the chance of a big divi-
dend. (To “make up a ticket” with sporting
friends, and hope for a huge, unknown return, is one
of the pleasures of the tote.) Scotch Peer, one of the
three fancied, dead-heated with Boulogne for second
place, Devron winning easily. Thus three dividends
had to be paid. The 55| people who backed Devron
got £23 each. Boulogne’s 19 supporters got
£ll 3s. each —for a dead-heat second!
Before La Steppe went away from Sydney
to win the Brisbane Thousand last Saturday, her
owner made no secret of his belief in her ability to
succeed. She humped' 9st. 61b. in front all the
way over the seven furlongs, and won well in lmin.
29lsec. As it is a change to see a favorite land a
big stake, La Steppe will probably be very popular
in Bananaland. Just before the last Newmarket
the mare appropriated three races, the last at Rose-
hill a week before the big Flemington dash. The
stable thought she had a capital chance and hurried
her across to Peacock’s capital, but she didn’t hurry
up in the race and had not appeared much in public
since.
* * * *
The well-bred Eighteen Carat, who had
numerous wins to his credit in three States —a
Hobart Cup the most striking of them —has come to
an ignominious end in Adelaide. He fell in a hurdle
race, and, breaking his leg, was shot.
* * * «
Maoriland’s flying machine, Desert Gold,
has finished her three-year-old career, owner Lowry
having decided to give her a spell until the spring.
This season she started in 14 races and arrived home
first every time, which sounds suspiciously like a
world’s record, and collected £B2OO. Including
her two-vear-old winnings of £3765 she has now
earned £11,965.
If Desert Gold had never been born or
had died last year, or had never been taught to
gallop, a nag named Eligible would be enjoying the
limelight as the best three-year-old in the wintry
isles. He has bagged the Trentham Gold Cup, the
M.L. St. Leger, the Auckland Plate, City Handicap
and Summer Cup, the Canterbury Fendalton Handi-
cap, and the Hawkes Bay Cup.
The Westland Racing Club (M.L.) tried
an experiment the other day and had girl clerks at
the tote-receiving windows. If this spreads punters
over yonder will be able to strike a new vein in the
search for luck, backing chestnuts at the blonde
girl’s window, and bays and blacks at the brunette’s.
* * ■* *
A couple of welshing Ikeys were im-
pounded by the police up at Bathurst (N.S.W.) the
other day, mainly because they were in danger of
being beaten on the head by a post or something;
and the local beak passed them three months, remark-
ing that “their actions might have led to bloodshed.’
Of course these guns deserved what they got and
were lucky that the Law had rescued them from
the infuriated punter. But Sydney Telegraph at
once demanded to know 1 “how far a bookmaker who
fails in his business can be held accountable for
the murderous instincts of his clients.” Then it
philosophically reflected that a man unable to pay
an ordinary debt would not be gaoled because his
creditors thirsted for his blood, and wildly asked
why the creditor of a bookmaker should be entitled
to more protection than other men? The answer to
the last question is easy. He is not. The man
who puts up his money to buy the odds from I key
is not in the position of a creditor if Ikey either
disappears over the fence or announces that he cannot
deliver the goods, nor is Ikey in the position of a
debtor. The pur.ter has been robbed. Ikey is the
robber. Talk about “ordinary debts” in such cases
is blithersome. If the Telegraph happened to want
a stimulant some evening and drifted into a pub
with demands for beer, and if Bung, after taking
the cash, remarked that there was nothing doing and
leaped out of the back door, it occurs to this paper
that the editorial yells for blood would bring all
the John Hops in the land to the scene of disaster.
JACK DAVIS (“Not Out”), Editor
Sydney Referee.
“Musketoon”: Most of the aristocrats
seem to be racing at country meetings nowadays.
At the recent Grenfell (N.S.W.) picnic there were
Queen Rice, Queen Milli, Lady Smock, Lady Ganara,
Lord Rice and Silent King, to say nothing of Sir
Sago. There were also some kings about, and they
were not silent. They belonged to the House of
Solomon and took away most of the district’s spare
cash.
Punters who drop money on the ponies
seem to have harder work getting rid of their cash
than the misguided sports who put their trust in
dogs. At the Metrop. (Sydney) coursing flutter held
last week, the favorite for the May Stakes was a
lady who was backed to win outright at five to two.
As this meant winning six consecutive courses the
odds denoted a cheerful optimism which had its reward
in the animal’s defeat in the second effort. The
noble art of puss-chasing is a thrilling business
sometimes for both puss and the punter.
* * * *
Dave Smith arrived in the ring at Syd-
ney Stadium on Saturday after a long absence, and
as his old-time opponent, Jimmy Clabby, was there, the
crowd saw a good rough disturbance and had a fierce
argument afterwards. Clabby abandoned his usual
tactics and carried the battle to close quarters as
often as he got a chance, and there he more than
held his own. Smith for half of the time at any rate
boxed with all his former speed and used his right
at times to good purpose. But as a rule his timing
was poor, with the result that when he landed on
Clabby the door was usually swinging the wrong
way. At the tenth round Smith was leading by a
fair margin. Then the Yank woke up and Smith
began to tire. At the end Scott gave Clabby his
blessing, and the back seats broke into a wild roar
of protest. The contest was one in which any
decision given by the referee would have been
challenged, though perhaps a draw would have
satisfied the majority of spectators. But Scott’s
job is not to look for majority backing; it is to
give his own unbiased judgment, and as far as
the writer of this paragraph reckoned it up Scott
did the correct thing. Weights: Smith, list. 61b.;
Clabby, lOst. 131 b.
* * # *
Harry Stone and Tommy Uren provided
the disturbance at Melbourne Stadium on Saturday
night, and science figured more than bash, to the
great disgust of the gallery, which yearns for some-
thing with a meat axe in it. But there was plenty
of action. The two pugs bobbed, pranced and smote
like cockerels looking for trouble. Uren had the
better of frequent clinches, tickling Stone’s ear
with short, strenuous rights. The Amurkan-Yid-
disher’s left, however, never ceased operations, and
two-handed quick-firing bombardments at the enemy’s
commissariat in the last few rounds turned the
scale in his favor. The referee’s decision brought
great rejoicing to Judah, and milk and honey and
shekels flowed through the land.
It hasn't grown too dark to see the goal
as far as “Joker” Hall is concerned. After a futile
shot he has, as the football reporters used to say,
notched the big point, and hopes to help in compiling
the winning score in the premiership match, Kultur
v. Civilisation. Which is to say that, after having
been once rejected by the recruiting sergeant, “Joker”
has become Private Alexander Hall, and has gone
into camp. Followers of Essendon (Vic. League)
will remember him well as a brilliant player and
subsequently as a coach for Melbourne, St. Kilda and
Williamstown. He has persuaded 14 others to
wear khaki, two of his sons are at the Front, and a
third is munition-making. A brother and two
nephews are also in the ranks.
Western Suburbs’ success in Sydney lawn
tennis premiership saves the bother of shifting the
bauble to another cupboard. It needed a fine
effort to win, and Horace Rice supplied it, wresting
the game from Sydney b"y five rubbers to three.
The season just ended in time for Moran and You-
dale (who played for Wests in the final) to pack up
their rifles for a rubber with the Hun.
Jack Healy, secretary of the Vic. Cricket
Association, who died in his office last week, was
never a great cricketer, but he was a useful club
batsman, a brilliant outfield, and a wicketkeeper of
considerable ability. On one occasion he got into
the Victorian Eleven against N. S. Wales when
Blackham was unable to play. It was as the con-
temporary and pal of the famous East Melbourne
players of the late ’7o’s and the ’Bo’s that he was
most noted. The ranks of these old stagers have
been sadly thinned of late. He survived Tommy
Horan, perhaps the closest of his old-time friends,
by r a few weeks only. Both wrote on sport for the
Australasian for many years —Horan on cricket and
Healy on football. Healy was 65.
Passed along the Sunset Trail in France
lately, Lieut. Frank Cheadle, known best in this
city as one of the prettiest side-steppers either
Union or League Rugby ever had in these parts.
Cheadle was an exceedingly light man even for a
wing three-quarter, but that handicap didn’t stop
him reaching the top. Always a member of the
Newtown Club, both as amateur and pro., Cheadle
won his way into several rep. teams. His greatest
achievement was to secure selection in the League
team which toured Britain in 1908-9. Jack Fihelly,
at present acting Minister for Justice in Banana-
land, was one of the same crowd, which had a pretty
frigid time. Cheadle went into the big game as a
non-com. in the 17th Battalion.
Lacrosse premierships are off the menu
in most Australian States. N. S. Wales has dropped
it; Victoria will hold club games, excluding military
eligibles; Queensland runs a side-show for juniors
only; South Aus. (which boasts of having 200 out of
300 in khaki) has decided to place pennant matches
on the shelf while the guns are booming.
* * * *
The rival factions in Sydney baseball are
fighting their battles over again. Despite rumors
N.S.W’. Baseball Association didn’t call in the under-
taker, but pushes off the season with 13 clubs in
two grades; while the National League goes on with
16 clubs in two grades. Thus far the League has
the big end of the stick.
W. S. Noble (secretary Balmain League
Football Club, 1915) whacks back: “The person
responsible for the paragraph (B. 11/5/T6) re the
non-enlisting of League Footballers indulges in polite
fiction. If he had been in the habit of using soap
and water when cleaning his glimmers he would
have noted the absence from Balmain of big Geo.
Cummins (now Lieutenant). He would also have
missed McMurtrie and Blinco, now at the Front.
I have taken it for granted that the man he did
miss was Kelly, late skipper. Further, those players
of the Balmain first-grade club who haven t enlisted
so far include four married men with families. There
may be others in the same boat. This only refers
to the first-grade team. If the Rugby League cared
to supply a list of names of players who have
grabbed a gun, it could produce just as many as
any other athletic body, without having to include
vice-presidents and committeemen and retired players.
Noble’s effort is worthy of the cause it is made in.
The Bulletin’s statement that there was only one
new player in the Balmain team was quite in order.
Would Noble care to say that Wall, Miller, C.
Fraser, J. Craig, Robinson, Johnston, Halloway, R.
Craig, Latta, A. Fraser, Burnicle, Baulkwell and
Schultz are new players ? In fact, the writer will
now withdraw his statement that there is one new
face and take his oath there are none! Good luck
to George Cummins, Charlie McMurtrie, Blinco and
Kelly, who have grabbed a gun ; but the fact remains
that every player who appeared for Balmain against
Glebe on the Agricultural Ground on May 6 repre-
sented the same club at one time or another last
season. Noble says that if the Rugby League “cared
to” it could supply a list of names as big as any
other athletic body. W T hy not stop cackling and
come along with a list of the players who haven’t
gone? That was what The Bulletin was talking
about. And that’s what it intends to go on talking
about.
• * * »
Stan Gregory, a sergeant in the A.1.F.,
and a nephew of Syd Gregory, has left for the Front
a second time. Severely wounded in Gallipoli, he
was sent to Lemnos, thence to London and finally to
Sydney, where his cure was completed. Now he
reckons to get some of his own back.
Cricket and football in Hobart have for
years been very sick, and after a long fight against
public indifference and natural disadvantages the
Upper Cricket Ground has given up the ghost. When
the bank got tired of the overdraft an attempt was
made to raise cash by £6 debentures alleged to
yield 5 per cent.; but as the public showed no yearn-
ing to get rich quick this way, the enthusiasts who
backed the scheme have been called upon to shed
the £2O each had guaranteed. Also the ground
has been shut. This ground, where Tasmanian
players have battled against invaders for some 30
years past, has always been regarded as a batsman’s
paradise, and several world’s records have been put
up there. In 1901 C. J. Eady hit up the handy
total of 566 for Break-o’-Day against Wellington,
and in 1908 Colin Newton (who has since repre-
sented the State against N.S.W. and Vic.) and J. A.
Barnett (last year’s Rhodes scholar) put another
world’s record on the slate by scoring 400 for no
wickets for Queen’s College. Again in 1913 F. E.
Woolley, the lengthy Kent lefthander, amassed 306
(not out) against Tas., thus beating by a brace
W. W. Armstrong’s 303 not out against Somers ■
1905. Here also C. J. Eady, for the fim g “
Australia, scored a century in each iimines rJ
inter-State match, notching 116 and 112
Victoria in 1894. * n ’ o ’ a e aiI *t

There was a real old Outback sports meet
tng on Easter Monday at Ooldea. 100 miles ahead of
the transcontinental railhead, fullv 100 people being
present. A crowd of blacks turned up from the Mnf
grave Range, but the Fowler’s Bar binehi kent
away to mend himself—he has been having a war
lately. The programme included a tug-of-war for
honor and glory, a high jump for which the first
prize was a pipe, and a camel race for 10s l nc i

“Mulga”: The death of Billy Kuhnel
reminds me of an incident on Adelaide Oval in the
old high-bike days. Busst, Davis and Cowling were
grinding out a hard finish, when all three toppled
over. Kuhnel, who was a few yards behind, looked
a certain winner then; but a pup, rushing furiously
towards the debris, upended him too. I forgot who
won, but I know it wasn’t the poodle.
Wilding is not to be forgotten in Aus-
tralasian lawn tennis. Recently his parents offered
the choice of Anthony’s trophies to the Lawn Tennis
Association of Australasia for a memorial competition,
and the gift will be the groundwork of an annuai
“Wilding Perpetual Championship.” The event is
of course hung up until the war clouds roll by.
*j* * »
For some time past there has been war.
in Sydney between the person with a motor-boat and
the man with a fishing-line. Bream fishers alwavs
moor their boat fore and aft across the tide, the
favorite way being to anchor at the bow and make a
line fast ashore. In narrow channels this means
blocking up the fairway, so that all traffic must
keep in midstream instead of, as the rules require,
on the starboard side. A mooring-line is hard to
see, and when a boat-owner gets one round his
propeller his profanity is nearly as dreadful as that
which emerges from the fishing-boat. In order to
save bloodshed the Amateur Fishermen’s Association
at its last meeting decided to instruct its members
to anchor bow and stern, and do away with the
mooring line.
* * * *
“Spitzer”: I don’t know whether well-
bred dogs need training to “point,” but I am ready
to swear that a mongrel I owned learned the game
for himself. Although he never saw another dog at
work he would “set” anything that stirred. Many
a day I have sat on the fence of' a small paddock
while he worked it for quail, and not a bird was
Hushed until I had the gun at the ready. This
mongrel was equally good at retrieving, but only
from water. He would not retrieve on land. Once
I brought down five whistlers —four into water and
one on a mud island. I had to get the last one my-
self. Another thing: he always dropped the game
at the water’s edge.
N.S.W. champion cyclist G. A. Halpin
had a busy day at Sydney on Saturday. Besides
winning a heat in a mile handicap and filling second
hole in the final of the half-mile handicap (both from
scratch) he tackled Jack Parsons’s Australian mile
grass record of 2min. 13 2-ssec., which has stood
for 21 years. But he failed to do better than
2min. 37 2-ssec.
* * » *
A new record in uselessness, which looks
safe for a while, was put up in Paris recently, when
a daredevil dropped out of a balloon and fell
11,500 ft. attached merely to a parachute.

was held in the Banking House, Queen Street, Brisbane, on Thursday, 27th April, 1916.
Mr. Thomas AVelsby, Chairman of Directors, presided.
The General Manager read the notice convening the meeting.

One member of an early battalion
must have found himself in an awk-
ward fix after he got to the 1 ront.
He was a Bulgarian, and when he
left Australia his native country was
still on the fence. Nothing pleased him bet-
ter then than the idea of having a slap at
Turkey. But no word has come back as to
how he got on when Ferdinand decided to
line up on the Sultan’s side.
“Stenog”: Four lines of prayer for the
economical use of paper came from the Min-
ister to our department the other day on
114 sq. inches of paper. Four inches of
space were taken up by the heading and two
inches by the margin. The back covered
the entire surface —an aching void like the
place where the boss keeps his ideas on
economy.
The conference of superfluous State Pre-
miers in Adelaide this month is to have a
representative cackle. N. S. W ales sends
Holman and his man Friday Hall; Victoria,
Peacock and Lawson; Queensland, Acting-
Premier Theodore ; Tas., Lee and the more-
experienced Elliott Lewis ; V estralia, Scad-
dan. The home team is made up of Vaughan
and Styles. A conference on forestry, to be
held at the same time, takes to Adelaide also
the G.-G. and a lot of State Ministers for
Agriculture and Conservators of Forests.
We live in an unpoetic age. This, from a
50-year-old Government Gazette, shows
how appropriate were the official costumes
for a sub-tropical climate in ISGG: —
Tenders are invited until Monday, May 7, for the
supply of 15 (more or less) letter-carriers’ uniform
scarlet coats and black silk hats with gold bands.
After the letter-carrier had sweltered under
his black silk hat they naturally decided
that it was no climate for a white man,
whereas all that the fools had_ really dis-
covered was that it was no climate for a
black silk hat.
The Peacock Ministry evidently loves to
do good by stealth. A certain Woman s
Labor Bureau, having offices in the same
building as the Woman’s Political Associa-
tion, of which Vida Goldstein is president
and Adela Yarra Bankhurst “organiser.”
is in receipt of a useful little £45 per week
from the Victorian Government. This sum
is specifically granted for the purpose of
relieving the distress of unemployed women,
and Alexander the Great cackler is loud in
his assurance that it is so used. But there
does not appear to have been a balance-
sheet rendered since the grant was first
instituted. The most honest of women are,
no doubt, often backward in these small
formal matters; nevertheless a little illumi-
nation on the subject would do no harm and
might conceivably do a lot of good.
Particulars were given lately of the men
concerned in the pioneer Australian con-
struction of aeroplane-engines, three machines
being mentioned, with their builders and
pilots. All these Australians are now at
war jobs. John Tilly, who built the first
engine, is in Sydney, working on naval sup-
plies. J. R. Duigan, the Victorian w T ho
built and flew the completed aeroplane and
got his pilot’s certificate at Brooklands
(Eng.) before the war started, is a lieu-
tenant in the Australian Flying Corps.
Warrant-officer Aubrey Lock, of Malvern
(Vic.), who built the next engine, is attached
to the travelling workshop which is about
to leave with the newest Australian fleet of
motor-ambulances. Alec Porter, who flew
the Lock-Porter ’plane at Albury (Vic.),
is with a transport column in parts un-
known. And Colonel Harley Tarrant, out
of whose Melbourne workshops came the
third ’plane-engine, is organiser of all the
transport outfits leaving Australia.
Six months ago it was announced that
Staff-sergeant Heath, of the Australian Fly-
ing Corps in Mesopotamia, had won the
D.C.M. “for distinguished conduct and devo-
tion to duty in assisting to pole a bellum
28 miles in 12 hours in intense heat, when
proceeding to the rescue of aviators who had
been forced to descend in the enemy’s coun-
try.” Nobody could explain exactly what
Heath had done ; but late details relating to
the Kut-el-Amara business show that the
transport-boats used by General Townshend
to make his plucky river advance last May
were called heliums. So what Heath did
was 12 hours’ punting of a heavy barge up
the Tigris, in hot weather, and with a good
chance of being sniped from either bank at
any time during the trip. The medallist
was one of the “advanced” pupils at the
Point Cook flying-school when that concern
was a very small war-baby.
Private “Scotty” Fernie, who has brought
back a Distinguished Conduct Medal from
Gallipoli, helped to give the A.M.C. a busy
time for a while. Since he took the count
at Anzac he has been on the operating-table
eight times. A Turkish shell which burst
near him in the lines killed the sergeant
next him, and took away the calf of Fernie’s
left leg, besides smashing his right arm and
some of his ribs and leaving a shrapnel
bullet in his groin and steel splinters scat-
tered through his anatomy. Seventeen
wounds was the total tally. Fernie was a
coal-miner at Wallsend (N.S.W.) when, the
Kaiser broke loose, and while some of his
mates scented a chance to hit the bosses for
better wages he promptly downed tools to
strike against Bill, Abdul and co. He got
away with the first lot, and was in the original
landing at Gallipoli. For six months and
1G days Abdul failed to wing him, and mean-
time he collected his D.C.M. by outing a
sniper and bombing two Turkish parties
out of their trenches. He has not even yet
quite finished giving occupation to the doc-
tors. and is at present in the military hos-
pital at Randwick.
After the fashion of the Western U.S.A.
restaurant which always put “Frogs’ legs a
la Delmonico” on its bill of fare, though it
didn’t have a single frog-leg in the larder,
one leading Melbourne feedery usually in-
serts “snails among a lot
of ordinary dishes with names that are either
French or near it. Like the frogs’ legs, the
snails are supposed to give an air to the pro-
gramme. But the management takes good
care to print its snails in English. If it
called them escargots somebody might acci-
dentally go and order ’em.


Your Piano
grows old with you, and its life should he as long as
yours. It, in its purchase, you allow price to speak too
eloquently in emphasis of money saved, instead of quality
secured, you will be sorry “ever after.” In the choice of
a Piano never forget that it will he with you all the days
of your life, and if it becomes harsh, discordant, tinny, or
dull and lifeless, you have only yourself to blame.
Therefore hesitate to select any instrument until you have
heard and seen
I
The BEALE
Beale Pianos are not only made in Australia by your own countrymen,
but they are the result of conscientious labor and untiring and enthusiastic
activity. The technical details are as near perfection as it is possible to
get, for you will find richness and resonancy, and the triumph of a perfect
touch and glorious tone.
The Beale Piano will bring permanent pleasure, satisfaction and comfort
into your home.
We can help you. to pay for one by allowing you full value for your old
instrument, and giving you extended time to pay off the balance.
We give a Life Policy, if desired, to every purchaser, which provides that
if the hirer, with intent to purchase, should die before the completion of
payments, the Piano will become the property of the person named in
the Policy without further payment.
Call and let us talk this matter over with
you it is worth serious consideration.
Mr OCTAVIUS C. BEALE, Founder and present Controlling Director
of this business, was born at Mt. Mellick, Queen’s County, Ireland,
of an old Irish family. The directors and managers throughout the
business are all descendants of British families for many generations.
There are no Germans employed in the business, and no supplies are
obtained from Germany.

“Grave-digger”: There are not many
really live citizens in Hobart, but there are
rows and rows of dead ones. In fact, it
takes 15 cemeteries to hold them all, in area
ranging from 20 acres downwards. The
two largest are still in use, but the others
are in various stages—mostly well advanced
—of dilapidation and decay. Nowadays the
various sects all go peacefully enough into
the one fold, a gravel path being fence
enough between them; but in the good old
days every religion ran its own yard. Even
the Society of Friends and the Children of
Israel each had a little pool to them-
selves. though those queer fish have always
made a very small shoal.
Maoriland’s scheme of handing different
badges to (1) discharged fighting men back
from the Front, (2) enlisted men waiting
to be called up, (3) men rejected as unfit,
and (4) men retained for special work, has
been an utter failure. Very few of the
decorations were worn, the unfit man speci-
ally refusing to brand himself as such. So
the first lot of badges have been hastily
withdrawn and have been replaced by (1)
a scarlet band indicating that the wearer
has had a go, and (2) a khaki ditto in-
forming white-feather fiends that the wearer
is willing to go. And that’s all that was
really required.
“Analyst”: A garrulous lady the other
day paid an account with a damp and filthy-
looking fl note. As I took the thing she
apologised for its condition by stating that
she had kept it under the mattress and when
her child 'ad the dip’theria “it got wet
without me knowin’ it.” I dropped the
horror into my cash-box with a shudder and
washed my hands. If we must keep our
rag money so long on the roads, isn’t there
any method of armor-plating it so that
clothed savages can't make it a passport
to the cemetery?
Of course, the Law has to do it. still
In the Casement case, hearing something
in the evidence which aroused its suspicion,
the Ass gravely brayed an inquiry whether
Britain was at war with Germany. Being
assured that it was, the Animal shook its
ears and remarked that it must have proof
of the disturbance, if any. So an official
solemnly _ produced the London Gazette of
August 5. 1914, containing the declaration
of war. The Law duly .made a note of the
evidepce, an(l all was well.
A centipede and scorpion
Beside a waterhole did meet,
AVhere the scorpion gave the warning
Of surrender or retreat;
A sharp and awful battle
Between the vipers did ensue.
To the pleasure of a little frog
Who watched the willing two ;
Beside the shinev waters
The warrior’s did war,
The scorpion was mangled
His piracy was o’er.
The little frog did leapeth
Around the vanquished one,
The centipede was a russian
And the scorpion a Hun.

The two proposed transfers to the Reserve Fund, together totalling £45,000, will bring the amount
of the Fund up to £610,000.
During the Half-year Agencies were opened at Xyah Railway Station (Victoria), Point Pass and
Water vale (South Australia), and kukerin (Western Australia); and the Agency at Mt. Kokeby
(Western Australia) was elo.-ed.
Since our last Report 51 additional officers of the. Bank's staff have enlisted with the Australian
Expeditionary Forces, making the number 229 in all.
The Bank's donations to the various War Funds during the Half-year amounted to £1550, making,
with the £2200 referred to in previous report, a total of £3750 given during the vear to these
objects.
The Ordinary General Annual Meeting will be held at the Board Room, at the Bank's Head Office,
Collins Street. Melbourne, on Wednesday, 3rd May. at noon, when the Shareholders will be called upon
to elect two Directors in lieu of John Grice, Esq., and Edward Trtnchard, Esq., who retire by rotation
in accordance with the Articles of Association, and are eligible for re-election. There are no other
candidates.
The Shareholders will also he called upon to elect two Auditors for the ensuing year.
By order of the Board of Directors,
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
Head Office: Melbourne. 19th April, 1916.
E. 11. WREFORD, Chief Manager.

I, Ernest Henry Wreford, Chief Manager of The National Bank of Australasia Limited, do solemnly
and sincerely declare:—
That the Reserve Fund, amounting to £565,000, and the Accumulated Profits, to £114,539/7/11,
are used in the business.
That the accompanying statement and balance-sheet of the Bank is, to , the best of my knowledge
and belief, true in every particular.
The names, addresses and occupations of the persons who are Directors of - the Bank at the date
hereof are: John Grice, Esq., Melbourne, gentleman; Edward Trenchard, Esq., Melbourne, stock and
station agent; Hugh Murray Strachan, Esq., Melbourne, gentleman; aiid Joseph Newman Barker, Esq.,
Melbourne, gentleman. - ' ~
And I make .this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue
of the provisions of an Act of the Parliament of Victoria rendering persons making a false declaration
punishable for wilful and corrupt perjury.
Declared at Melbourne, in the State of Victoria, this nineteenth day; of April, 1916.
Before me —D. Buzolieh, J.P. E. H. WREFORD, Chief Manager.
To the Shareholders of The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —
We report that we have examined and audited the accounts of your Bank for the Half-vear ended
31st March, 1916, and that, in our opinion, the Balance-sheet and Accounts have been properly drawn up
and exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the Bank's affairs according to the best of our
information, and the explanations given to us and as shown by the Books of the Bank.
The London Office Return to 26th February, 1916, duly audited by Messrs. Price, Waterhouse and
Company, has been submitted to us.
The assets and liabilities at the Branches are taken on the certificates of the Managers and
Accountants.
We have received every assistance from the officers of the Bank, and they have supplied us with all
the information and explanations required by us in the course of the audit.
Melbourne, 19th April, 1916.

FOR BAD COUGH USE TAR SYRUP.
HOW TO MAKE IT.
Coughs, colds, catarrh and bronchial
troubles all yield quickly to the concentrated
healing properties of pine forests as found
in ordinary bitrate of tar. The most
effective way in which to use it is to add
two ounces to a syrup made by dissolving
half a pound of sugar in half a pint of hot
water. Stir thoroughly, and pour into a
pint bottle. One or two teaspoonfuls of this
tar syrup will quickly stop tickling coughs,
and taken regularly two or three times a
day will afford relief to the most stubborn
cough, cold or case of catarrh. It is also of
great value in the treatment of throat and
bronchial trouble arising from exposure to
the damp and cold. Care should be used to
get the bitrate instead of other forms of tar,
which are not to be recommended for this
purpose.— Advt.

IN BANANA LAND
“Mulga” : The town of Condamine affords
one of the most striking examples of depopu-
lation. Half a century ago it was as busy
as any town in Queensland ; being the centre
of a thriving pastoral district, with the
main routes to Roma and St. George passing
through it. Then the traveller had four-
places to quench his thirst; now there is
only one. In this district the pear octopus
has a firm hold of some of the best land in
the Commonwealth, and where was once
life and bustle is now prickles and stagna-
tion.
C. F. .T. Holmes, who used to be one of
Archbishop Donaldson’s “bush brothers”
back of Charleville, has been figuring in
despatches. He is doing his bit as regi-
mental chaplain to the Lincolnshire Yeo-
manry. and was with them in the Mediter-
ranean on the transport Mercian when that
vessel was chased and shelled for an hour
and a quarter by a Hun submarine. Accord-
ing to the report to the Army Council, one
shell, the fuse of which was of the “time”
instead of the “percussion” variety, fell on
deck. Holmes picked it up and threw it
overboard before it could explode. The
same act. in the case of an old-fashioned
Turkish bomb-shell, won a V.C. for a naval
seaman at the bombardment of Alexandria
in 1882. Holmes came to Queensland
straight from Oxford University about ten
years ago. arid after going back to the Cold
Country in 1013 got a job as Anglican
rector near Grantham, in Lincolnshire,
where Carr Smith runs a tabernacle nowa-
days.
Dunn, M.L.C.. of Maryborough, attempted
to get the futile. Commerce Congress to con-
sider the best means of encouraging the
close settlement of Northern Australia. But
he made a tactical error and suggested that
the North should, be favored with a special
tariff; whereupon the importers and agents
promptly rejected the proposal and refused
further to discuss the advancement of their
own country. immediately afterwards the
congress unanimously passed a motion in
favor of a preferential tariff being extended
to all Allies —presumably including Japan,
where labor can be got for a few pence per
day.
“Gum-sucker”: There’s one shameless
Englishwoman living in a Northern district
who is doing her little bit to make the local
Hun feel quite at home. The lady’s husband
enlisted, and directly he had got safely out
of the way on the track to Berlin a burly
Hun neighbor showed his very ardent sym-
pathy by moving himself into her house. lie
now boldly parades in the departed hubby’s
togs. but. of course, that saves the bother of
putting them in moth-balls. This war has
taught people to put up with makeshifts,
but isn't it over the limit when we use an
import of the Ilun brand as a substitute
for a good Australian husband?
THE RIVAL'S LIQUID NOTES.
TRAGEDIAN : “What caused Ranter's downfall? Too many empty houses ?”
COMEDIAN : “No; too many counter attractions."
Tragedy has been crammed into the 26
years of Elsie Shaw, who was the other day
sentenced at Brisbane to five years for
fatally stabbing Willie Nielsen. At Six her
runaway mother took her to Brisbane; at
13 she was the associate of Chows and
negroes; and at 17 she was a registered
woynan of the slums. Incidentally she had
served sentences for stealing and vagrancy.
Then she married, but her husband deserted
her, and at 26, while living with
Nielsen, himself separated from his
wife, she plunged a knife into his
heart during a drunken brawl. At
the conclusion of the trial she learned for
the first time of the existence of her father
and brother. Judge Lukin admitted that
she never had a chance, but handed out five
years, tempered with a promise that he
would recommend her release at the end of
two years if her record was good.
The Workshop.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the workshop of the mind ply the busy
carpenters;
Many gleaming tools you find, anyone your
hand prefers.
Here a plane by wisdom made, here a saw
by Sorrow.
Sharp the teeth? Are you afraid? They
will blunt to-morrow.
Here a turning lathe of Life, twisting sure
and slow :
Crude beginnings choke its strife, but how
smooth .they grow !
Sawdust fogs the air sometimes. By the
window pane
One bright beam of fancy climbs, pricks
with gold the grain,
And the wood you thought just pine, scoured
and citrus-white.
Where the Vision lit may shine filigreed
with light!
In the workshop of the mind haunted cor-
ners lie ;
Here a burial chest to find, dowelled heavily.
With the steel pins row on row mortising
each part,
Some are red with rust to show where they
pierced a heart!
In the workshop of the mind grey dust
cloaks the floor.
Old winds stir the ragged blind (phrases
spelled before),
Litter crowds the entrance way, shreds of
shavings fly ;
Yet who knows but some rare day, on the
bench near by.
When your gipsy fancies roam in rebellious
mood,
To your chisel there may come a block of
sandalwood.
Till you carve with soul aflame forms of
Asian grace
Spicy tokens without name, or a woman’s
face.
Elephants in jungle lands, rajahs brown
and grave,
Palm leaves'/in a eunuch's hand, jewels on a
slave.
If the sawdust outward drift's, it is incense
now
That some reverent Naga lifts, hooded to the
brow....
Plain deal was your task to-day? Easily
understood
Why your chisel snapped that way....
dream iny sail dal wood!

My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
We haven't been a dull old community
this week, anyhow. In between junketing
the Chamber of Commerce Congress we have
razz led with the Presbyterian Assembly and
.the Congregational Union; and the Operatic
Players’ season of "La Fille du Tambour
Major” and a multitude of coin teas and
fetes have been minor incidents.
L. M. Bond, the present serang of the
Brisbane Chamber of Commerce, and Mrs.
Bond gave the glad hand t<> about 200 inter-
Staters and local folk on Monday at a river
trip. There was more river than trip; for
the Lucinda couldn’t move on an exception-
ally low tide, and made a playful attempt to
keep the guests anchored at the wlian for
the afternoon. Same night. Mayor and
Mavoress blethering ton. aided by their popu-
lar cluck Ena, fed the delegates at the Exhi-
bition Hall. The person responsible for the
decorations did a service to the city by
successfully hiding the ugliness of the barn.
City-organist Sampson also did excellent
woik at odd moments.
Governor Gould Adams and his lady sum-
moned the Commerce delegates and many
local citizens to an At Home on Wednes-
day night. Several topnotfch local artists
were engaged to provide music; but Mrs
Robert Bell, in a gold brocade with tricky
touches of blue, was the only songstress. F.
M. Fisher (who wore the khaki of a private
in the A.1.F.), Eric Hayne and Victor Gal-
way were other contributors.
A very swagger dinner on Finney’s para-
pets was the semi-final to the Commerce
Congress; and delegates had their final
course on a motor-trip to the strawberry
and pineapple lands around Cleveland and
Redland Bay.
Chaplain Father Barry has had two gold-

for one of the Fronts, has the distinction of
being the youngest chaplain to leave Aus-
tralia.

a quarter of a century had battled hard for
the Girls’ Friendly and other virtuous thh.,,
that we’re all the better for.

AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Queenslander” : B. 18/5/16, referring to
the damages given against John Wren in' the
Goodna libel case, speaks of the Brisbane
Daily Mail articles as having caused the
trouble. So they did; but the paper which
was hit by the verdict, and which let Wren
m for the damages aforesaid, was a very
different proposition from the Daily Mail
of to-day—the present 1).M., with a new
management, with men of good Australian
standing handing out its articles, arid at
least three Bulletin bards helping to top
it off on the literary side, is a more readable
concern—and. 1 understand, a better bnsi
ness one—than at any previous period of its
history. After years of wandering in the
wilderness it looks like winning through.
“Ab Infra": The new quarters of the
Strait Hospital, referred to by “Ah
luxi ra (B. Ui/3/’lO), are perhaps a trifle
elaborate, but they are no better than the
good white men and women who tend
1 hursday Island ’s variegated sick deserve.
Previously they had to combat the dreadful
diseases of Asia under conditions which
would not be tolerated in an all-white com-
munity. Ihe living quarters were low and
cramped, and as they really formed part
of the hospital the nurse off duty was never
dissociated from her work even during her
hours of sleep. Sanitation was of a sweetly
primitive kind, the sink arrangements con-
sisting of an old gramophone trumpet and
a piece of discarded diving hose. Anything
was good enough for the angels of mercy
who ministered chiefly to discolored brother
—nothing mattered so long as dearly-beloved
Colored Brother recovered quickly and was
again at work chasing the furtive pearl-
oyster and dividends for the boss.
The Government is still doubtful whether
it has enough business ability to manage its
own butcher’s shop. The other day 3500
Ipswich unionists sent a delegation to the
Minister requesting a local branch. Hunter,
acting Chief Sec., after informing the depu-
tants that their application was about the
200th. suggested that they should establish
a co-operative concern of their own. The
wisdom or otherwise of continuing the State
business would, lie said, be ascertained this
year, and “it would be no use the Govern-
ment pursuing a policy that was doomed to
failure.” But even if the Government re-
solves that it hasn’t the capacity to run a
butcher's shop, it will no doubt still insist
that it has a perfect genius for controlling a
whole State.

Why is this Studebaker car so
ECONOMICAL?
because
of the efficient manufacturing methods and the excep-
tional facilities of the" 147-acre Studebaker plants, Stude-
baker cars are ECONOMICAL in first-cost; and they are ECONOMICAL in
after-cost because they combine STRENGTH, ENDURANCE and SIMPLICITY
with LIGHTNESS OF' WEIGHT —all the way through, the Studebaker engi-
neers have made the Studebaker an ECONOMICAL car to buy, to own, to operate.
Concentration on practically one chassis for the past five years, instead of experimenting with
different types every year or two, has brought the Studebaker to a degree of perfection not
attained by other cars. Every unnecessary part has been eliminated. Weight has been carefully
balanced so that the car rides easily on its springs, while alignment is so precise that the car
drives straight as an arrow. Hence Studebaker's exceptional ECONOMY on tyres and ECONOMY
of upkeep.
And the Studebaker engine is ECONOMICAL of fuel. The carburetor is placed high, lessening
the distance the gas has to travel to the cylinders —the design of valves, manifolds, pistons, every
detail has been carefully studied—thus making a great saving in petrol and oil consumption
But the best way to judge the ECONOMY of the Studebaker is to critically examine the
car itself, to study it. Or if you will let us demonstrate it, well PROVE its economy to you.
