AMRANTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ex-Empress Eugenie celebrated her 83rd
birthday on May 4.
The gallant youth of France that went to death
That a masked fool might look Imperial,
Nigh fifty years ago it gave its breath
And changed its sunlight tor a dirty pall,
Sobbing its life out for that charlatan
Who gave dear France to rapine at Sedan.
In sunny fields of Alsace and Lorraine
The soldier of this war turns up the bones
Of sires who died in dolor and in pain
Republicans to bolster tott'ring thrones.
Spanish Eugenie still sleeps soft, eats full,
And ev’ry furrow shows a soldier’s skull.
Yet the comparison is not for naught
The people’s courage and the puppet's greed.
Though yet no regiment of kings is brought
Into the battle-front to thrill and bleed,
The people see the kings bestow them all,
As David sent Uriah out to fall.
They see through all the acid fogs of death
That peace shall never be while there are kings ;
They see that Privilege is but a breath,
That prince and duke are vain and empty things ,
Across the pain-lashed world they clear their eyes
To see the stars of the Republics rise.
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Gone at 56 Hugh Duff, a familiar figure in
Sydney, and a leading real estate auctioneer
for over 30 years. He was an enthusiastic
fighter for the North Shore bridge, and any
precarious hope it ever had of being built
was in a large measure due to him.
“Bill Back”: Re Frederic Villiers’s
French generals, “the worst-dressed men m
the armies, moving about in sagging over-
alls” (B. 4/5/’IG). Leslie Maygar, one of
the bravest men on Gallipoli, an officer and
a gentle V.C., habitually did his duty in
long-distance slops, depressed headgear and
an outsize jacket having a forlornly old-
maidish expression about the collar and
skirt. But his men willingly followed to the
hobs of Hades.
W. J. Fullerton, M.L.A., has discovered
the true cause of Whitsitt and all the other
evils which afflict the Speck. It is, he told
the Anglican Synod, partly because his fel-
low-members are a godless, wicked, material-
istic set, but mostly because of the party
system. Looking at the curse from a new
point of view, Fullerton, M.L.A., says that
the main reason why Parliament lets social
and moral questions rip is that they , can’t
very well be treated as party matters, and
so nobody cares a red cent about them.
In a case heard before Justice Darling in
London last month, in which a top-line vaude-
villian named Mark Sheridan was sued by a
hook-nosed agent for commission alleged to
be owing on engagements procured, evidence
was given by the chairman of the Tivoli,
Oxford and Pavilion to the effect that Sheri-
dan had, in consequence of the war, “volun-
tarily agreed” to a reduction of his weekly
screw from £7O to £5O. The cue was
promptly taken up by Darling, the accredited
funny man of the Bench, who observed : “Per-
haps I ought to follow his example !” The
witness, as in duty bound, smiled, and then
had his own little joke. “We as directors,”
said he, “have also reduced our salaries. I
am sorry to say that the example of the direc-
tors of the Tivoli has not been followed by
the directors of the Empire.” At which,
strange to relate, there was no witticism
forthcoming from Mr. Justice Darling.
“Bill Bow y yang”: I came across an ancient
Bulletin the other day in which “Trooper
Bluegum” had a few words to say about the
songs the Anzacs sang in the Gallipoli
trenches. I noticed that Billjim when in the
firing-line preferred the melancholy ditty.
One night we had an impromptu concert at
Anzac and only one comic song was volun-
teered. All the rest were about consumptive
children, orphans, deserted maidens, the re-
turn of erring wanderers, and so on. No one
seemed to see anything at all out of place in
a burly member of an infantry unit asking
“Shall I be an angel, daddy?” or a six-foot
trooper, twiddling his hat round as he fixed
his eyes on the stars, singing “Don’t Go
Down the Mine, Daddy.” During the time
I was at Anzac I never heard a song that
could not be sung in the rector’s drawing-
room —the obscene and the salacious ditty
found no place in Private Billjim’s reper-
toire.
“Gordon G.” : Picture-show critics miss the
moral motor-’bus when they talk of the bad
effect some films have on kids from 10 to IS.
My complaint is as to the bad effect on the
average female between 14 and 41—married
ladies not excepted. Take the customary
erotic “sentimental” slush wfflich is dished
up at least once in every programme. Through
these frauds little Mrs. Queenanne and
her picture chum, Mrs. Shingleroof, nightly
compare the splendid creatures of the film
with their own unromantic rent-getters—to
the invariable detriment of the latter. The
“pernicious novelette” is a bunch of violets
alongside the film, for the reason that the
habit of picture-gazing is much more simply
acquired and retained than book-reading and
requires less brain. This suits Mrs. Queenanne
and Mrs. Shingleroof down to the ground, for,
if they are f.a.q. suburban matrons, they
“don’t get much time for reading.” and, if
they did, they would read the sort of tripe
the picture people boil down for them.
A Music Club which is threatening to
arise in Melbourne is vowed to shed a beam
of patronage upon any kind of foreign and
home-made music that deserves it, and
to roll a big drum full of lead over the other
kind; Fritz Hart, the Conservatorium’s
musical director, who is English right
through, and only got his front name by
some unfortunate mistake at the font, is pro-
claiming in 16 different keys that good old
roast-beef British music is what his soul
craves seven days a week. If this is so. he
may feel a little nervous about the projected
club, which is to float round amongst the
composers in a state of beatified neutrality.
Melba herself is to have a big say in its
doings. Quiet concert haunters who only
play things that can be pedalled or wound-
up may join by merely planking down ten-
and-six. You don’t even need to have long
hair; a fairly soulful expression will meet
the case.

Little Lectures
by Nurse ‘Wincarnis.’
[Lecture No 3.)
Run-Down
When your system is under-
mined by worry or overwork
when your vitality is
lowered when you feel
“ any-how” when your
nerves are “on edge ” (
when the least exertion tires ,

of his work among the people of the Adirondack
country is intensely dramatic, and comes to
a thrilling climax with a forest fire, described

Watts, Author of “The Rise of Jennie

*** Mrs. Watts’s new volume contains the
history of an intelligent, well-bred girl, who
touches life at many points. The story is
told with the charm of manner, the know-
ledge of human nature, and the humor that
are always to be found in books by this
author, and it is consequently of very great
interest.

Eighty-second Annual Report of the Directors of the Rank of Australasia,
With the Accounts for the Half-year to 11th October, 1915,
Presented to the Proprietors at the Annual General Meeting, held on Thursday, 30th March, 1916.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-sheet as at ilth October last, with the Profit
Account f v,„ half-vc-ar to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, tor British and
Australasian rates and taxes, and after making provision for bad and doubtful debts and other con-
tingencies, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £201,(43 3s. sd.
To this sum mi st be added £113.353 4s. lid. brought forward from the previous half-year making
a total of £315.096 Bs. 4d„ out of which the Directors have declared a Dividend for the lialf-ieai at
the rate of 14 per cent, per annum, or £2 16s. per share, and a Bonus at the rate of 3 pci cent, pci
annum, or 12s. per share, together £3 Bs. per share, free of income tax.
The Dividend and Bonus will absorb £170,000, and the Directors have furthei appropriated
£30,000 to the Reserve Fund. There will then remain £115,096 Bs. 4d. to be carried forward to the
Idle Dividend and Bonus will be payable, in London and in Australasia, on the 31st March
The directors have to record, with much regret, the death of their esteemed colleague, the Rt. Hon.
SU The'' rotation are: Arthur Whitworth, Esq., the Rt. Hon. the Earl of Lichfield
and Sir E Montague Nelson, K.C.M.G., who, being eligible, otter themselves for re-election
The directors have filled the vacancv caused by the death of the lit. Hon. Sir Andrew R. Scoble by
the election as a director of Mr. T. R. Johnson, late Chief Commissioner for Railways and Tramways m
New South Wales, and the proprietors will be asked to approve his election.
The auditors, Messrs. Welton, Jones and Co., offer themselves for re-election.
Since the date of the last report a branch lias been opened at Albany, Western Australia.
4 Threadneedle Street, London, 20th March, 1916. A. WHITWORTH, Chairman.

We have examined the cash and securities in London and the London books, and have verified the
transfers from the several branches in the Commonwealth and New Zealand, and we beg to report that
in our opinion, the foregoing is a full and fair balance-sheet of the bank, and that it exhibits a true
and correct view of the state of the bank’s affairs as shown by the books. The investments were
valued, so far as could be ascertained, at market price or under.
London, March 15, 1910. WELTON, JONES AND CO., Auditors.

The Inky Way.
“Harrison O.”: Even while Armageddon
rages the English press lives up to its col-
lective motto: “Snoblesse oblige.” Despite
the fact that men of all classes have joined
the colors, the writers of yarns in maga-
zines and newspapers persist in making all
Tommies and N.C.O.’s mislay their aspirates
and otherwise maltreat their native
tongue, while every young officer is the
“Hon. Jimmy” or some other brand of noble
who treats with good-humored condescen-
sion the rough, uneducated but good-hearted
rank and file. The popular _ magazines,
which rely on the snobbish middle-classes
for support, are the worst offenders. They
never dream of allowing their heroes to be
anything less than a lootenant, and mostly
in the Guards at that. That’s another great
advantage of the “democratic” voluntary
system which the P.L.L. so loves; it breeds
snobs and sycophants. None of your beastly
conscription, under which a hard-working
cauliflower-grower like Joffre is liable to
become General of a whole army!
* * * *
W. T. Stead’s daughter Estelle is now
editing the English Refuse of Refuse, the
peace-time editor, brother John, having gone
to take a hand in the Big Job. A while back
Estelle made a mild reputation as a Shak-
spearian actress.
A special jury in the King’s Bench Divi-
sion of the High Court of Justice in Eng-
land has held that Frederick Grundy, Lon-
don correspondent of New York Sun, was
entitled to 12 months’ notice of dismissal,
and awarded him £9OO damages because be
was paid only a month’s salary when his
services were dispensed with in October,
1915. When Grundy entered the service of
the Sun in 1907 he was paid the moderate
wage of £4 a week, and signed an agreement
for a month’s notice; but when he gradually
advanced in salary and became chief corres-
pondent in Britain at £2O a week, he con-
tended that he was entitled to a year’s
notice, and the jury accepted this view. The
decision should interest newspaper proprie-
tors and journalists in Australia, where the
question of what notice should be given and
taken has always been a cause of difference.
It looks from the judgment as if the higher
a man climbs the longer the notice he’s
entitled to, presumably on the very fair
ground that places are few at the top and
take more looking for.
* * * *
“Bad Australian”: The contributor who
wrote about South American newspapers a
month or two ago forgot to mention the
English publications. South Pacific Mail
(Valparaiso) and Standard and Herald
(Buenos Aires). The Mail is a weekly
summary of local news varied by clippings
from overseas and an occasional sensational
yarn like that of Cap’n Besley, who crossed
the Andes in midwinter after undergoing
untold hardships which only his inflexible
determination enabled him to overcome. No-
body but the Mail and the Wide World
took his yarn seriously. Of course, it should
be admitted that there were only four pubs
for the Oap’n to set his course by in 18 miles,
and it is fairly easy to miss pubs when they
are so far apart and not linked up by wire-
less. The Standard and Herald are issued
daily and are in heavy debt to Scissors and
Paste. The Standard is the oldest English
publication in South America —stolid and
highly respectable, with a strictly Dry Dog
policy and a lofty manner towards its reptile
contemporary. But the reptile has had new
blood infused into it of late, and is making
the ancient think hard. The Herald prints
a few good original articles at times, is right
down on any kind of public abuse, and has
a definite policy of which it is a fairly lucid
exponent and a vigorous defender. The
paper owes a good deal of its new reputa-
tion as a live wire to the pens of Australian
inky wayfarers.
* * * *
“T.M.W.”: Harry Ivneebone, an Austra-
lian journalist attached to the office of the
High Commissioner, is returning to Austra-
lia. He humped his capacity for work along
the Inky Way on the Westralian goldfields
for years, and I remember him as the head
and shoulders of a little syndicate of four
that battled with the old Coolgardie Miner
when Coolgardie had lost its punch and Fly
Flat was like a deserted cemetery. In those
days he was reporter, editor and stone-hand
roiled into one, and managed to look hearty
on about five hours’ sleep. When Cool-
gardie could no longer support its daily
“crib wrap” Kneebone joined Adelaide
Herald, and from there graduated to the hub
of things.
A BEACH RHAPSODY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When on the beach at ease I lie
And clothe my thoughts in modest speech
(I have a notebook near when I •
Am lying lazy on the beach),
I praise the genial gods that made such beaches
For me to 101 l on, as religion teaches.
I bite my pencil at the end
That isn’t pointed, and I think
What waste of time to strive and spend
And toil and moil and swank and swink!
(A coy, lithe girl, her costume more adorning
Than in the street, floats by. It is good morning.)
I note how playful cloudlets dance
Across the blue, and how there are
Ten million reasons quite, perchance,
Why there’s no fun in being Tsar,
When one can be, as I am at this minute,
A nobody, but happy as a linnet.
The sun is warm, and I admit
I’d love some sylph with waving fan
To cool me. That were apt and fit,
As I’m a Christian gentleman.
My neck is scorched, my brow grows moist and
moister,
My sinciput steams like a stewing oyster.
The day is fine, but work’s a curse
When guineas are so hard to get.
If I were rich, no line of verse
I’d scribble on this pad... .and yet
There are some compensations, well I know it:
Six simple Simons think I am a poet.
N.S.tV.

The Call of Bells.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“TiI OIL I cannot,” said the King, “for
' I 1 those bells, silver bells,
A- From valley-top to valley-top they
leap and make a chain
Whose every link is musical. What is the
old refrain?
’Tis a tune I have forgotten, yet a tune
which once I knew,
Like to the clap of merry leaves that in a
forest grew.”
And the parchment with the heavy seal fell
to the polished floor,
The jewelled pen was put away, the hand
would sign no more ;
Though the courier was waiting in the ante-
room till noon
The King was wandering forest-wards with
pollen on his shoon.
“Guard I cannot,” cried the Prince, “for
those mocking, hindering bells” ;
And he cast the buttoned foil aside and
smoothed his crisp red curls
And donned the little emerald coat that
shimmered o’er with pearls,
And fled away from priest and page and all
the flattering train
To make a cowslip girdle in a sheltered
country lane, . ,
To pluck a bunch of briar rose with thorn-
pricked fingertips,
To snare a shy jack-rabbit and to court a
goose-girl’s lips;
And the lackeys and the men-at-arms went
seeking high and low,
For the lilting bells had shown the Prince
the way he wished to go.
“Pray I cannot,” said the Queen, “for those
ribald, foolish bells” —
The Queen with her pale flower-face and
manner most devout;
And she left the velvet cushion where her
missals were spread out,
And the cross that threw a shadow on the
tesselated floor.
And she found a quiet passage and she
found an open door,
A garden with some shallow steps, a peacock
on the lawn,
A dove with rosy strutting feet, while
through the air was drawn
A folk song that a huntsman sang in a
remembered wold
Where sun on cottage tiles was warm and
unreaped fields were gold ;
While, in the chapel, filtered rays fell from
the painted arch
On her neglected missal. Loudly hymning
in the larch
She heard the happy blackbird, and the
flower-cups among
The buoyant worker-bee had thrust its
greedy pointed tongue,
The whiie her tire-maid trembled sore,
deserted shrines to see.
And a velvet cushion dented by the pressure
of a knee!
“Learn I trill not,” said the Princess, “for
the clatter of those bells
Is like a company of knights below the
battlements,
A gay cortbge, with banner that is full of
noble rents,
From distant frays victorious” ; and she
pouted and she prest
And coaxed her portly governante, and
leaned against her breast.
“Oh! Let us take the grey jennet and
through the forest ride
Below the babbling waterfall, white veiled
as any bride,
Till we find the great musician who is ring-
ing in the trees
To the nodding of the petals and the chorus
of the breeze ;
I will give you silks and sweetmeats, I will
ply you with old wine,
An you blind yourself, consenting to this
latest prank of mine!”
When the Abbe came, with jangling beads,
to hear what she had learned
He found a scented slipper and a little stool
o’erturned.
King and Queen, Prince and Princess, in
the forest met at last;
When they recognised each other royalty
was quite aghast;
Till the young Princess said, laughing, “As
for once we’re of a mind,
Let us link our hands in searching for the
bells we came to find.”
Where the lilac crocus blossoms, and the
lily droops its head,
And the purple violet crouches in the honey
of its bed,
They could hear the clear bells clashing, and
they hurried through the green,
Idle King and wilful Princess, dreamy
Prince and pious Queen.
V hen at length they found the Ringer in
leaf-sway’s shade and shine
, Tices 'i- Fool his. brass bells shaking to
entice a herd of swine!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Funk.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PRIVATE MASTERS lay in his dugout
and shivered, the guns boomed and
rattled, and the shells shrieked over-
head. His lips moved soundlessly, and at
each succeeding bang from “Beechy Bill” he
shuddered again and pressed back closer to
sheltering, comforting Mother Earth. He
was in a blue funk ; his thoughts were going
on continuously, monotonously, “I can’t stand
it, I can’t stand it.”
His best pal had been killed that morn-
ing. and he shuddered at the mere thought
of the manner of it. “I must get out of it,”
he muttered helplessly to himself. He had
no wish to die yet; life was very sweet and
held so many possibilities. But death, and
a death like Len Pile’s, when there had sud-
denly been no Len—only shattered fragments
of flesh ! If only he could get wounded and
sent from the firing-line—wounded badly
enough to preclude any possibility of having
to return !
So far, none of his companions had
become aware of his terror. If only he could
get out before he was branded as a coward!
There was talk of a bayonet charge to-night;
that was why they were resting now. In
imagination he could hear his own shriek of
terror as an enemy’s bayonet was thrust into
his body—could feel the sickening tear of
the flesh.
Or perhaps he would never get that far.
What if he lacked the courage to go forward?
The horror of the thought appalled him.
Then he thought of a term he had heard
used scornfully, and it ran through his head
with maddening persistency: “Left hand
wounds”—the left-hand wounds of a tribe of
Indians who failed the test of courage and
injured themselves.
lie drew his rifle toward him. No ; not a
left-hand wound —a wound in the foot. Was
it easier to limp with the left or right foot,
he wondered dully. But he must not do it in
the dugout. That was bullet-proof. He
must go out where strays sometimes fell.
If he met any fellows he would tell them he
was going to see some pals further along
the trenches. Carrying his rifle on such an
errand was rather suspicious, but he hoped
they wouldn’t notice that. If they did —•
well, he would say that he hoped to pot
something on the way.
He traced out all details cunningly. The
thought of a time free from all these hellish
noises sustained him: but at intervals he
trembled so that he could scarcely hold his
rifle. Len’s pack was lying where he had
left it that morning. He hoped the fellows
would think to send some of the things it
contained to Len’s mother. Then he ven-
tured out of the dugout, and held liis head
low and crouched as he walked. He came
to a fairly-exposed position and listened. So
far as he could tell from the incessant crash-
ing overhead he was absolutely alone. He
lay down carefully ; he thought it would be
easier that way. lie held the rifle as far
from his foot as he conveniently could. It
was an awkward position, but he did not
want his foot absolutely shattered. Had he
intended it to be his left or his right foot?
Ah, yes; his left, it was easier. He pointed
the rifle carefully.... shut his eyes.... and
pulled the trigger. There was a deafening
explosion and —oblivion.
Private Masters woke up to the conscious-
ness of voices—and pain. He was being
lifted carefully on to a stretcher. “A little
higher,” the stretcher-bearer at his feet was
saying ; and with extreme gentleness he was
raised so that the injured foot was resting as
comfortably as possible.
Masters closed his eyes and groaned.
“Does it hurt much, old chap?” one of the
Ambulance boys inquired. “Take a nip of
this.”
He gratefully sipped the water and slipped
once more into insensibility, to wake again
as his bearers were resting a few brief
moments in shelter before resuming the peril-
ous journey. One of them was binding up
the arm of the other, who was laughing and
saying: “By Jove, a narrow squeak!
Quarter inch nearer, and it would have shat-
tered the bone, and you’d have had two
patients, Jerry.”
Masters shivered uncontrollably ; he could
dimly see the red staining the bandage at
his foot and the stretcher-bearer bend over
it anxiously.
“We’ll have to hurry on,” he said to his
injured mate, “if you think you can stand it.
I can’t stop this haemorrhage.”
The other boy laughed again.
“Of course, I can stand it,” he said cheer-
fully. “Come on !” And he stooped to the
handles.
But Private Masters was filled with a
great horror. To save him these two boys
were running unutterable risks, cheerfully
and contemptuously!
“Leave me,” he muttered ; “I’m not worth
it. Run the risk for some better chap”
“Cheer up, matey,” the wounded boy
laughed. Nothing seemed to damp his
spirits. “If we weren’t carrying you we
would be carrying someone else. We’re
nearly there now; and, anyway, it’s all in
the game.”
He was conscious through nearly all the
rest of the terrible journey, and his mental
suffering was indescribable; but by the time
the hospital tent was reached he had lost
himself in a sea of pain. He knew nothing
of the hurried dressing at the field hospital,
the transfer to the hospital ship, or the final
arrival at the General Hospital. He was
only dimly conscious of pain and more pain,
of faces bending pitifully over him; then a
relapse into nothingness, and once again
pain.
His foot had been amputated four days
when he once more became aware of the
world about him —a world that in his dreams
had been shadowy faces and kind voices and
gentle hands. But still he lay through the
long days and nights motionless and voice-
less. His thoughts were the dominant fac-
tors in his life, and they filled him
with such unutterable repulsion of himself
that he shrank even when a hand touched
him. He knew that he was not worth caring
for, but he realised dumbly that he could not
openly say so. He was an exceedingly docile
patient, and in spite of his silence became a
great favorite with the nurses and orderlies.
They realised that the shock and the strain
of the Front had temporarily shattered his
nerves, and they nursed him skilfully and
tenderly.
But the dumb agony in his eyes so
appealed to the orderly on night-duty—a man
who had always been renowned for his in-
tensely human sympathy, and bad before the
war written world-read books —that one
night, when the morphia had had no effect
and the patient had said “No, thank you,” to
all suggestions of remedies, he sat down by
the bed and took Masters’s hand in one of his
own firm, cool ones.
Masters had been lying thinking, dully
wondering what Lettie would say if she ever
knew how he had been wounded. Lettie had
been so proud when he enlisted, and he re-
membered the night before he left she had
said: “Jim, whatever happens to you. you
are mine ! If you come back maimed, you

are still mine—l shall not give you up.” lie
moaned slightly and tried to withdraw his
hand from the orderly’s. But Hepworth had
had the reputation during a journalistic
career of such sympathetic understanding
that lie could win his story from the most
reserved of human beings ; and in some cases
when the story had been told he drove his
editor into a frenzy by withholding all the
principal features from publication.
So Hepworth only leaned forward and said
quietly: "Is there anything on your mind,
old chap, that it would help you to tell? You
know. I might be able to help you to
straighten things out.”
Masters shuddered and turned his head
restlessly on his pillow; then he laid his
other hand convulsively on Hepworth’s.
"Swear you won't tell." he whispered.
"Swear it! But I'm in Hell —I’m afraid to
live and afraid to die.”
"Your secret’s quite safe with me, old
man,” the orderly said gently; "I swear that
it will go no further.”
“You’ll hate me as I hate myself,” Masters
said; and for a few minutes he lay back,
watching his companion’s face intently.
’“I trust you." he said at length : “I must
have been mad with fear. I always was a
coward. I remember the first time I shot
a man" —he paused and moistened his lips.
He spoke disjointedly, but the other filled in
all the gaps.
"Len had been killed the day—this hap-
pened.” Masters went on. "It was hor-
rible —horrible!” And he shuddered again.
"I made up my mind that 1 must get out of
it before —what happened to Len happened to
me. I went out into the exposed sap—it was
supposed to be dangerous there. .. .and shot
myself... .through the foot!"
The orderly gripped his hand sympatheti-
cally.
"By Jove!" he said. "It shows what a
state your nerves must have been in to
imagine a thing like that.”
"It wasn't imagination.” Masters gasped.
"I lay down and deliberately aimed my rifle
and fired at my deft foot!”
“But it was your right foot that was
wounded." Hepworth broke in.
"The rifle must have slipped: it was diffi-
cult to keep it firm. I remember pulling the
trigger"
But the orderly spoke very quietly and
slowly, so that his words sank into the boy’s
mind.
"From the nature of your wound you could
not have done it yourself." he said. "It was
absolutely impossible."
Masters was trembling. "Is that true?”
he almost screamed. “God! Is that true?
Swear it! I have been in Hell!"
Hepworth’s fingers had slipped to his
patient’s pulse.
“Try not to get excited, old man." he said
quietly, and then continued in a whimsical
way that soothed Masters's nerves indescrib-
ably : "Unfortunately you did not speak to
me soon enough after the operation for me to
show you the proof. You came in in a
dangerous condition. The wounds were
septic, and au immediate operation was im-
perative ; but I can find out which of the
other fellows was in the theatre at the time.
We were infernally busy, going all da> and
most of the night; and I cant remembei
offhand who else was in. But if you want
more evidence I’ll look it up.
“No, thanks!” Masters said quickly. “If
I’ve been a fool, I don’t want anyone else to
know it.” He put his hand tip to his head. 1
was sure I pulled that trigger. But you ate
certain?”
“Absolutely certain that you could not have
done it yourself,” the orderly said con\ mc-
iugly. "Your nerves were evidently in a
terrible state. So you may have gone out
intending to do it. You may even have pulled
the trigger. But remember how difficult it
would be to aim a rifle from the position m
which vou must have been holding it, and m
the nervous state that you were in. The
enemy shell must have landed just about the
time you planned your little stunt. But why
didn't vou wake up sooner, old man, while
vou still had the foot as proof? I would
gladly have taken the dressing off to show
you.”
Masters was lying back with his eyes shin-
ing.
•I wish I had.” he said. “It would have
saved me the torture of the damned."
His voice was already clearer; his hand
firmer. Hepworth stood up and arranged the
bedclothes over him as tenderly as a woman
would.
"I believe you will sleep now." he said.
The orderly moved away slowly. He went
out on to the balcony and looked up at the
quiet stars.
“Poor devil!” he muttered. “He must have
been in Hell. I wonder"
He stepped back abruptly into the ward.
MYAH.
Everybody Dies.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OF all the people who derive a large
measure of happiness from the con-
sideration of melancholy matters.
Old Man Andrews is the most shining
example amongst my acquaintance. He
developed his faculty for this particular
form of enjoyment one day as he absorbed
gin and gingerbeer.
“Everybody dies.” he said, following a
sorrowful trail of thought from the mention
of the death of an old lady at the age of 90.
"The old lady was a friend of my m -ther's,
and nursed me when I was so high. My
mother died 25 years ago. and now her
friend's dead too !"
"Anyway.” I said, "she had her full share
of living, and probably she did not object
to dying, as a change."
"Yes. of course she's the exception. It’s
when people die in the flush of their health,
or when it's young children who should have
years to live, that you feel it most That's
the position I took about my daughter
To divert his mind from what seemed a
sorrowful subject I suggested another drink,
and he assented. He took the same mourn-
ful kind of liquor, and, having got it, pur-
sued the theme.
“My girl gets a lover when she’s only 16;
not a bad fellow he isn’t, either; finely set
up, and a manly, straight chap all ways.
They wanted to marry, but I put my foot
down on it. The way I looked at it v as
that if they married there’d probably be chil-
dren. and it was only bringing people into
the world to die. I told them that she
could do what she liked when she was 21
and her own mistress, if she didn’t die before
then; but from what I had seen of people
dying I wasn't going to encourage it. Every-
body dies, and what’s the good of bringing
more people into the world to do it.'
“Well, of course, I’m set on my ideas, but
they're just as set on theirs. So I forbade
the young fellow to come near the place.
“ ‘Not that I have anything against you,’
I says to him; ‘but you’ve got no more
chance than if I hated you. My daughter
isn’t going to marry you. I’m not going to
encourage this dying.’
“ ‘lt’s living we were thinking of, he
answered. But I told him how I saw things.
“Well, he didn’t come near after that; but
by-and-bye I discovered that they were meet-
ing one another on the quiet. I was angry
at that; and you know how obstinate a
man gets when he’s angry! Well, I made a
sort of prisoner of Margie.
"I noticed a sort of change come over her
in a few weeks’ time; noticed it, but did not
heed it —you know what I mean. She seemed
restless, as if she was thinking over some-
thing that was worrying her. Then one
night she comes into the room, throws her
arms round me and kisses me. saying ‘Good-
night father, darling!’ I hardly noticed the
unusualness of it until afterwards, when the
wife comes in and says she can't find Margie
anywhere in the house, and she knew some-
thing was going to happen by the way she
kissed her good-night.”
He paused, and I twisted my glass,
scarcely knowing how to express sympathy
at the tragedy I understood.
“Poor girl!” I said at last.
"Just then." he pursued, “the young fellow
rang up on the 'phone, and he says that
Margie is all right, but they're determined
to marry, and he wants to see me. but sup-
poses that, as it's so late, he'd better leave
it till morning. Of course, I wouldn’t allow
anything of that sort, so I told him to come
out straight away.
"Imagine how I felt while I waited. I
was wondering whether to knock his brains
out with a poker or use my revolver. But
when he came in he was so straightforward
that, after the first few moments. I started
poking the fire to show that that was what
I had the poker in my hand for.
"Margie, it appears, just like a silly girl,
had run away to him; and he. being a decent,
straight fellow, but- one that wanted to
marry her, had helped her find a hen-
house Where she could stay without &'
any aspersions upon herself while he 5
tackle me. lie put their scheme to m ?- h°
had not abducted the girl, or harmed her in
any way, and he had used no influence I
her. She was all right; but she did Tot
mean to return, and he did not mean to Ml
me where she was until I consented to V
marriage. ineir
‘•lt was the wife that induced me to
way; so I said I would promise mv consent
but they d have to listen to me talk first ana
they might change their minds. And T ,n
talk to them ! I pointed out that they m s„ ,1
have children that might die; and that if he
died she'd be a widow; and if she died heVl
be a widower. But it didn’t change their
minds.”
“Why, that,” I said, “is quite a bright
little^ romance—a thing with life, not death
“Wait a bit,” he warned me, sombrelv
“He was an officer on one of the inter state
boats —a risky sort of job, and he had to be
out at all times and in all weathers. They’d
been married less than a year, and he was
away on one of his trips when it hanoenefi
“lie died?” I murmured.
“No: The baby was born. It was a fine
boy. though a bit delicate; and I used to
think of what would become of his parents
who simply adored him, if anything hap-
pened to him. Dear little lad,'he was- I
remember the day he asked me which was
the oldest, me or Methuselah; because, if I
was. he meant to be as old as me some day.”
lie gave a melancholy chuckle.
“They lived in a wild sort of place down
near the shore. George’s River way; and you
know how a child has to be watched where
there are rocks hanging over deep water.
Well, another baby comes along, and—l
suppose it’s because it looks more like its
father than the first—Margie simply is more
madly attached to this one than the other,
even: consequently ooor little Reggie is
allowed to wander about by himself.
“I well remember the day. I was sitting
on the corner of the verandah. I’d just been
warning him against leaning over the water,
where he'd gone with a bent pin on some
cotton, when I heard little Reggie’s voice
call out.”
"Fell in?" I suggested.
“Not he ! Damned if he hadn’t caught a
fish on that ridiculous fishing-line, a thing
about the size of a whitebait; and he comes
up. proud, to show me. saying I can have
some, but there must be some for daddy and
mummy. Then, going into the bedroom, he
sees the baby lying awake, and gives if the
fish. As babies will, the poor little mite tried
to eat it.”
He paused again.
"It was the baby that died?” I hinted.
“Died?" he echoed. "It's fine and healthy,
and growing : got eight teeth already.”
“I thought.” I excused myself, lamely, “you
implied that there was a death"
"Oh. no I” he retorted, gravely. “What I
did say. was that they 11 all die some day.
Everybody dies I”
